Intention Not Required for Wiping Over Khuffs and Head
States the Hanafi ruling that intention is not required for wiping over khuffs and head.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intention for wiping over khuffs and head and explain the usul principle that distinguishes between ibadah and wasilah.

Core Idea
Intention is not required for wiping over khuffs and head because they are not acts of worship.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the Hanafi position on a frequently misunderstood point: whether making an intention (niyyah) is necessary when wiping over khuffs and the head during wudu. The ruling derives from a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: intention is only required for acts that are either inherently worship (ibadah) or a substitute for worship (wasilah). Wiping over khuffs is neither; it is a concession and a means of purification. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and addresses a contrary view found in some later Hanafi works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Locate the act being discussed: wiping over khuffs and the head. State the Hanafi ruling: intention is not required for these acts.
The ruling is stated as 'on the correct view' (على الصحيح).
2
Extract the dalil (proof)
The proof is based on the Hanafi usul principle that intention is only required for acts that are ibadah (worship) or wasilah (substitute). Wiping over khuffs is neither, so intention is not required.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) derived from usul al-fiqh.
3
Contrast with tayammum
Tayammum is a wasilah (substitute for wudu) and therefore requires intention. This contrast reinforces that wiping over khuffs is not a wasilah.
The author uses tayammum as an example of a case where intention is required.
4
Address the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafis (e.g., in Jawami' al-Fiqh) required intention. The author dismisses this as weak (za'if). Identify the relied-upon view: intention not required.
The text states 'the weakness of what is in Jawami' al-Fiqh becomes apparent'.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school requires intention for wiping over khuffs. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on differing usul frameworks.
Thamarah al-khilaf: validity of wudu if intention is omitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ( ولا يفتقر إلى النية في مسح الخف والرأس) على الصحيح لأنهما ليسا بعبادة على أصلنا لأن النية لا تشترط إلا فيما هو عبادة أو وسيلة دل الدليل على اشتراطها فيها كالتيمم ولم يوجد فيما نحن فيه، وبهذا ظهر ضعف ما في جوامع الفقه أن النية شرط في مسح الخف والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
He said: (And one does not need intention in wiping over the khuff and the head) according to the correct view because they are not acts of worship in our school, for intention is only required in what is a worship or a means for which evidence indicates its requirement, like tayammum, and this is not found here. Thus, the weakness of what is in Jawami' al-Fiqh that intention is a condition for wiping over the khuff becomes apparent. And Allah the Exalted knows best.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the requirement of intention (نية) for wiping over khuffs and the head in wudu. The author clarifies the Hanafi position that intention is not a condition for these wipes. The reasoning (dalil aqli) is that these acts are not considered acts of worship (عبادة) in the Hanafi usul framework that necessitate intention; only acts that are worship or a substitute for worship (like tayammum) require intention. The passage also refutes a contrary opinion found in some later Hanafi collections (جوامع الفقه). This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that wudu itself does not require intention for its validity, though some parts like tayammum do.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention; the mental resolve to perform an act of worship for the sake of Allah.
عبادة (ibādah): Act of worship; a religious devotion that requires intention for validity.
وسيلة (wasīlah): A means or substitute; here, tayammum is a substitute for wudu and requires intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is intention required for wiping over khuffs?
A) Yes, because it is part of wudu.
B) No, because it is not a worship.
C) Only during travel.
D) Yes, but only for the khuff, not the head.

Key Takeaway
Intention is not required for wiping over khuffs and head because these acts are not worship; they are mere purification.

Definition of Menstruation: Duration and Classification
Defines menstruation and notes the scholarly dispute over its classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of menstruation, identify its minimum and maximum duration, and classify its legal status as najis.

Core Idea
Menstruation is blood from a healthy woman lasting 3–10 days; it is a physical impurity (najis), not a ritual event.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq opens the chapter on menstruation (hayḍ) with its core Hanafi definition. It specifies the minimum and maximum duration and notes a subtle scholarly dispute among Hanafi commentators: is menstruation a ritual impurity (ḥadath) or a physical impurity (najis)? The preferred view is that it is a physical impurity. This lesson anchors the essential definition and the legal classification that governs a woman's worship during her cycle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
Read the Arabic: 'وهو دم ينقضه رحم امرأة سليمة عن داء وصغر'. What is being defined? Menstruation is blood discharged from the womb, with the condition that the woman is free from disease and not a minor (i.e., has reached puberty).
Emphasize that the blood must come from the womb, not from a disease or injury.
2
Extract the temporal limits
Note the explicit minimum (ثلاثة أيام) and maximum (عشرة). These are the Hanafi thresholds. Any bleeding less than three days or more than ten is not considered menstruation.
Memorize: minimum 3, maximum 10.
3
Understand the legal classification dispute
The commentators differ: some call menstruation a 'ritual impurity' (حدث), others a 'physical impurity' (نجس). The author states the latter is more appropriate (الأنسب), based on an inference from 'after wiping' (بعد مسح).
The preferred view in the Hanafi school is that menstrual blood is najis, but it also requires ghusl (similar to hadath).
4
Recognize the practical consequence
The passage closes with a warning: ignorance of these rulings leads women to leave prayer unnecessarily. Knowing the exact duration prevents incorrect cessation of worship.
The mufta bihi: menstruation is najis; its presence invalidates prayer and fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحيض
وهو دم ينقضه رحم امرأة سليمة عن داء وصغر وأقله ثلاثة أيام وأكثره عشرة
اختلف الشارحون في التعبير عن الحيض والنفاس بأنهما من الأحداث أو الأنجاس؛ فمنهم من ذهب إلى الثاني ومنهم من ذهب إلى الأول وهو الأنسب لأن المصنف يقول بعد مسح .
قوله: (وضرر الجهل الخ) وذلك لأن المرأة إذا لم تعلم مسائل الحيض ربما تترك الصلاة
Chapter of Menstruation. It is blood that leaves the womb of a woman free from disease and young age. Its minimum is three days and its maximum is ten days. The commentators differed in describing menstruation and postpartum bleeding as events or impurities; some went for the second, some for the first, and the latter is more appropriate because the author says after wiping... His statement: (And the harm of ignorance etc.) because if a woman does not learn the rulings of menstruation, she might leave prayer.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on menstruation (حيض) with the standard Hanafi definition: it is blood discharged from the womb of a healthy adult woman, lasting a minimum of three full days and a maximum of ten. The commentator then notes a theoretical dispute: is menstruation legally categorized as a 'ritual impurity' (حدث) or a 'physical impurity' (نجس)? The preferred view is that it is a physical impurity, based on an inference from the author's earlier wording 'after wiping' (perhaps referring to the need for ghusl). The final sentence warns of the practical harm of ignorance: a woman unaware of these rulings may erroneously abandon prayer. This lesson is foundational for understanding the rules of hayd in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
حيض (hayḍ): Menstrual blood discharged from a healthy, adult woman's womb, with a minimum of three days and maximum of ten.
حدث (ḥadath): Ritual impurity that is removed by wudu or ghusl (e.g., after passing wind, intercourse).
نجس (najis): Physical impurity, like blood or urine, that requires washing off.
نفاس (nifās): Postpartum bleeding following childbirth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum duration of menstruation in the Hanafi school?
A) One day
B) Two days
C) Three days
D) Ten days

Key Takeaway
Menstruation in Hanafi fiqh is defined as blood from a healthy adult woman lasting 3–10 days, classified as physical impurity (najis).

Thirteen Further Distinctions Between Splint and Khuff Wiping
Lists additional distinguishing rulings (13–25) between wiping on splint and khuff.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state at least five differences between wiping on a splint and wiping on khuff, and explain the legal basis for each.

Core Idea
Wiping on a splint is more restrictive than khuff because it is a concession for necessity, not a substitute for washing.

In Hanafi fiqh, both wiping on a splint (jabira) and wiping on khuff are concessions in wudu, but they are governed by different rules. This lesson presents a list of thirteen additional distinctions beyond the initial twelve, highlighting how the splint’s medical context affects its legal treatment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognise that the list contrasts splint wiping with khuff wiping. Each point isolates a specific ruling where the two differ.
The first twelve differences were covered earlier; these are points 13–25.
2
Group the differences
Classify them into three categories: conditions of permissibility (e.g., inability to wash directly, no harm), invalidators (e.g., healing, falling off), and procedural rules (e.g., combining with washing the other foot, immersion effect).
This helps see the pattern: splint rules are stricter.
3
Analyse a key invalidity rule
Focus on points 19–20: the splint wipe is invalidated by healing even without falling, and by falling off due to healing. Contrast with khuff, which is invalidated by falling off unconditionally.
The splint’s invalidity is tied to the reason for the concession (the wound), whereas the khuff is a pure substitute.
4
Examine the immersion dispute (point 24)
Note the intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf holds that immersing the splint does not spoil the water, but Muhammad says it does. The relied-upon position is that it spoils the water.
The underlying reasoning: wiping is performed by wetness, so the water is not used; but the splint is like washing the wound underneath.
5
Synthesise the overall principle
Conclude that splint wiping is a more limited concession, conditional on need and directly linked to the wound. It is not a substitute like khuff, but a temporary dispensation.
This explains why many conditions and invalidators are unique to the splint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف الخف الثالث عشر إذا كان الباقي من العضو المعصوب أقل من ثلاثة أصابع كاليد المقطوعة والرجل جاز المسح عليها بخلاف المسح على الخفين كما قدمناه. الرابع عشر أن الجبيرة ليس ثابتاً بالكتاب اتفاقاً بخلاف مسح الخف فإن خلافاً كما قدمناه. الخامس عشر أن مسح الجبيرة يجوز تركه في بعض الروايات بخلاف المسح على الخفين فإنه لا يجوز تركه مع إرادة عدم الغسل. ثم قال في النهر: وزدت السادس عشر أن المسح على الجبيرة ليس خلفاً ولا بدلاً عن الغسل بخلاف الخف. وقد يزاد غيرها كما في التنوير وغيره فنقول: السابع عشر أن المسح على الجبيرة يترك إن ضر وإلا لا بخلاف الخف. الثامن عشر أنه مشروط بالعجز عن مسح نفس الموضع فإن قدر على مسحه فلا مسح عليها. التاسع عشر أنه يبطل ببرء موضعها وإن لم تسقط. العشرون أنه يبطل سقوطها عن برء بخلاف الخف فإنه يبطل بسقوطه بلا شرط. الحادي والعشرون أن جبيرة رجل يجمع مع غسل الأخرى بخلاف الخف. الثاني والعشرون أنه مشروط بالعجز عن مسح الموضع بخلاف الخف. الثالث والعشرون أنه يجوز ولو كانت على غير الرجلين بخلاف الخف. الرابع والعشرون إذا غمس الجبيرة في إناء يريد به المسح عليها لم يجز وأفسد الماء بخلاف الخف، وكذا الرأس فلا يفسد، ويجوز عند الثاني خلافاً لمحمد كما في المنظومة وشرحها الحقائق. والفرق لأبي يوسف أن المسح يتأدى بالبلة فلا يكون الماء مستعملاً ويجوز المسح، أما مسح الجبيرة فكالغسل لما تحته. قال في الحقائق: ذكره في الخزانة وأحاله إلى المنتقى. الخامس والعشرون لو كانت على رجله وسقطت عن برء ويخاف إن غسلها أن تسقط أن يتيمم بخلاف الخف على ما فتدبر والله تعالى أعلم.
Contrary to the khuff, the thirteenth: if the remaining part of the bandaged limb is less than three fingers, like a cut hand or foot, it is permissible to wipe over it, unlike wiping over khuff as we have mentioned. The fourteenth: that the splint is not established by the Book by consensus, unlike wiping over khuff, which has disagreement as we have mentioned. The fifteenth: that wiping over a splint may be omitted in some narrations, unlike wiping over khuff, which cannot be omitted when intending not to wash. Then he said in al-Nahr: I added the sixteenth: that wiping over the splint is neither a substitute nor a replacement for washing, unlike the khuff. And others may be added as in al-Tanwir and others, so we say: the seventeenth: that wiping over the splint is left if there is harm, otherwise not, unlike the khuff. The eighteenth: that it is conditioned on inability to wash the actual spot; if one can wash it, there is no wipe over it. The nineteenth: that it becomes invalid when the place heals, even if it does not fall off. The twentieth: that it becomes invalid by its falling off due to healing, unlike the khuff, as it becomes invalid by falling off unconditionally. The twenty-first: that the splint on one foot is combined with washing the other foot, unlike the khuff. The twenty-second: that it is conditioned on inability to wash the spot, unlike the khuff. The twenty-third: that it is permissible even if on other than the feet, unlike the khuff. The twenty-fourth: if one immerses the splint in a vessel intending to wipe over it, it is not permissible and spoils the water, unlike the khuff; similarly the head does not spoil it, and it is permissible according to the second (Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad as in al-Manzuma and its commentary al-Haqa'iq. The difference for Abu Yusuf is that wiping is performed by the wetness, so the water is not used and wiping is permissible; but wiping over the splint is like washing what is under it. He said in al-Haqa'iq: It was mentioned in al-Khizana and referred to al-Muntaqa. The twenty-fifth: if there is a splint on his foot and it falls off due to healing, and he fears that if he washes it, it will fall off, then he should perform tayammum, unlike the khuff as above. So ponder, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a list of additional differences (points 13–25) between wiping over a splint (jabira) and wiping over khuff (leather socks). The author, Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, is expanding on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The first twelve differences were mentioned earlier (not in this window). The commentator adds these to clarify the unique rulings of the splint. Key distinctions include: (13) if the bandaged part is less than three fingers, wiping is allowed; (14) splint is established by ijtihad, not nass; (15) wiping on splint can be omitted in some narrations; (16) splint is not a substitute for washing; (17) it is left if harmful; (18) it is contingent on inability to wash directly; (19) invalidated by healing even without falling; (20) invalidated by falling due to healing; (21) one splint can be combined with washing the other foot; (22) conditioned on inability; (23) allowed on non-foot body parts; (24) immersing splint spoils water unlike khuff, with a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; (25) if splint falls after healing and washing risks it falling, tayammum is performed. These points emphasize that the splint is treated more strictly than khuff due to its medical necessity and lack of explicit textual basis.

Key Terms
الجبيرة (al-jabīrah): A splint or bandage placed on a wound or fracture; in fiqh, it is a covering over a body part that may be wiped instead of washing.
الخف (al-khuff): Leather socks worn over the feet; in fiqh, wiping over them is a substitute for washing the feet in wudu under specific conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the list, what happens if the splint falls off due to healing?
A) The wipe remains valid.
B) The wipe becomes invalid.
C) It depends on whether the wound has healed completely.
D) One must perform tayammum.

Key Takeaway
Wiping on a splint is a distinct legal category with its own conditions and invalidators, different from khuff wiping.

Introduction to Menstruation: Importance and Legal Definition
Opens the chapter on hayd with discussion on its nature, importance, and definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of hayd, explain why knowledge of its rulings is wajib, and identify the key dispute about its nature (hadath vs najas).

Core Idea
Ignorance of menstruation rulings leads to grave sin, making their study a wajib obligation.

This lesson opens the chapter on menstruation (Hayd) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author first addresses a subtle scholarly debate: is menstruation an "event" (hadath) that breaks wudu, or an "impurity" (najas) that renders the body impure? He stresses that learning these rulings is among the greatest obligations due to the severe harm of ignorance. Finally, he provides the linguistic and legal definitions of hayd, setting the stage for detailed discussions to follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The passage begins by noting a disagreement among Hanafi commentators: are hayd and nifas considered hadath (breaking wudu) or najas (impurity)? Note that the author, Ibn Nujaym, leans toward najas based on the matn's definition.
While the dispute exists, the passage states there is no practical difference in rulings.
2
Understand the importance
The author argues that knowledge of hayd is among the greatest obligations because ignorance causes severe harm, including forbidden intercourse and even disbelief if istihlal occurs. The hadith "Whoever has intercourse with his menstruating wife has disbelieved..." is cited.
This highlights why the subject is treated as a serious legal necessity.
3
Extract the linguistic definition
Linguistically, hayd means "flowing" (e.g., wadi hayd). It is called so because blood flows at regular times. The term ha'id (menstruating woman) is used without the feminine taa because it is inherently feminine.
Other names include tamth, dahk, etc., but hayd is the most common.
4
Extract the legal definition
The legal definition according to the matn is: "Blood emitted by a woman's womb, free from illness and young age" (i.e., before puberty). This excludes blood due to disease or from a prepubescent girl.
The indefinite "dam" (blood) in the definition suggests it is considered najas.
5
Appreciate the scope
The passage outlines ten topics to be covered: definition (linguistic and legal), cause, pillar, condition, duration, colors, times, time of establishment, and related rulings. This lesson focuses on the introductory aspects.
This shows the systematic approach of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحيض
اختلف الشارحون في التعبير عن الحيض والنفاس بأنهما من الأحداث أو الأنجاس؛ فمنهم من ذهب إلى الثاني ومنهم من ذهب إلى الأول وهو الأنسب لأن المصنف يقول بعد ذلك: (وهو دم ينفضه رحم امرأة سليمة عن داء وصغر) فدخل في قوله «دم» غير المعرف، ولقب الباب بالحيض دون النفاس لكثرته أو لكونه حالة معهودة في بنات آدم دون النفاس. كذا في العناية. لكن الظاهر من كلام المصنف أنه من الأنجاس بدليل التعريف وأفرده لاختصاصه بأحكام على حدة وقدمه لكثرة مناسبته بالأحداث حتى كانت الأحكام المختصة بالأحداث ثابتة له. ولا يضر اختصاص نوع من النجس بأحكام وبهذا اندفع ما في النهاية كما لا يخفى، والظاهر أنه لا ثمرة لهذا الاختلاف.
واعلم أن باب الحيض من غوامض الأبواب خصوصاً من المتحيرة وتفاريعها، ولهذا اعتنى به المحققون وأفرده محمد في كتاب مستقل. ومعرفة مسائل الحيض من أعظم المهمات لما يترتب عليها ما لا يحصى من الأحكام كالطهارة والصلاة وقراءة القرآن والصوم والاعتكاف والحج والبلوغ والوطء والطلاق والعدة والاستبراء وغير ذلك من الأحكام. وكان من أعظم الواجبات لأن عظم منزلة العلم بالشيء بحسب منزلة ضرر الجهل به، وضرر الجهل بمسائل الحيض أشد من ضرر الجهل بغيرها فيجب الاعتناء بمعرفتها وإن كان الكلام فيها طويلاً فإن المحصل يتشوف إلى ذلك، ولا التفات إلى كراهة أهل البطالة.
ثم الكلام فيه في عشرة مواضع: في تفسيره لغة وشرعاً وسببه وركنه، وشرطه، وقدره، وألوانه، وأوانه، ووقت ثبوته والأحكام المتعلقة.
أما تفسيره لغة فقال أهل اللغة: أصله السيلان يقال حاض الوادي أي سال فسمي حيضاً لسيلانه في أوقاته. وقال الأزهري: الحيض دم يرخيه رحم المرأة بعد بلوغها في أوقات معتادة، ويقال حاضت المرأة تحيض حيضاً ومحيضاً ومحاضاً فهي حائض بحذف التاء لأنه صفة المؤنث خاصة فلا تحتاج إلى علامة التأنيث بخلاف قائمة ومسلمة، هذه اللغة الفصيحة المشهورة. وحكى الجوهري عن الفراء أنه يقال أيضاً حائضة وله عشرة أسماء: حيض وطمث بالمثلة وضحك وإكبار وإعصار ودراس وعراك وفراك بالفاء وطمس بالسين المهملة ونفاس. وزاد بعضهم طمت بالمثناة وطمء بالهمزة.
وأما تفسيره شرعاً بناء على أنه من الأنجاس فما ذكره المصنف بقوله: (وهو دم ينفضه رحم امرأة سليمة عن داء وصغر) فدخل في قوله «دم» غير المعرف والصوم وقت الوجوب وتأتي بهما في وقت وجوب الترك وكلاهما أمر حرام وضرر عظيم، ولأن ضرر هذا الجهل يختص ويتعدى بخلاف الجهل فيما سواه. أما المختص فهو ما ذكرناه وأما المتعدي فهو غشيان الرجل في حالة الحيض وذلك حرام بالنص والاعتقاد بحله كفر. قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من أتى امرأته الحائض فقد كفر بما أنزل على محمد» أي مستحلاً وحكى أن هارون الرشيد تزوّج امرأة من بنات الأشراف وبها من الجهاز العظيم ما لا يعد ولا يحصى فلما زفت إليه ودخل هو معها
Chapter of Menstruation.
The commentators differed in expressing menstruation and postpartum bleeding as being among the events (hadath) or impurities (najas). Some went for the second and some for the first, and the latter is more appropriate because the author says after this: (It is blood emitted by a woman's womb free from illness and young age), so enters under his word 'blood' which is indefinite. And he titled the chapter with menstruation rather than postpartum bleeding due to its frequency or because it is a familiar condition among daughters of Adam unlike postpartum bleeding. This is in al-'Inayah. But the apparent from the author's speech is that it is from impurities, by evidence of the definition, and he singled it out because it has unique rulings, and he presented it due to its strong connection with events so that the rulings specific to events are also established for it. And it does not harm that a type of impurity has rulings; accordingly, what is in al-Nihayah is repelled, as is not hidden. And it appears that there is no benefit in this difference.
Know that the chapter of menstruation is among the most subtle of chapters, especially regarding the confused woman (mutahayyirah) and its branches. Hence, the verifiers paid attention to it and Muhammad singled it out in an independent book. Knowledge of the matters of menstruation is among the greatest important duties because of the countless rulings that depend on it, such as purification, prayer, reciting Quran, fasting, seclusion, pilgrimage, puberty, intercourse, divorce, waiting period, and other rulings. And it is among the greatest obligations because the greatness of the status of knowledge of something is according to the greatness of the harm of ignorance of it, and the harm of ignorance about menstruation matters is more severe than ignorance about other matters. So it is obligatory to care about knowing it, even if speech about it is lengthy, because the earnest student desires that. And no attention is paid to the disapproval of the people of idleness.
Then speech about it is in ten topics: its interpretation linguistically and legally, its cause, its pillar, its condition, its duration, its colors, its times, its time of establishment, and the rulings related to it.
As for its linguistic interpretation, the linguists said: its root is flowing; it is said 'the valley flowed' so it is called menstruation due to its flowing at its times. Al-Azhari said: Menstruation is blood that the woman's womb releases after her puberty at customary times. And it is said 'the woman menstruated' and she is a menstruating woman (ha'id) with the deletion of the t because it is specifically a feminine descriptor so it does not need the feminine marker, unlike qa'imah and muslimah; this is the eloquent and famous language. Al-Jawhari related from al-Farra' that it is also said 'haiḍah', and it has ten names: haiḍ, tamth (with th), ḍahk, inkabār, iʿṣār, dirās, ʿirāk, firāk (with f), ṭams (with important sīn), and nifās. Some added ṭamt (with t) and ṭam' (with hamzah).
As for its legal interpretation, based on it being from impurities, it is what the author stated: (It is blood emitted by a woman's womb free from illness and young age). So enters under his word 'blood' indefinite and fasting... [text unclear] and both matters are forbidden and a great harm. And because the harm of this ignorance is specific and extends, unlike ignorance in other matters. As for the specific, it is what we mentioned; as for the extending, it is a man having intercourse with his wife during menstruation, which is forbidden by text and believing it to be lawful is disbelief. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever has intercourse with his menstruating wife has disbelieved in what was revealed to Muhammad' meaning considering it lawful. And it is narrated that Harun al-Rashid married a woman from noble families with a great dowry beyond count, and when she was brought to him and he entered with her...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Bab al-Hayd (Chapter of Menstruation) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, first discusses a scholarly dispute: whether menstruation and postpartum bleeding are considered 'events' (hadath) that break ablution or 'impurities' (najas) that affect the body. He notes that the author (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) treats it as najas based on the definition 'blood from a healthy womb'. The commentator leans toward considering it najas due to the evidence of the definition, but says the difference has no practical effect. He then emphasizes the importance of learning menstruation rulings because of their widespread application in acts of worship, family law, and the severe harm of ignorance, including unlawful intercourse and even kufr if one deems it permissible. He cites a hadith: 'Whoever has intercourse with his menstruating wife has disbelieved...'. The passage ends with a story of Harun al-Rashid, which is cut off. After this, the commentator outlines ten topics to be discussed, and begins with the linguistic definition of hayd, listing its etymology and various names. The legal definition is then given as per the matn: blood from a womb free of illness and youth (i.e., before puberty). This definition excludes blood from disease or from a young girl before menarche.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-ḥayḍ): Linguistically: flowing. Legally: blood emitted from a woman's womb, free from illness and pre-puberty, occurring at regular times.
النفاس (al-nifās): Postpartum bleeding; mentioned alongside hayd but not the focus of this chapter.
حائض (ḥāʾiḍ): A menstruating woman; the feminine form is used without the feminine tā because it is inherently feminine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition given in the passage, which of the following blood types is NOT considered menstruation?
A) Blood that flows for 5 days from a 15-year-old healthy girl
B) Blood that flows for 2 days from a 45-year-old woman
C) Blood from a 10-year-old girl (before puberty)
D) Blood that flows for 8 days from a 25-year-old woman

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of menstruation rulings is wajib; the legal definition requires blood from a healthy, post-pubescent woman.

Classification of Menstruation in the Hanafi School: Najas or Hadath?
Introduces the chapter of hayd, discusses its classification as najas, and explains its importance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school classifies hayd as najas (impurity) rather than hadath (ritual impurity), and state the practical significance of this classification.

Core Idea
Hayd is substance-impurity (najas), not state-impurity (hadath), but its rulings mirror those of hadath.

This opening passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq sets the stage for studying menstruation by addressing two key issues: its legal classification (najas vs hadath) and its monumental importance. The author clarifies the apparent view of Kanz al-Daqaiq that hayd is a type of impurity (najas), and explains why the chapter is placed under impurities. He then emphasizes that knowing hayd rulings is a great obligation because of the severe harm of ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification dispute
Read the passage to determine the key difference: does the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq consider hayd to be najas or hadath? Note that al-Inayah suggests the chapter is titled 'hayd' due to frequency, but the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq argues that the definition and placement indicate it is from najas.
The dispute is between the apparent meaning of the matn and the commentary in al-Nihayah, but the author says there is no practical benefit in this difference.
2
Follow the author's argument
The author supports his view with two points: (1) the definition of hayd (which is based on najas), and (2) the fact that the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq placed it under the chapter of impurities. He also refutes the objection from al-Nihayah by stating that a type of najas can have specific rulings.
The author's reasoning is based on textual evidence and logical consistency.
3
Assess the practical consequence
The author explicitly states that there is no practical difference (la thamara) in this classification dispute. Both najas and hadath lead to the same rulings for hayd, such as prohibition of prayer.
This shows the author's focus on practical fiqh over pure semantics.
4
Understand the chapter's importance
The passage lists numerous rulings that depend on knowing hayd: taharah, salah, qira'ah, fasting, i'tikaf, hajj, puberty, intercourse, divorce, iddah, and istibra. The harm of ignorance is emphasized as severe.
The author cites Imam Muhammad's independent book on hayd and calls it a 'great obligation' to study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة - باب هذا باب الأنجاس. ولما فرغ من الأحداث التي يكثر وقوعها ذكر ما هو أقل وقوعاً منه، ولقب الباب بالحيض دون النفاس لكثرته أو لكونه حالة معهودة في بنات آدم دون النفاس. كذا في العناية . لكن الظاهر من كلام المصنف أنه من الأنجاس بدليل التعريف وأفرده لاختصاصه بأحكام على حدة وقدمه لكثرة مناسبته بالأحداث حتى كانت الأحكام المختصة بالأحداث ثابتة له. ولا يضر اختصاص نوع من النجس بأحكام وبهذا اندفع ما في النهاية كما لا يخفى، والظاهر أنه لا ثمرة لهذا الاختلاف واعلم أن باب الحيض من غوامض الأبواب خصوصاً من المتحيرة وتفاريعها، ولهذا اعتنى به المحققون وأفرده محمد في كتاب مستقل. ومعرفة مسائل الحيض من أعظم المهمات لما يترتب عليها ما لا يحصى من الأحكام كالطهارة والصلاة وقراءة القرآن والصوم والاعتكاف والحج والبلوغ والوطء والطلاق والعدة والاستبراء وغير ذلك من الأحكام. وكان من أعظم الواجبات لأن عظم منزلة العلم بالشيء بحسب منزلة ضرر الجهل به، وضرر الجهل بمسائل الحيض أشد من ضرر الجهل بغيرها فيجب الاعتناء بمعرفتها وإن كان الكلام فيها طويلاً فإن المحصل يتشوف إلى ذلك، ولا التفات إلى كراهة أهل البطالة .
Book of Purification - Chapter: This is the chapter of impurities. When he finished from the hadath (ritual impurities) that occur frequently, he mentioned what occurs less than that. And he titled the chapter with menstruation (hayd) and not with postnatal bleeding (nifas) because of its frequency or because it is a condition familiar among the daughters of Adam unlike nifas. Thus in al-Inayah. But the apparent meaning from the author's speech is that it is from the impurities (anjas) as evidence by the definition, and he singled it out because it has rulings specific to it, and he presented it due to its strong connection to hadath, so much so that the rulings specific to hadath are also established for it. And it does not harm that a type of impurity has specific rulings, and with this the objection in al-Nihayah is repelled, as is not hidden. And apparently there is no benefit in this difference. And know that the chapter of menstruation is among the most subtle chapters, especially regarding the mustahadah (woman with chronic bleeding) and its branches, and so the verifiers paid attention to it and Muhammad wrote an independent book on it. Knowing the issues of menstruation is among the greatest important matters because of the countless rulings that depend on it, like purity, prayer, reading Quran, fasting, i'tikaf, hajj, puberty, intercourse, divorce, waiting period ('iddah), istibra' (declaring the womb free from pregnancy), and other rulings. Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the chapter of menstruation (hayd) from al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq. The author begins by explaining why the chapter is placed under 'impurities' (anjas) and not 'hadath' (ritual impurity). He quotes al-Inayah that the chapter is titled 'hayd' rather than 'nifas' due to frequency. But he clarifies that the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq apparently considered hayd as najas (impurity), not hadath, and that the definition supports this. He also discusses a difference among commentators: some (like al-Nihayah) consider it from hadath, but the author argues this is a semantic difference with no practical consequence. Then he emphasizes the importance of studying hayd, calling it one of the most subtle chapters because many rulings depend on it, such as taharah, salah, fasting, etc. He quotes Imam Muhammad's independent work on the topic. He declares learning it a great obligation because ignorance causes severe harm, including acts that are haram. The passage sets the stage for the detailed definition and rulings that follow.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood; the chapter topic. The text discusses its classification as najas or hadath.
نجاسة (najasah): Legal impurity attached to the substance itself. The author's apparent view is that hayd is a type of najasah.
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity that prevents acts of worship. The text contrasts hadath with najas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) There is no practical consequence.
D) It affects the validity of her prayers if she is unsure.

Key Takeaway
Hayd is classified as najas in the Hanafi school, but this classification has no practical effect on rulings; the real emphasis is on the immense importance of learning hayd rulings due to the severe harm of ignorance.

The Linguistic and Legal Definition of Hayd (Menstruation)
Defines hayd linguistically and legally with detailed exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of hayd and identify which types of blood are excluded by each qualifier (disease, childhood, and source).

Core Idea
Hayd is blood from a healthy womb after puberty, excluding pathological, postnatal, and pre‑pubertal bleeding.

Menstruation is a fundamental concept in fiqh that affects prayer, fasting, and marital relations. This lesson unpacks the precise Hanafi legal definition of hayd, distinguishing it from istihadah (chronic bleeding), nifas (postnatal bleeding), and the blood of young girls. Understanding the definition’s qualifiers is essential for correct application of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic origin and synonyms
Identify that hayd literally means 'flowing' and list its ten synonyms. This step grounds the legal definition in customary usage.
2
Isolate the legal definition and its three elements
Extract the author's definition: 'blood that a woman's womb discharges, free from disease and childhood.' Analyze each element: 'blood' (real and legal), 'discharges' (excludes blood from other sources), 'free from disease' (excludes istihadah and nifas), 'childhood' (excludes blood before nine years).
3
Map exclusions to specific qualifiers
For each excluded type (nosebleed, wound, istihadah, nifas, pre‑pubescent blood), determine which word in the definition excludes it. Understand the debate over whether the word 'childhood' is redundant.
The commentary defends the definition against criticism from Fath al-Qadir.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of hayd
Distinguish between the majority view that hayd is najas (filth) and the minority view that it is hadath (ritual impurity). See how the definition changes depending on this classification.
The author of al-Nihayah held the hadath view, but the commentator notes a contradiction.
5
Apply the definition to a practical scenario
Consider a girl who begins menstruating at age 12. Determine that her blood is hayd because all conditions are met. Contrast with a woman with a uterine disease whose bleeding is istihadah.
This step reinforces the importance of the 'healthy womb' qualifier.

والجواب منع تسميته فساد كما قاله بعضهم قوله : (لكن قال بعضهم الخ) أي فلا يكون خارجاً بقوله سليمة عن داء ولا يخفى أنه يتوقف على ثبوت أن الفساد ليس عن داء، ولكن ظاهر تسميته داء فيخرج بقوله سليمة على أن ما استدل به من أنه لا يقال لدم الصغيرة استحاضة بذلك أنه عن غير ظاهر لأنه يصدق عليه أنه على صفة لا تكون حيضاً . قوله : وبهذا التقرير يندفع الخ) لا يخفى
Then the discussion about it is in ten topics: its interpretation linguistically and legally, its cause, its pillar, its condition, its duration, its colors, its times, the time of its establishment, and the rulings related to it. As for its linguistic interpretation, the linguists said: its origin is flowing; it is said 'the valley flowed' meaning it flowed, so it is called hayd because of its flow at its times. Al-Azhari said: Hayd is blood that the woman's womb releases after her puberty at customary times, and it is said 'the woman menstruated' as hayd, mahid, and madah, so she is 'ha'id' with the deletion of the 'ta' because it is specifically a feminine description so it does not need a feminine marker unlike qa'imah and muslimah. This is the eloquent and well-known language. Al-Jawhari narrated from al-Farra' that it is also said 'ha'idah'. It has ten names: hayd, tamth (with tashdid), dahk, ikbar, i'sar, diras, 'irak, firak (with fa'), tamus (with important sin), and nifas. Some added 'tammat' (with tashdid) and 'tam' (with hamza). As for its legal interpretation, based on it being from the impurities, it is what the author mentioned by his statement: (It is blood that a woman's womb discharges, free from disease and childhood). So by his word 'blood' indefinite, real and legal blood enters, and by his word 'discharges' the blood of nosebleeds and wounds exits, and what comes from other than a human, and blood exiting from the anus, for these are not hayd, but it is recommended for her to perform ghusl when the blood stops, and if the husband refrains from coming to her it is more beloved. Thus in al-Khulasah. Istihadah (chronic bleeding) is not excluded because what is meant by 'womb' here is the vagina, and it is only excluded by his word 'free from disease', meaning disease of her womb. He stipulated 'needing' because the illness of a woman whose womb is healthy does not prevent what she sees in her habit, for example, from being hayd, as is not hidden. Nifas (postnatal bleeding) is also excluded because the womb has a disease due to childbirth. This is more appropriate than what they said that nifas is excluded because the woman in nifas is considered ill such that her gifts are considered... because the apparent meaning is that the woman's disease prevents her from being ha'id, but you know the contrary. Also excluded by it is what a young girl sees, for it is blood of istihadah, but some said that what a woman sees before completing nine years is corruption (fasad) and is not called istihadah because istihadah is only of a quality that is not hayd. For this reason al-Azhari said: Istihadah is the flow of blood outside its customary times. So because of this he mentioned what excludes what a young girl sees by his word 'childhood'. With this explanation, what was mentioned in Fath al-Qadir about this definition being not free from repetition and surplus is repelled, because the word 'childhood' is surplus and istihadah is repeated, so it is blood... its exclusion by mentioning womb and free from disease, and its definition without surplus and repetition: blood from the womb not due to childbirth. End. The author of al-Bada'i preceded him in this definition. And in al-Zahiriyyah: If khantha (hermaphrodite) emits semen and blood, the consideration is for the semen, not the blood. Then this definition is based on the meaning of hayd being filth (khabath). As for if its meaning is the hadath (ritual impurity) that arises from blood, prohibited for recitation and touching, like the name janabah for the specific hadath not the specific water, then its definition is a legal prevention due to that blood from what requires purity, and from fasting, mosque, and prayer. The author of al-Nihayah affirmed that it is from hadath, not from najas, and defined it as in the book, so there was contradiction from him. As for its cause, it is said: [hadith about Harun al-Rashid] ... His statement: (And istihadah is not excluded) etc. Al-Nahr said: We do not accept that what is meant by 'womb' is the vagina, because his word 'discharges' refutes that, since it is established that discharge is only from the womb. So what is in the sharh about istihadah being excluded is more appropriate. But it is objected that his word 'childhood' is superfluous because what a young girl sees is istihadah. The answer is to deny that it is called fasad as some said. His statement: (But some said etc.) i.e. it is not excluded by his word 'free from disease', and it is not hidden that this depends on establishing that fasad is not from disease, but the apparent naming of it as disease would exclude it by his word 'free from disease'. And as for his evidence that the girl's blood is not called istihadah, that is not apparent because it is true that it is of a quality that is not hayd. His statement: 'And with this explanation, it is repelled etc.' is not hidden.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of hayd (menstruation) from al-Bahr al-Raiq. It begins by listing ten topics to be covered. The linguistic definition: hayd originally means flowing. Al-Azhari defines it as blood released by the womb after puberty at customary times. Ten synonyms are listed. The legal definition is from the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq: 'It is blood that a woman's womb discharges, free from disease and childhood.' The commentary then explains each element: 'blood' includes real and legal blood; 'discharges' excludes blood from nosebleeds, wounds, non-human sources, and anal blood; 'free from disease' excludes istihadah (chronic bleeding) and nifas (postnatal bleeding); 'childhood' excludes blood from girls before completing nine years (considered fasad or istihadah by some). The commentator discusses a dispute about whether 'childhood' is redundant and whether the definition is circular. He defends the definition against criticism from Fath al-Qadir, citing earlier scholars like al-Bada'i. The discussion also touches on the classification of hayd: if considered khabath (filth) then the definition stands; if considered hadath (ritual impurity), a different definition would apply. The author notes a contradiction in al-Nihayah. A hadith about Harun al-Rashid is narrated to emphasize the seriousness of the issue. Finally, a hashia from al-Nahr refines the point about istihadah exclusion, arguing that 'womb' means the organ itself, not the vagina, and that 'childhood' may not be superfluous if one considers the girl's blood as fasad rather than istihadah. This passage shows the rigorous analytical style of the Hanafi school in defining legal terms.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Blood discharged from a woman's womb, free from disease and childhood, as per the author's definition.
استحاضة (istihadah): Chronic bleeding outside the normal menstrual period; not considered hayd. It is blood from a vein, not the womb. Some scholars define it as bleeding that does not meet the criteria for hayd.
نفاس (nifas): Postnatal bleeding caused by childbirth; excluded from hayd definition because the womb has a disease (due to childbirth).
فساد (fasad): Blood seen before completing nine years; some scholars call it 'fasad' (corruption) rather than istihadah, to distinguish it from true chronic bleeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of blood is excluded from the definition of hayd by the phrase 'free from disease' (سليمة عن داء)?
A) Blood from a nosebleed
B) Blood from istihadah (chronic bleeding)
C) Blood from a young girl before nine
D) Blood from the anus

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi legal definition of hayd precisely identifies the blood that triggers ritual impurity and prohibitions, excluding pathological, postnatal, and pre‑pubescent bleeding.

Definition of Menstrual Blood and Its Exclusions
Commentary on the definition of hayd from Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hayd and identify the exclusions based on the conditions of the definition.

Core Idea
Menstrual blood: blood from a healthy woman's womb, not due to childbirth.

The passage provides the precise legal definition of menstrual blood (hayd) as established in the Hanafi school. Understanding this definition is essential for distinguishing hayd from other types of uterine bleeding, such as istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding due to disease) and nifas (postpartum bleeding). The commentary also addresses internal criticism, reinforcing the definition's clarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components of the definition
The definition has three key parts: (1) blood discharged from the womb, (2) by a woman, (3) free from disease and not due to childbirth. Each part excludes specific types of bleeding.
The term 'womb' is interpreted as the vagina, and 'free from disease' excludes istihadah and nifas.
2
Understand each exclusion
Examine how each condition excludes certain blood: 'discharged by a woman' excludes non-human blood; 'from the womb' excludes blood from other body parts; 'free from disease' excludes istihadah; 'not due to childbirth' excludes nifas. Also, young girls (under nine) are excluded by the condition of womanhood or by a separate exclusion.
The commentator clarifies that illness in other parts of the body does not affect the ruling if the womb is healthy.
3
Analyze the defence against criticism
The commentator refutes the objection from Fath al-Qadir that the definition is redundant. He argues that the word 'youngness' is necessary to exclude the blood of a young girl, which is neither hayd nor istihadah according to some scholars.
This step shows the analytical rigor expected in Hanafi scholarship.
4
Note the legal effect and epistemological status
The passage indicates that hayd is a ritual impurity (najis) or an event (hadath) with legal consequences (e.g., prohibition of prayer, fasting). The definition is from the zahir al-riwayah and is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The commentator acknowledges a disagreement on whether hayd is najis or hadath, but this does not affect the definition.

Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of menstrual blood (hayd) from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author, Imam Ibn Nujaym, explains the components and exclusions of the definition. The definition is: 'blood discharged by a woman from the womb, free from disease, not due to childbirth.' The commentary expands on each part: 'discharged by a woman' excludes blood from wounds, nosebleeds, animal blood, and anal blood. Although these are not menstruation, it is recommended for the woman to perform ghusl when such blood stops, and it is preferable for the husband to refrain from intercourse. 'From the womb' is interpreted as the vagina; thus istihadah (non-menstrual uterine bleeding) is not excluded by this phrase but by 'free from disease'. A woman with a disease of the womb (like istihadah) is not menstruating. However, a woman with a healthy womb who is ill (e.g., with fever) still has her usual menstrual blood counted as hayd. 'Not due to childbirth' excludes postpartum bleeding (nifas), as the womb has a 'disease' due to childbirth. Some said nifas is excluded because the postpartum woman is like a sick person, but Ibn Nujaym considers the first explanation better. The passage also discusses the blood of a young girl (below nine years): it is not hayd but istihadah, though some scholars call it 'corruption' (fasad) rather than istihadah. The commentator defends the definition against criticisms from Fath al-Qadir, arguing that the word 'youngness' is not redundant. The definition indicates that menstrual blood is a ritual impurity (najis), but some scholars consider it an event (hadath) like janabah, leading to a contradiction. The author also notes the cause of menstruation: it originated from Eve's sin and is written by Allah upon the daughters of Adam, as per a hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): The ritual state of a woman due to blood from the womb free from disease and not due to childbirth, with legal restrictions.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Non-menstrual uterine bleeding that occurs due to a disease in the womb, often continuous or outside the normal habit.
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding that follows childbirth, considered a type of disease in the womb due to delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The woman must be free from disease of the womb.

D) The blood must not be due to childbirth.

Key Takeaway
The definition of hayd is precise: blood from the womb of a healthy woman excluding disease and childbirth.

The Pillar and Conditions of Menstruation
Defining the rukn and shurut of hayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pillar and three conditions of menstruation according to the Hanafi school, and explain why the extension of blood is not a pillar.

Core Idea
The pillar of menstruation is the emergence of blood from the womb, not its extension.

Menstruation (hayd) is a key legal state affecting worship and family law. In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author defines the essential component (rukn) and the necessary conditions (shurut) that must be present for bleeding to be considered hayd. Understanding these definitions is critical for correctly applying rulings such as prohibition of prayer, fasting, and the waiting period after divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of hayd
First, locate where the author states the legal definition of menstruation and its pillar. Look for the phrase 'ركنه فهو بروز الدم من محل مخصوص'.
The definition is broad: blood emerging from the womb.
2
Pinpoint the rukn (pillar)
The author explicitly states that the pillar is the emergence of blood from the specific place. Note that this is a direct refutation of the view that the pillar is the extension of the blood cycle.
The rukn is the first appearance of blood, not its continuation.
3
Analyze the critique of the extension view
Read the author's argument against the Nihaya view: if extension were the pillar, the ruling would not be established before the blood spreads. Since the ruling is established immediately upon emergence, the pillar must be the emergence itself.
The author uses a logical proof to refute the alternative.
4
Extract the three conditions (shurut)
The text lists three conditions for blood to be hayd: (1) prior completion of the minimum pure period (15 days truly or legally), (2) the woman is not a minor (age of puberty is 9), and (3) the womb is empty of a deliverable fetus (she is not pregnant with a child that will be born alive).
The third condition applies only if the pregnancy is with a fetus that would be delivered; otherwise, bleeding can be hayd.
5
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on the mubtadi'ah
The passage records a difference: according to most Bukhara scholars, a novice (mubtadi'ah) who sees blood at an age of presumed puberty must abandon prayer and fasting immediately. Abu Hanifa held that she must not abandon until blood continues for three days. The relied-upon view is the Bukhara position.
This ikhtilaf affects when a young girl begins to observe the rulings of hayd.

The first time menstruation was sent down was upon the Children of Israel. Al-Bukhari said: And the Prophet's hadith is greater. Al-Nawawi said: Meaning it is general for all children of Adam. As for its pillar, it is the emergence of blood from a specific place so that rulings are established by it. And from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) is that it is by sensing it. Its manifestation appears in the case where she performs wudu and places a cotton pad, then feels the descent of blood to it before sunset, then removes it after sunset: she must make up the fast according to him, contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), meaning if the moisture from the cotton does not reach the inner labia. If it reaches the moisture from the cotton, it is menstruation and post-childbirth bleeding by agreement, and similarly the nullification of wudu by urine. If she placed it at night, and in the morning she saw purity, she must make up the Isha prayer. If she was pure and saw moisture in the morning, she also makes it up if she had not prayed it before placing it, considering her pure in the first case from the time she placed it, and menstruating in the second case when she removed it, taking precaution in both. This is more sound than what was mentioned in al-Nihaya that its pillar is the extension of the blood cycle from the woman, because the pillar of something is that by which that something is established, and menstruation is not established by it, because the specific extension is a marker for it, not a pillar, because if extension were its pillar, its ruling would not be established before it, and you know that its ruling is established by mere emergence. As for its condition, it is the prior completion of the period of purity, in reality or by legal ruling from the minimum, and not being a minor, and the womb being empty of a fetus that is delivered by birth, because a pregnant woman does not menstruate. We restricted by our saying 'that is delivered' because if something falls from her while its creation is not apparent, then what she sees is according to this: it is menstruation because it is not known to be a fetus, rather it is flesh from the abdomen, so prayer is not waived due to doubt. The verification is that it has the first two conditions. As for what a pregnant woman or a minor sees, it is not from the womb, so the pillar is not found. Not being a minor is known by estimating the minimum age at which she is judged to have reached puberty if she sees blood. There is disagreement on the statements; the chosen one is nine, and the fatwa is upon it, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. If a novice sees blood at an age where she is judged to have reached puberty, she abandons prayer and fasting according to most scholars of Bukhara. From Abu Hanifa: she does not abandon until it continues for three days. Then the most correct is that menstruation is timed until the age of menopause, and most scholars estimate it at sixty years. The scholars of Bukhara and Khwarazm estimate it at fifty-five. So what she sees after that is not menstruation in the apparent madhhab. In al-Mujtaba, and the fatwa in our time is that menopause is judged at fifty. In Sharh al-Wiqaya and al-Mukhtar: if she sees strong blood like black or deep red, it is menstruation and invalidates the waiting period by months before completion, not after. If she sees yellow, greenish, or turbid, it is istihadah. This exposition is far and forced as you know from what preceded, so the apparent is what the investigator said. In al-Nahr: it remains that one must say 'and menopause' because what the menopausal woman sees, i.e., one who has reached fifty-five, in the apparent madhhab is not... In Fath al-Qadir: then the ruling of menopause is only overridden by pure blood in the future, not the past, so that marriages contracted before the recurrence are not invalidated. In al-Qunyah: the judge's decree is not a condition for ruling menopause, and this is the apparent view, so that when she reaches the age of menopause, she observes the waiting period by months and does not need a judge for that. Its time and moment of establishment are known, and its duration, colors, and rulings will come.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq. It first establishes the pillar (rukn) of menstruation: the actual emergence of blood from the specific place (the womb). The author refutes a view that the pillar is the extension of the blood cycle, arguing that the ruling is established immediately upon emergence, not upon extension. Then it lays out the conditions (shurut): (1) a prior complete period of purity (at least 15 days, which is the minimum tuhr), (2) not being a minor (age of puberty is set at 9 years for a girl as the relied-upon view), and (3) the womb being empty of a fetus that would be delivered—meaning the woman is not pregnant with a child that will be born alive. The text explains that if a woman passes something that is not clearly a fetus, it is considered menstruation. Later, it discusses the age of menopause (iyas), with the preferred estimate being 55 years, though some say 60, and the fatwa in the author's time is 50. This is important because after menopause, bleeding is not hayd but istihadah. The passage also touches on colors: strong red or black is hayd; yellow or greenish is istihadah. The overall focus is on defining the legal category of hayd precisely to determine when rulings such as prohibition of prayer, fasting, and sexual intercourse apply.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): The legal state of menstruation, defined by the emergence of blood from the uterine cavity (محل مخصوص), which triggers rulings such as prohibition of prayer and fasting.
الركن (al-rukn): The essential component that constitutes the act or state; here, the emergence of blood from the specific place.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition required for the ruling to apply; here, three conditions: prior purity, not minor, not pregnant (with deliverable fetus).
الإياس (al-iyas): Menopause; the age beyond which a woman does not normally menstruate, estimated at 55 or 60 years (relied-upon 55).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the pillar (rukn) of menstruation?
A) The continuous flow of blood for three days.
B) The emergence of blood from the specific place (womb).
C) The sensing of blood by the woman.
D) The completion of a minimum pure period.

Key Takeaway
The pillar of menstruation is the emergence of blood from the womb, and it is subject to three conditions: prior purity, not being a minor, and not being pregnant with a deliverable fetus.

The Minimum and Maximum Duration of Menstruation: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute over the temporal boundaries of menstrual blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi minimum (3 days) and maximum (10 days) of hayd, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using hadith methodology.

Core Idea
For blood to be menstruation, it must last at least three days and at most ten days; otherwise it is istihadah.

Determining the exact boundaries of menstruation is crucial for a woman's worship: prayers, fasting, and purity are affected. The Hanafi school sets a minimum of three full days and a maximum of ten, relying on multiple weak hadiths that collectively strengthen. The Shafi'i school argues for a one-day minimum and fifteen-day maximum, using a sahih hadith about the quality of blood. This lesson unpacks the evidence, the intra-Hanafi variations, and the strength of each position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds as the minimum and maximum duration of hayd, and note the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.
The zahir al-riwayah is three days and nights minimum, ten days and nights maximum.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the weaker narrations from Abu Yusuf (two days + part of third; three days + two nights) and why they are considered weak (conflict with textual stipulation).
Al-Hasan's narration from Abu Hanifa is deemed weak by al-Bada'i.
3
Follow the Shafi'i opponent's proof
Examine the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh: 'Menstrual blood is black and known…' – how the Shafi'i school infers a one-day minimum.
Al-Nawawi: 'This description exists in a day and a night.'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand why the Hanafi school argues that the Shafi'i proof does not establish a duration (it describes quality, not length), and how the Hanafi hadiths (though individually weak) become hasan via multiple chains.
The Hanafi response also rejects the 'half a lifetime' hadith as false.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Work out the practical difference: a woman who bleeds for 2 days is in istihadah for Hanafis (must pray/fast) but in hayd for Shafi'is (must not pray).
This affects the number of qada prayers required.

Source Text

عمران: ٤١] وقال في موضع آخر ثلاث ليال سوياً [مريم: ١٠] والقصة واحدة. وهذا هو ظاهر الرواية حتى لو رأت عند طلوع الفجر يوم السبت وانقطع عند غروب الشمس يوم الاثنين لا يكون حيضاً. وعن أبي يوسف روايتان: الأولى وهي قوله أنه مقدر بيومين وأكثر الثالث وهو سبع وستون ساعة على ما في العناية عن النوادر. الثانية أنه مقدر بثلاثة أيام وليلتين على ما في التنجيس وفي غيره أنه رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة. وفي البدائع : : رواية الحسن ضعيفة لأن كل واحدة من عدد الأيام والليالي منصوص عليه فلا يجوز أن ينقص عنه وقال الشافعي وأحمد أقله يوم وليلة وأكثره خمسة عشر يوماً لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لفاطمة بنت أبي حبيس : دم الحيض أسود يعرف فإذا كان كذلك فأمسكي الصلاة . أبو داود وغيره بأسانيد صحيحة. قال النووي: وهذه الصفة موجودة في اليوم والليلة. ولنا قوله «أقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام وأكثره عشرة أيام. هكذا ذكره أصحابنا وخرجه الزيلعي المخرج من حديث أبي أمامة وواثلة ومعاذ وأبي سعيد الخدري وأنس بن مالك وعائشة بطرق ضعيفة وأطال الكلام فيها قال في فتح القدير بعد سردها : فهذه عدة أحاديث عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم متعددة الطرق وذلك يرفع الضعيف إلى الحسن والمقدرات الشرعية مما لا تدرك بالرأي فالموقوف فيها حكمه الرفع بل تسكن النفس بكثرة ما روي فيه عن الصحابة والتابعين إلى أن المرفوع مما أجاد فيه ذلك الراوي الضعيف. وبالجملة فله أصل في الشرع بخلاف قولهم أكثره خمسة عشر يوماً لم نعلم فيه حديثاً حسناً ولا ضعيفاً وإنما تمسكوا فيه بما رووه عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال في صفة النساء تمكث إحداكن شطر عمرها لا تصلي وهو لو صح لم يكن فيه حجة. قال البيهقي : إنه لم يجده وقال ابن الجوزي في التحقيق : هذا حديث لا يعرف وأقره عليه صاحب التنقيح .اهـ. وقال النووي في شرح المهذب : إنه حديث باطل لا عن رواه
His statement: 'And its minimum is three days and its maximum is ten days' — The minimum of menstruation is three days, read with nominative or accusative. As for the nominative, it is as the predicate of the subject, and in this case omission is necessary because it is impossible for blood itself to be three days, so the estimated meaning is 'the minimum duration of menstruation'. As for the accusative, it is as an adverb. And it is not hidden that it is not a condition that the blood be continuous for three days without stopping for a single hour, because that only happens rarely. Rather, the blood stopping for an hour or two or more does not invalidate menstruation. Likewise in al-Mustasfa. And the meaning is that its minimum duration is the extent of three days with their nights, and its maximum is the extent of ten days with their nights, as stated explicitly in al-Wafi. It was omitted here because mentioning days in the plural form includes their equivalent of nights. Allah says: 'three days except for a gesture' (Al-Imran 3:41) and in another place: 'three nights sound' (Maryam 19:10), and the story is one. This is the apparent narration, so much so that if she sees blood at dawn on Saturday and it stops at sunset on Monday, it is not menstruation. From Abu Yusuf there are two narrations: The first is his saying that it is estimated at two days and the greater part of a third, which is sixty-seven hours, according to what is in al-'Inayah from al-Nawadir. The second is that it is estimated at three days and two nights, according to what is in al-Tanjees, and in other sources it is the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifah. And in al-Bada'i: The narration of al-Hasan is weak because each one of the number of days and nights is textually stipulated, so it is not permissible to reduce from it. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: Its minimum is a day and a night, and its maximum is fifteen days, due to his saying صلى الله عليه وسلم to Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh: 'Menstrual blood is black and known; if it is so, then refrain from prayer.' Abu Dawud and others narrated it with authentic chains. Al-Nawawi said: This description exists in a day and a night. Our proof is his saying: 'The minimum of menstruation is three days and its maximum is ten days.' This is how our companions mentioned it, and al-Zayla'i extracted it from the hadith of Abu Umamah, Wathilah, Mu'adh, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, Anas ibn Malik, and Aisha through weak chains and discussed it at length. Fath al-Qadir said after listing them: These are several hadiths from the Prophet ﷺ with multiple chains, and that raises the weak to the level of hasan. And the legal estimations are not reached by opinion, so the mawquf (stopped) among them has the ruling of being raised (marfu'). Rather, the heart is at peace with the abundance of what has been narrated from the Companions and Successors to the point that the marfu' is what that weak narrator excelled in. In summary, it has a basis in shari'ah, unlike their saying: its maximum is fifteen days, for which we do not know a hasan nor even a weak hadith. They only rely on what they narrated from him عليه الصلاة والسلام that he said in Sifat al-Nisa': 'One of you spends half of her life not praying.' Even if that were authentic, it would not be a proof. Al-Bayhaqi said: He did not find it. Ibn al-Jawzi said in al-Tahqiq: This hadith is unknown, and the author of al-Tanqih affirmed him. Al-Nawawi said in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab: It is a false hadith, not transmitted by any narrator.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute passage. The author explains the matn's statement about the minimum (three days) and maximum (ten days) of menstruation. He first clarifies that the minimum means three full days and nights, but the blood need not be continuous; short interruptions do not break the continuity of hayd. He then addresses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf has two narrations — one that the minimum is two days plus a third (67 hours), another that it is three days and two nights. But the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is three full days and nights, as stated by the author of al-Wafi. The narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa is considered weak because days and nights are textually stipulated. The author then presents the Shafi'i/Ahmadi position: minimum one day and night, maximum fifteen days, based on the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh about black, known blood. The Hanafi response is that this hadith describes the quality, not the duration, and is silent on the minimum duration. The Hanafi proof is a set of weak hadiths (through multiple chains raising them to hasan level) that explicitly state 'minimum three days, maximum ten days.' The author argues that these hadiths, though individually weak, gain strength through multiplicity. He also criticizes the Shafi'i proof for the maximum of fifteen days as having no basis. The commentary emphasizes that legal estimations in shari'a cannot be based on opinion but must have textual support.

Key Terms
أقل الحيض (aqall al-hayd): The minimum duration for blood to be legally considered menstruation: three full days and nights (72 hours) in the relied-upon Hanafi view.
أكثر الحيض (akthar al-hayd): The maximum duration of a single menstrual period: ten days and nights. Bleeding beyond this is istihadah.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted and relied-upon position from Hanafi sources, here the three-day minimum and ten-day maximum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hadith is weak in its chain of transmission.
B) The hadith describes the color and quality of menstrual blood, not its duration.
C) The hadith specifically says 'day and night' contradicts the Hanafi view.
D) The hadith is abrogated by later verses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi minimum of three days and maximum of ten for hayd is based on explicit hadiths that gain strength through multiple chains, while the Shafi'i counter-evidence addresses quality rather than duration.

Duration of Hayd: Minimum, Maximum, and Istihadah
Defining the minimum and maximum duration of hayd and what constitutes istihadah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi minimum and maximum durations of hayd, identify the proof from the hadith of Umm Salama, and classify bleeding as hayd or istihadah.

Core Idea
Hayd lasts 3 to 10 days; less or more is istihadah.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on the duration of menstruation (hayd). The school sets a minimum of three days and a maximum of ten, based on the hadith of Umm Salama and the reasoning of al-Tahawi. Understanding these limits is crucial for distinguishing valid menstrual blood from irregular bleeding (istihadah), which carries different obligations regarding prayer and fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled? The act of menstruation (hayd). The ruling is that hayd has a defined minimum and maximum duration. Blood outside that range is istihadah.
This is a fiqh ruling that conditions the legal status of bleeding.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Umm Salama where the Prophet instructed her to leave prayer for the number of nights and days she used to menstruate. This hadith is the primary legal proof.
The hadith is from Sunan, and its transmission is considered ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's answer did not ask for a specific amount but said 'the number of nights and days.' The Hanafi scholars derive from this that the maximum is ten days (since 'days' in Arabic can extend to ten) and the minimum is three days (by inference from the general practice and the fact that the Prophet did not specify a shorter duration).
Al-Tahawi's reasoning: the Prophet did not ask about the length of her menses, implying a general rule.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools, particularly the Shafi'is, do not recognize a fixed minimum or maximum for hayd. They rely on different interpretations of the same hadith or other evidence.
The Hanafi position is a mufta bihi (operative) within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعرف وإنما ثبت في الصحيحين تمكث الليالي ما تصلي .اهـ. واحتج الطحاوي للمذهب بحديث أم سلمة إذ سألت عن المرأة تهراق الدماء فقال عليه السلام: لتنظر عدد الليالي والأيام التي كانت تحيضهن من الشهر فلتترك قدر ذلك من الشهر ثم تغتسل وتصلي. فأجابها بذكر عدد الليالي والأيام من غير أن يسألها عن مقدار حيضها قبل ذلك وأكثر ما يتناوله الأيام عشرة وأقله ثلاثة اهـ . وأما ما استدلوا به على أقله فلا دليل فيه لأنه لما جاز أن تكون الصفة موجودة في اليوم والليلة جاز وجودها فيما دونه فلم لم يجعله حيضاً. قوله (فما نقص من ذلك أو زاد استحاضة) أي ما نقص من الأقل أو زاد على الأكثر فهو استحاضة لأن هذا الدم إما أن يكون دم حيض أو نفاس أو استحاضة فانتفى الأولان فتعين الثالث، ولأن تقدير الشرع يمنع إلحاق غيره به
It is known, and it is established in the two Sahihs: 'She stays nights not praying.' End. And al-Tahawi argued for the school with the hadith of Umm Salama when she asked about a woman whose blood flows, and he (peace be upon him) said: 'Let her look at the number of nights and days that she used to menstruate in the month, so let her leave that amount from the month, then bathe and pray.' So he answered her by mentioning the number of nights and days without asking her about the extent of her menses before that. And the maximum that the days encompass is ten, and the minimum is three. End. As for what they used as proof for its minimum, there is no proof in it, because when it is possible that the attribute exists in a day and a night, it is possible for it to exist in less than that, so why did he not make it hayd? His statement: 'So what is less than that or more is istihadah' meaning that what is less than the minimum or more than the maximum is istihadah, because this blood is either blood of hayd, or nifas, or istihadah. The first two are negated, so the third is established. And because the determination of the law prevents attaching other than it to it.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the duration of menstruation (hayd). The author begins by citing a hadith from the Sahihayn that a woman stays nights without praying, indicating the interruption of prayer during menses. Then he mentions al-Tahawi's argument for the Hanafi position using the hadith of Umm Salama: the Prophet instructed her to count the days she used to menstruate and leave that amount, then bathe and pray. The Hanafi school derives from this that the maximum is ten days and the minimum is three days, as these are the common limits. The author then refutes those who argue for a lesser minimum, saying that if an attribute (like being menses) can exist in a day, it does not necessitate it existing in less; the hadith does not prove a minimum less than three. Finally, the passage defines istihadah (irregular bleeding) as any blood less than three days or more than ten days, reasoning by exclusion: the blood is either hayd, nifas, or istihadah; if it is not the first two, it must be the third.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood that occurs within the recognized duration (3-10 days) and has the characteristic colors.
استحاضة (istihadah): Irregular vaginal bleeding that falls outside the parameters of hayd (less than 3 days or more than 10 days).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, hayd has a fixed duration of 3-10 days; blood outside that range is istihadah, with different legal rulings.
