Colors of Menstrual Blood: What Constitutes Hayd
Defining the colors that constitute menstrual blood (hayd).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six colors considered hayd in the Hanafi school, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on kudrah, and apply the ruling to a scenario of colored discharge during menses.

Core Idea
All non-white discharges during the menstrual period are hayd — until the white qassa appears.

After establishing the duration of menstruation, the text now defines its quality: which discharges are considered hayd? The Hanafi school provides a clear rule — any color except pure white counts as hayd during the menstrual days. This ruling is supported by the practice of the Companions and has practical implications for a woman's prayer and fasting. Within the school, there is a subtle difference of opinion regarding cloudy discharge (kudrah) at the beginning of menses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six colors and the exception
List the six colors: black, red, yellow, cloudy (kudrah), green, and earthen. Note the exception: pure white (al-bayad al-khalis) is not considered hayd.
The earthen color (turbiyya) is a type of kudrah.
2
Locate the general principle
The default ruling: all these colors are hayd in the days of menstruation until she sees the pure white discharge (qassa). This is the foundational rule.
This principle is derived from the hadith of Aisha and Umm Atiyya.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that kudrah at the beginning of the menses is not hayd, but at the end it is. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold it is hayd throughout. Identify the evidence for each view.
The text says 'wa lahuma' indicating the majority's proof.
4
Analyze the dalil
The hadith: Aisha told women not to hasten until they see the white qassa. This implies that any color before that is still hayd. Umm Atiyya's statement confirms that kudrah and sufra after purity are disregarded, which implies they are counted during the days.
The hadith is mursal but accepted by the school.
5
Note additional nuances
The ruling considers the color at the time of seeing, not after drying. Green discharge is treated as hayd for regularly menstruating women but not for postmenopausal women.
These nuances show the school's comprehensive approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وما سوى البياض الخالص حيض) لما فرغ من بيان كميته شرع في بيان كيفيته. اعلم أن ألوان الدماء ستة: السواد والحمرة والصفرة والكدرة والخضرة والتربية وهي التي على لون التراب نوع من الكدرة وهي نسبة إلى الترب بمعنى التراب ويقال تربيّة بتشديد الياء وتخفيفها بغير همزة، وتريبة مثل تريعة وتربية بوزن ترعية وقيل هي من الرئة لأنها على لونها. كذا في المغرب. ويقال أيضاً الترابية. وكل هذه الألوان حيض في أيام الحيض إلى أن ترى البياض. وعند أبي يوسف لا تكون الكدرة حيضاً إذا رأتها في أول أيام الحيض، وإذا رأتها في آخرها تكون حيضاً لأنها لو كانت دم رحم لتأخرت عن الصافي ولهما ما روي عن مولاة عائشة قالت: كان النساء يبعثن إلى عائشة بالدرجة التي فيها الكرسف فيه الصفرة من دم الحيض لتنظر إليه فتقول : لا تعجلن حتى ترين القَصَّة البيضاء تريد بذلك الطهر من الحيض. رواه مالك في الموطأ. والقَصَّة بفتح القاف وتشديد الصاد المهملة وذكره البخاري تعليقاً بصيغة الجزم فصح بهذا اللفظ عن عائشة. وذكر في الصحيح والسنن عن أم عطية قالت : كنا لا نعد الكدرة والصفرة بعد الطهر شيئاً وهذا يدل على أنهما في أيام الحيض حيض لأنها قيدت بما بعد الطهر وفي التنجيس: امرأة رأت بياضاً خالصاً على الخرقة ما دام رطباً، أصفر فحكمه حكم البياض لأن المعتبر حال الرؤية لا حالة التغير بعد ذلك اهـ. وكذا لو رأت حمرة أو صفرة فإذا يبست ابيضت يعتبر حالة الرؤية لا حالة التغير بعد ذلك اهـ. ومن المشايخ من أنكر الخضرة فقال : لعلها أكلت قصيلاً استبعاداً لها. قلنا: هي نوع من الكدرة ولعلها أكلت نوعاً من البقول. وفي الهداية وأما الخضرة فالصحيح أن المرأة إذا كانت من ذوات الأقراء يكون حيضاً ويحمل على فساد الغذاء، وإن كانت آيسة لا ترى غير الخضرة يحمل على فساد المنبت فلا يكون حيضاً .اهـ. وفي البدائع قال بعضهم: الكدرة فإذا يبس حيضاً. وأجاب منلاخسرو بأنه مختلف فيه فلا وجه لإدخاله في الحد. قوله : (أكلت قصيلا الخ) القصيل زرع أخضر مقطوع قبل أوانه يقال قصلت الدابة أي علفتها القصيل. قوله : (وإن كانت آية لا ترى غير الخضرة قال في فتح القدير: كونها لا ترى غيرها ليس بقيد على ما ذكره الصدر الشهيد حسام الدين مما قدمناه عنه أول الباب من أن الشرط في نفي كون ما تراه حيضاً أن لا ترى الدم
His statement: 'And whatever except pure white is menstruation' – when he finished explaining its quantity, he began to explain its quality. Know that the colors of blood are six: black, red, yellow, cloudy, green, and earthen, which is the color of dust, a type of cloudy. It is related to 'turb' meaning dust. It is said 'turābiyya' with shadda of ya' or without hamza, and 'turiyya' like 'turi'a', and 'turbiyya' like 'tur'iyya'. It is also said to be from the lung because it is its color. This is in al-Mughrib. It is also called 'turābiyya'. All these colors are menstruation in the days of menstruation until she sees whiteness. According to Abu Yusuf, cloudy is not menstruation if she sees it at the beginning of the days of menstruation, but if she sees it at the end, it is menstruation because if it were womb blood, it would be delayed from the clear. For both (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is what is narrated from the freed slave of Aisha who said: 'The women used to send to Aisha the vessel in which was the cotton with the yellowness of menstrual blood for her to look at it, and she would say: Do not hasten until you see the white qassa, intending by that purity from menstruation.' Malik narrated it in al-Muwatta. Qassa is with fatha of qaf and shadda of sad (the unpointed). Al-Bukhari mentioned it in a definitive way, and thus it is authenticated from Aisha. It is mentioned in al-Sahih and al-Sunan from Umm Atiyya who said: 'We did not consider cloudy and yellow after purity as anything.' This indicates that they are menstruation in the days of menstruation, because she conditioned it with after purity. And in al-Tanjis: A woman saw pure white on the cloth, but as long as it is wet, it is yellow, so its ruling is the ruling of whiteness because what is considered is the state at the time of seeing, not the state of change after that. End. Similarly, if she saw redness or yellowness, and when it dried it became white, the state at the time of seeing is considered, not the state of change after that. End. Some of the mashayikh denied green and said: 'Perhaps she ate green fodder' as a far-fetched possibility. We say: It is a type of cloudy, and perhaps she ate some type of vegetable. In al-Hidayah: 'As for green, the correct view is that if the woman is one who has regular menses, it is menstruation and is attributed to food corruption, but if she is postmenopausal and sees nothing but green, it is attributed to corruption of the origin (womb) and is not menstruation.' End. In al-Bada'i: 'Some said: Cloudy, when it dries, is menstruation.' And Mulla Khusraw replied: 'It is disputed, so there is no justification for including it in the definition.' His statement: 'She ate green fodder etc.' – 'qasil' is green crops cut before their time; it is said 'qasaltu al-dābba' meaning I fed the animal qasil. His statement: 'And if she is postmenopausal and sees nothing but green' – In Fath al-Qadir: 'The condition that she does not see anything else is not a condition according to what Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din mentioned earlier in the chapter: the condition for negating that what she sees is menstruation is that she does not see blood.'
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the colors of menstrual blood. The author first lists six colors: black, red, yellow, cloudy (kudrah), green, and earthen (turbiyya). The default ruling is that all these colors are considered hayd during the menstrual period, until the woman sees pure white discharge (al-qassah al-bayda'). The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that cloudy discharge (kudrah) at the beginning of the menses is not hayd, but at the end it is, because if it were uterine blood, it would not precede the clear blood. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) maintain that it is hayd in both cases, citing the practice of Aisha who instructed women not to stop until they see the white qassa, implying that all colors before that are hayd. The hadith of Umm Atiyya confirms that they did not consider kudrah and sufra after purity as anything, implying that during the days they are considered. There is also a discussion about green discharge: some scholars deny it as hayd, but the preferred view in al-Hidayah is that if a woman has regular cycles, green is hayd and attributed to dietary change; for a postmenopausal woman, it is not hayd. The author also addresses the issue of color change after the blood dries: the ruling is based on the color at the time of seeing, not after it changes. The passage ends with a clarification of the word 'qasil' (green fodder) and a note from Fath al-Qadir about the condition for negating hayd in postmenopausal women.

Key Terms
قصة (qassa): A white discharge that signals the end of menstruation, often described as a white thread-like substance or simply the absence of any color.
كدرة (kudrah): A cloudy or muddy color of discharge, one of the six colors that may be seen during menstruation.
صفرة (sufrah): Yellowish discharge, considered hayd during the menstrual period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a woman seeing yellow discharge (sufrah) during her menstrual days?
A) It is not hayd; she must pray
B) It is hayd and she must not pray
C) It is hayd only if it is continuous
D) It is hayd only at the end of the menses

Key Takeaway
During the menstrual period, all colors of discharge except pure white constitute hayd, and the mufta bihi position is that even kudrah is included throughout.

Rulings on Menstrual Colors and the Debate on al-Qussah
Expansion of fiqh rulings on menstrual colors and purity definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the colors that count as hayd for non-elderly women, explain the exception for elderly women, and argue why the Hanafi school accepts complete cessation (inqita') as the end of menstruation over the literal interpretation of al-qussah.

Core Idea
Complete cessation of discharge suffices for purity; al-qussah is a metaphor for cessation, not a literal white thread.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common confusion: what marks the end of menstruation? While some scholars insisted on seeing a white discharge (qussah), the Hanafi school, based on the hadith, teaches that complete cessation of any colored discharge is sufficient. The author builds a careful argument, distinguishing between the colors that are hayd, the special case of elderly women, and the proper use of the cotton pad to check for purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the colors considered hayd
List earthy (turbah), yellow, and green as menstrual blood for non-elderly women. Note that the same applies to nifas.
The author states these are hayd 'on the absolute' for non-elderly.
2
Understand the exception for elderly women
For elderly women (al-'aja'iz), the color judgment depends on how long the cotton pad was inserted: if short, it's hayd; if long, it's putrefaction, not hayd.
This is based on the possibility of decomposition over time.
3
Analyze the dispute over al-qussah
Some early Hanafis held that seeing the white discharge (qussah) is necessary for purity. The author contrasts this with the majority view that qussah is a metaphor for complete cessation.
The hadith states 'until she sees nothing', supporting cessation.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author prefers cessation, citing the hadith's phrasing and the fact that companions used 'cessation' consistently. The literal interpretation of qussah as a white thread is weak, as indicated by the phrase 'yuqalu' (it is said).
Al-Mughrib is cited to show qussah is metaphorical.
5
Apply the ruling on the cotton pad (kursuf)
Placing the cotton pad is sunna muakkadah for a non-virgin during hayd and mustahabb during purity; it is placed externally and disliked internally.
Praying without the pad is still valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتربة والصفرة والخضرة إنما تكون حيضاً على الإطلاق من غير العجائز، أما في العجائز فينظر إن وجدتها على الكرسف ومدة الوضع قريبة فهي حيض وإن كانت مدة الوضع طويلة لم تكن حيضاً لأن. العجوز يكون منتناً فيتغير الماء فيه لطول المكث، وما عرفت الجواب في هذه الأبواب من الحيض فهو الجواب فيها في النفاس لأنها أخت الحيض اهـ . وفي معراج الدراية معزياً إلى فجر الأئمة: لو أفتى مفت بشيء من هذه الأقوال في موضع الضرورة طلباً للتيسير كان حسناً .اهـ. وفي فتح القدير: ومقتضى المروي في الموطأ والبخاري أن مجرد الانقطاع دون رؤية القصة لا يجب معه أحكام الطاهرات، وكلام أنه قد الأصحاب فيما يأتي كله بلفظ الانقطاع حيث يقولون : وإذا انقطع دمها فكذا مع يكون انقطاع بجفاف من وقت إلى وقت ثم ترى القَصَّة فإن كانت الغاية القصة لم تجب تلك الصلاة، وإن كان الانقطاع على سائر الألوان وجبت وأنا متردد فيما هو الحكم عندهم بالنظر إلى دليلهم وعباراتهم في اعطاء الأحكام والله أعلم. ورأيت في مروي عبد الوهاب عن يحيى بن سعيد عن ربطة مولاة عمرة عن عمرة أنها كانت تقول للنساء : إذا أدخلت إحداكن الكرسف فخرجت متغيرة فلا تصلي حتى لا ترى شيئاً، وهذا يقتضي أن الغاية الانقطاع اهـ. وقد يقال : هذا التردد لا يتم إلا إذا فسرت القَصَّة بأنها بياض ممتد كالخيط، والظاهر من كلامهم ضعف هذا التفسير فقد قال في المغرب قال أبو عبيدة: معناه أن تخرج القطنة أو الخرقة التي تحتشي بها المرأة كأنها قصة لا تخالطها صفرة ولا تربية، ويقال إن القصة شيء كالخيط الأبيض يخرج بعد انقطاع الدم كله ويجوز أن يراد انتفاء اللون وأن لا يبقى منه أثر البتة فضرب رؤية القصة مثلاً لذلك لأن رائي القصة غير رائي شيء من سائر ألوان الحائض .اهـ. فقد علمت أن القصة مجاز عن الانقطاع وأن تفسيرها بأنها شيء كالخيط ذكره بصيغة يقال الدالة على التمريض، ويدل على أن المراد بها الانقطاع آخر الحديث وهو قوله «تريد بذلك الطهر من الحيض فثبت بهذا أن دليلهم موافق لعباراتهم كما لا يخفى. وفي شرح الوقاية ثم وضع الكرسف مستحب للبكر في الحيض وللثيب في كل حال وموضعه موضع البكارة ويكره في الفرج الداخل .اهـ. وفي غيره أنه سنة للثيب حالة الحيض مستحبة حالة الطهر ولو صلتا بغير كرسف جاز .
And the earth-like (turbah), yellow, and green [discharges] are considered menstruation absolutely, except for elderly women. As for elderly women, it is considered: if she finds it on the cotton and the period of placement is short, then it is menstruation; and if the period of placement is long, it is not menstruation because an elderly woman's [discharge] becomes putrid and the water changes due to long retention. And whatever you know of the answer in these chapters of menstruation, that is the answer concerning lochia (nifas) because it is the sister of menstruation. End. In Miraj al-Dirayah, citing Fahr al-A'imma: If a mufti gives a fatwa with some of these opinions in a case of necessity seeking ease, that is good. End. In Fath al-Qadir: The purport of what is narrated in al-Muwatta' and al-Bukhari is that mere cessation without seeing the white discharge (qussah) does not necessitate the rulings of purity. And his statement: 'that the companions' etc. All the [later] statements use the word 'cessation', where they say: 'and when her blood ceases, then such and such' — with cessation by dryness from time to time, then she sees the qussah. If the end is the qussah, that prayer is not obligatory; if the cessation is with other colors, it becomes obligatory. And I am uncertain what the ruling is according to them considering their evidence and expressions in assigning rulings, and Allah knows best. And I saw in the narration of 'Abd al-Wahhab from Yahya ibn Sa'id from Rabtah, the freed slave of 'Amrah, from 'Amrah that she used to say to women: If one of you inserts the cotton and it comes out changed, do not pray until you see nothing. This indicates that the end is cessation. End. And it may be said: This uncertainty is not complete unless al-qussah is interpreted as a white thread-like discharge. The apparent from their statements is that this interpretation is weak. For it is said in al-Mughrib: Abu 'Ubaydah said: Its meaning is that the cotton or cloth that the woman uses emerges like a qussah, not mixed with yellowness or [other]. It is said that al-qussah is something like a white thread that emerges after all blood has ceased, and it is permissible that it means the absence of color and that no trace of it remains at all. So he gave the example of seeing the qussah for that, because the one who sees the qussah does not see anything of the other colors of the menstruating woman. End. So you know that al-qussah is a metaphor for cessation, and its interpretation as a thread-like thing was mentioned with the phrase 'it is said' indicating weakness. And the end of the hadith indicates that what is meant by it is cessation: his statement 'she intends by that purity from menstruation.' So it is established that their evidence agrees with their expressions, as is not hidden. And in Sharh al-Wiqaya: Then placing the cotton is recommended (mustahabb) for a virgin during menstruation and for a non-virgin in every case, and its place is the place of virginity, and it is disliked in the inner vagina. End. And in other [books], it is a sunna for a non-virgin during menstruation and recommended during purity, and if they pray without cotton, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of rulings related to the colors of menstrual discharge and the definition of purity (tuhur). The author discusses that earthy, yellow, and green discharges are considered hayd for non-elderly women. For elderly women, the judgment depends on the duration the cotton pad was inserted: if short, it's hayd; if long, it's not, because their discharge may become putrid with prolonged retention. The same rulings apply to nifas (postpartum bleeding). The text then cites a fatwa permitting leniency in cases of necessity. A central debate is presented: whether mere cessation of blood suffices for purity, or if seeing the white discharge (qussah) is required. Citing narrations from Muwatta and Bukhari, some scholars hold that only qussah indicates purity. However, other Hanafi authorities interpret qussah metaphorically as complete cessation. The author resolves this by showing that the companions' expressions using the word 'cessation' align with the hadith evidence. The passage concludes with rulings on using a cotton pad (kursuf): it is recommended for virgins during hayd and for non-virgins at all times, placed externally, and disliked internally; it is sunna for non-virgins during hayd but only recommended during purity.

Key Terms
القصة (al-qussah): In this passage, al-qussah is the white discharge that some consider the sign of purity from hayd; the author argues it is a metaphor for complete cessation.
الكرسف (al-kursuf): A cotton pad inserted by a woman to check for menstrual discharge.
العجائز (al-'aja'iz): Elderly women, for whom some discharge may be due to putrefaction rather than hayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi view on the end of menstruation?
A) The woman must see a white discharge (qussah).
B) The woman must wait for three consecutive days without bleeding.
C) The end is marked by complete cessation of any colored discharge.
D) The end is marked by the appearance of the yellow discharge.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a woman's menstruation ends when any colored discharge ceases completely; the white discharge (qussah) is a metaphor for this cessation, not a required condition.

The Fourteen Rulings of Hayd: Prohibitions and Invalidities
Lists fourteen rulings of hayd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fourteen rulings of hayd and identify which acts are prohibited and which are invalidated.

Core Idea
Menstruation prohibits prayer, fasting, and many acts – but the obligation of prayer never attaches due to hayd.

In this passage, the commentator lists fourteen specific rulings that apply to a menstruating woman. These cover purification, prayer, fasting, Quran recitation, and more. Understanding each ruling helps you apply the correct hukm in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each of the fourteen rulings, note whether the act is prohibited (haram) or invalidated (batal) or prevented (mumtani).
E.g., prayer is both prohibited and invalid, while tawaf is prohibited but not invalid.
2
Distinguish prohibition from invalidity
Some acts are haram but still valid if performed (like tawaf), while others are invalid entirely (like prayer).
This distinction matters for compensation.
3
Understand the exception of wudu
Wudu during hayd is invalid for prayer but recommended for dhikr. The ruling says 'prevents validity' for purification.
She may do wudu for the reward of dhikr, not for tahara.
4
Differentiate prayer and fasting in obligation
Prayer obligation is prevented entirely; fasting obligation remains (must be made up later). The text explains why: hardship of makeup.
At most 15 days of fasting makeup per year vs many prayers.
5
Apply the list to a practical scenario
Given a menstruating woman, determine what she may and may not do based on the fourteen rulings.
Focus on common acts: prayer, fasting, Quran, mosque, dhikr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (يمنع صلاة وصوماً) شروع في بيان أحكامه فذكر بعضها ولا بأس ببيانها فنقول : إن الحيض يتعلق به أحكام أحدها يمنع صحة الطهارة وأما أغسال الحج فإنها تأتي بها لأن القصيل زرع أخضر مقطوع قبل أوانه يقال قصلت الدابة أي علفتها القصيل. قوله : (وإن كانت آية لا ترى غير الخضرة قال في فتح القدير: كونها لا ترى غيرها ليس بقيد على ما ذكره الصدر الشهيد حسام الدين مما قدمناه عنه أول الباب من أن الشرط في نفي كون ما تراه حيضاً أن لا ترى الدم المقصود منها التنظيف لا الطهارة وأما تحريم الطهارة عليها فنقول في شرح المهذب للنووي، وأما أئمتنا فقالوا: إنه يستحب لها أن تتوضأ لوقت كل صلاة وتقعد على مصلاها تسبح وتهلل وتكبر، وفي رواية يكتب لها ثواب أحسن صلاة كانت تصلي. وصحح في الظهيرية أنها تجلس مقدار أداء فرض الصلاة كيلا تنسى العادة. الثاني يمنع وجوب الصلاة وهو ظاهر ما في الكتاب وظاهر ما في القدوري أيضاً فإنه قال : والحيض يسقط فأفاد ظاهراً عدم تعلق أصل الوجوب بها، وهذا لأن تعلقه فائدته يستتبع وهي . إما الأداء أو القضاء والأول منتف لقيام الحدث مع العجز عن رفعه، والثاني كذلك فضلاً منه تعالى دفعاً للحرج اللازم بإلزام القضاء لتضاعف الواجبات خصوصاً فيمن عادتها أكثره، فانتفى الوجوب لانتفاء فائدته لا لعدم أهليتها للخطاب، ولذا تعلق بها خطاب الصوم لعدم الحرج إذ غاية ما تقضي في السنة خمسة عشر يوماً إذا كان حيضها عشرة، وبهذا اندفع ما في النهاية ومعراج الدراية وغيرهما من أن قوله «يسقط» يقتضي سابقة الوجوب عليها ويقولون إنه قول أبي زيد، وأما على قول عامة المشايخ لا يجب، وقد نقل النووي الإجماع على سقوط وجوب الصلاة عنها. الثالث يحرمها. الرابع يمنع صحتها الخامس يحرم الصوم. السادس يمنع صحته وأما أنه يمنع وجوبه فلا لما قدمنا وسيأتي إيضاحه. السابع يحرم مس المصحف وحمله. الثامن يحرم قراءة القرآن. التاسع يحرم دخول المسجد. العاشر يحرم سجود التلاوة والشكر ويمنع صحته. الحادي عشر يحرم الاعتكاف. الثاني عشر يمنع صحته الثالث عشر يفسده إذا طرأ عليه. الرابع عشر يحرم الطواف من جهتين دخول المسجد وترك الطهارة له لكن لا يمنع صحته كما هو المشهور من الخالص. قوله : (وبهذا اندفع ما في النهاية ومعراج الدراية (الخ قال العلامة الشيخ إسمعيل النابلسي في شرح الدرر والغرر : فيه بحث لأن قوله يفيد ظاهر الخ» ممنوع لأن السقوط مقتضاه سبق تكليف ظاهراً، به، ولو قال المراد بالتكليف السابق الذي سقط . هو ما كان قبل وجود العذر لكان وجهه وعليه يتساوى المنع مع السقوط فليتأمل. وأما حكاية النووي الإجماع فلا ترد على أبي زيد فإنه سابق على النووي فإنه توفي سنة ٤٣٥ والنووي مولده في المحرم سنة ٦٣١ بل اختياره والخلاف المتقدم وارد على الإجماع إن لم يرد به المذهبي .اهـ. كذا نقله بعض الفضلاء وقال بعده قلت : الذي حكاه النووي إجماع الأمة فلا يصح حمله على المذهبي. قال في شرح المهذب : أجمعت الأمة على أن الحيض يحرم عليها الصلاة فرضها ونفلها وأجمعوا على أنه يسقط عنها فرض الصلاة فلا تقضي إذا طهرت اهـ. أقول : ثم قوله : ولو قال المراد بالتكليف السابق الخ قد يقال إنه غير ظاهر بل الظاهر ما قاله المؤلف لأنه لو قال ذلك لما شمل المبتدأة بالحيض إذ لا وجوب عليها قبله اللهم إلا أن يجاب بأنه بناء على الغالب، ولعله لما قلنا أشار بقوله فليتأمل هذا. وقد دفع في النهر المنافاة من أصلها فقال : وكون عبارة القدوري ظاهرة فيما قال تبع فيه صاحب الفتح، ولقائل منعه إذ سقوط الشيء فرع وجوده وحكاية الإجماع لا تنافي ما قاله الدبوسي في أصوله إذ السقوط قدر متفق عليه لكن هل بعد تعلق
His saying: (It prevents prayer and fasting) is a beginning in explaining its rulings. He mentioned some of them, and there is no harm in explaining them. So we say: Menstruation has rulings attached to it. First: It prevents the validity of purification. As for the baths of Hajj, she performs them because... al-qasil is green fodder cut before its time; it is said: I fed the animal qasil. His saying: (And if she sees only green) – he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'That she sees only green is not a condition' according to what al-Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din mentioned, which we presented earlier at the beginning of the chapter: that the condition for denying that what she sees is menstruation is that she does not see blood. What is intended by it is cleaning, not purification. As for the prohibition of purification upon her, we say in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab by al-Nawawi: Our imams said: It is recommended for her to perform wudu' at the time of every prayer and sit on her prayer mat, glorifying, saying la ilaha illa Allah, and saying takbir. In one narration, she is rewarded with the reward of the best prayer she used to pray. And it is confirmed in al-Zahiriyya that she sits for the duration of performing the obligatory prayer so that she does not forget the habit. Second: It prevents the obligation of prayer. This is apparent from the book and apparent from al-Quduri as well, for he said: 'Menstruation lifts (yusqitu) [the obligation].' So it apparently indicates the non-attachment of the original obligation to her. This is because its attachment has a benefit that is necessary, which is either performance or makeup. The first is negated due to the presence of the state of impurity with inability to remove it, and the second also [is negated] out of grace from Allah to prevent hardship necessitated by requiring makeup due to the multiplication of obligations, especially for one whose habit is most of it. So the obligation is negated due to the negation of its benefit, not because she is not fit for the address. Hence the address of fasting clings to her because there is no hardship, since at most she makes up fifteen days in the year if her menstruation is ten days. And by this is refuted what is in al-Nihaya and Miraj al-Dirayah and others that his saying 'yusqitu' implies prior obligation upon her, and they say it is the view of Abu Zayd; but according to the view of general scholars, it is not obligatory. And al-Nawawi transmitted consensus on the lifting of the obligation of prayer from her. Third: It prohibits her. Fourth: It prevents its validity. Fifth: It prohibits fasting. Sixth: It prevents its validity. As for preventing its obligation, no, as we have presented and will clarify. Seventh: It prohibits touching and carrying the mushaf. Eighth: It prohibits reciting the Quran. Ninth: It prohibits entering the mosque. Tenth: It prohibits the prostration of recitation and gratitude and prevents its validity. Eleventh: It prohibits i'tikaf. Twelfth: It prevents its validity. Thirteenth: It invalidates it if it occurs during it. Fourteenth: It prohibits tawaf from two aspects: entering the mosque and leaving purification for it, but it does not prevent its validity as is well-known from al-Khalis. His saying: (And by this is refuted what is in al-Nihaya and Miraj al-Dirayah, etc.) – al-Allama al-Shaykh Isma'il al-Nabulsi said in Sharh al-Durar wa al-Ghurur: There is discussion in it because his saying 'it apparently indicates' is prevented, because 'lifting' implies prior obligation apparently. And if he had said: 'What is meant by the prior obligation that is lifted is what was before the existence of the excuse,' that would have a face, and then prevention and lifting are equal. So let it be considered. As for al-Nawawi's transmission of consensus, it does not refute Abu Zayd, for he is before al-Nawawi – he died in 435 AH and al-Nawawi was born in 631 AH. Rather, his choice and the presented disagreement affect the consensus if the latter is not meant as [consensus of] the school. End. This was transmitted by some virtuous people, and after it he said: I say: What al-Nawawi narrated is the consensus of the umma, so it cannot be applied to the school consensus. He said in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab: The umma agreed that menstruation prohibits prayer, obligatory and voluntary, and they agreed that the obligatory prayer falls from her (yusqitu) so she does not make it up when she becomes pure. End. I say: Then his saying: 'And if he had said: What is meant by the prior obligation...' it may be said that it is not apparent; rather, what is apparent is what the author said, because if he had said that, it would not include the woman who first menstruates, since there is no obligation upon her before. However, it may be answered that it is based on the majority. And perhaps for what we said, he indicated with his saying 'let it be considered'. And in al-Nahr, the contradiction was refuted from its root, and he said: 'And that the expression of al-Quduri is apparent in what he said is a follow-up of the author of al-Fath, but one may prevent it because the lifting of something is branch of its existence, and the transmission of consensus does not contradict what al-Dabusi said in his usul, for lifting is agreed upon, but whether after attachment...'
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn statement 'It (menstruation) prevents prayer and fasting.' The commentator lists fourteen rulings of hayd in detail. The first ruling: it prevents the validity of purification (wudu/ghusl), but the baths for Hajj are still performed for cleansing, not for purity. It is recommended for a menstruating woman to perform wudu at each prayer time and sit in dhikr. The second ruling: it prevents the obligation of prayer. The commentator explains that hayd lifts the obligation not because the woman is incapable of address, but because the benefit of obligation (performance or makeup) is absent. The obligation of prayer is removed due to the hardship of makeup, especially for those with long cycles. In contrast, fasting is still obligatory because makeup is easy (max 15 days/year). This argument refutes the view that the term 'yusqitu' (lifts) implies prior obligation, as some like Abu Zayd held. The commentator defends the general view that hayd prevents obligation from the start. He then lists the remaining rulings: prohibition and invalidity of prayer, prohibition of fasting, and its invalidity, prohibition of touching mushaf, reciting Quran, entering mosque, sujud al-tilawa, i'tikaf, and tawaf. The passage ends with a detailed discussion of the term 'yusqitu' vs 'yamna'u', citing al-Nabulsi and al-Nawawi, and concludes that the preferred view is that hayd prevents obligation rather than lifting it after attachment.

Key Terms
يسقط (yusqitu): In this passage, it is debated whether this word means that the obligation of prayer is lifted after being established (Abu Zayd) or that the obligation never attaches (majority).
يمنع (yamna'u): Prevents: the ruling of hayd that prayer does not become obligatory at all.
العذر (al-'udhr): Excuse: here, menstruation is an excuse that prevents the obligation of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acts is prohibited during hayd but still valid if performed?
A) Fasting
B) Prayer
C) Tawaf
D) Touching the mushaf

Key Takeaway
The fourteen rulings of hayd include prohibitions and invalidities, but the woman remains obligated to make up fasts and may engage in dhikr and permissible acts.

Taharah — Purification
advanced

Dispute Over 'Yusqitu' vs 'Yamna'u': Does Hayd Lift Obligation or Prevent It?
Discusses the linguistic and legal dispute over 'yusqitu'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether hayd lifts an existing obligation of prayer or prevents its attachment, and state the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Core Idea
The term 'yusqitu' in al-Quduri is interpreted by the majority as 'prevents' obligation, not 'lifts' a pre-existing one.

This passage delves into a subtle but important disagreement among Hanafi scholars. At issue is the precise meaning of the phrase 'الحيض يسقط' (hayd lifts) in al-Quduri. Does it mean that prayer was obligatory and then removed, or that it never becomes obligatory? The answer affects how we understand the legal capacity of a menstruating woman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Abu Zayd says 'yusqitu' means lifts after attachment; the majority says 'yamna'u' (prevents) obligation from the start.
The difference hinges on whether obligation ever attached before hayd.
2
Examine the majority's proof
If obligation attached, its benefit would be negated – performance impossible due to impurity, makeup causes hardship. Therefore, the obligation is negated because its benefit is negated.
This is a talil based on maqasid/shari'a purpose.
3
Consider the counter-argument
The literal meaning of 'yusqitu' implies prior existence. Also al-Nawawi claimed consensus on the lifting of obligation.
These are textual arguments.
4
Analyze the response
The term can be reinterpreted contextually; the consensus of Nawawi is countered by earlier disagreement (Abu Zayd predates Nawawi).
The commentator uses the benefit argument to refute the literal interpretation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority view is relied upon because it avoids the problem of the mubtadi'a (first-time menstruator) and respects the principle of hardship removal.
This is the operative position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قصلت الدابة أي علفتها القصيل. قوله : (وإن كانت آية لا ترى غير الخضرة قال في فتح القدير: كونها لا ترى غيرها ليس بقيد على ما ذكره الصدر الشهيد حسام الدين مما قدمناه عنه أول الباب من أن الشرط في نفي كون ما تراه حيضاً أن لا ترى الدم المقصود منها التنظيف لا الطهارة وأما تحريم الطهارة عليها فنقول في شرح المهذب للنووي، وأما أئمتنا فقالوا: إنه يستحب لها أن تتوضأ لوقت كل صلاة وتقعد على مصلاها تسبح وتهلل وتكبر، وفي رواية يكتب لها ثواب أحسن صلاة كانت تصلي. وصحح في الظهيرية أنها تجلس مقدار أداء فرض الصلاة كيلا تنسى العادة. الثاني يمنع وجوب الصلاة وهو ظاهر ما في الكتاب وظاهر ما في القدوري أيضاً فإنه قال : والحيض يسقط فأفاد ظاهراً عدم تعلق أصل الوجوب بها، وهذا لأن تعلقه فائدته يستتبع وهي . إما الأداء أو القضاء والأول منتف لقيام الحدث مع العجز عن رفعه، والثاني كذلك فضلاً منه تعالى دفعاً للحرج اللازم بإلزام القضاء لتضاعف الواجبات خصوصاً فيمن عادتها أكثره، فانتفى الوجوب لانتفاء فائدته لا لعدم أهليتها للخطاب، ولذا تعلق بها خطاب الصوم لعدم الحرج إذ غاية ما تقضي في السنة خمسة عشر يوماً إذا كان حيضها عشرة، وبهذا اندفع ما في النهاية ومعراج الدراية وغيرهما من أن قوله «يسقط» يقتضي سابقة الوجوب عليها ويقولون إنه قول أبي زيد، وأما على قول عامة المشايخ لا يجب، وقد نقل النووي الإجماع على سقوط وجوب الصلاة عنها. الثالث يحرمها. الرابع يمنع صحتها الخامس يحرم الصوم. السادس يمنع صحته وأما أنه يمنع وجوبه فلا لما قدمنا وسيأتي إيضاحه. السابع يحرم مس المصحف وحمله. الثامن يحرم قراءة القرآن. التاسع يحرم دخول المسجد. العاشر يحرم سجود التلاوة والشكر ويمنع صحته. الحادي عشر يحرم الاعتكاف. الثاني عشر يمنع صحته الثالث عشر يفسده إذا طرأ عليه. الرابع عشر يحرم الطواف من جهتين دخول المسجد وترك الطهارة له لكن لا يمنع صحته كما هو المشهور من الخالص. قوله : (وبهذا اندفع ما في النهاية ومعراج الدراية (الخ قال العلامة الشيخ إسمعيل النابلسي في شرح الدرر والغرر : فيه بحث لأن قوله يفيد ظاهر الخ» ممنوع لأن السقوط مقتضاه سبق تكليف ظاهراً، به، ولو قال المراد بالتكليف السابق الذي سقط . هو ما كان قبل وجود العذر لكان وجهه وعليه يتساوى المنع مع السقوط فليتأمل. وأما حكاية النووي الإجماع فلا ترد على أبي زيد فإنه سابق على النووي فإنه توفي سنة ٤٣٥ والنووي مولده في المحرم سنة ٦٣١ بل اختياره والخلاف المتقدم وارد على الإجماع إن لم يرد به المذهبي .اهـ. كذا نقله بعض الفضلاء وقال بعده قلت : الذي حكاه النووي إجماع الأمة فلا يصح حمله على المذهبي. قال في شرح المهذب : أجمعت الأمة على أن الحيض يحرم عليها الصلاة فرضها ونفلها وأجمعوا على أنه يسقط عنها فرض الصلاة فلا تقضي إذا طهرت اهـ. أقول : ثم قوله : ولو قال المراد بالتكليف السابق الخ قد يقال إنه غير ظاهر بل الظاهر ما قاله المؤلف لأنه لو قال ذلك لما شمل المبتدأة بالحيض إذ لا وجوب عليها قبله اللهم إلا أن يجاب بأنه بناء على الغالب، ولعله لما قلنا أشار بقوله فليتأمل هذا. وقد دفع في النهر المنافاة من أصلها فقال : وكون عبارة القدوري ظاهرة فيما قال تبع فيه صاحب الفتح، ولقائل منعه إذ سقوط الشيء فرع وجوده وحكاية الإجماع لا تنافي ما قاله الدبوسي في أصوله إذ السقوط قدر متفق عليه لكن هل بعد تعلق
His saying: (It prevents prayer and fasting) is a beginning in explaining its rulings. He mentioned some of them, and there is no harm in explaining them. So we say: Menstruation has rulings attached to it. First: It prevents the validity of purification. As for the baths of Hajj, she performs them because... al-qasil is green fodder cut before its time; it is said: I fed the animal qasil. His saying: (And if she sees only green) – he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'That she sees only green is not a condition' according to what al-Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din mentioned, which we presented earlier at the beginning of the chapter: that the condition for denying that what she sees is menstruation is that she does not see blood. What is intended by it is cleaning, not purification. As for the prohibition of purification upon her, we say in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab by al-Nawawi: Our imams said: It is recommended for her to perform wudu' at the time of every prayer and sit on her prayer mat, glorifying, saying la ilaha illa Allah, and saying takbir. In one narration, she is rewarded with the reward of the best prayer she used to pray. And it is confirmed in al-Zahiriyya that she sits for the duration of performing the obligatory prayer so that she does not forget the habit. Second: It prevents the obligation of prayer. This is apparent from the book and apparent from al-Quduri as well, for he said: 'Menstruation lifts (yusqitu) [the obligation].' So it apparently indicates the non-attachment of the original obligation to her. This is because its attachment has a benefit that is necessary, which is either performance or makeup. The first is negated due to the presence of the state of impurity with inability to remove it, and the second also [is negated] out of grace from Allah to prevent hardship necessitated by requiring makeup due to the multiplication of obligations, especially for one whose habit is most of it. So the obligation is negated due to the negation of its benefit, not because she is not fit for the address. Hence the address of fasting clings to her because there is no hardship, since at most she makes up fifteen days in the year if her menstruation is ten days. And if he had said: 'What is meant by the prior obligation that is lifted is what was before the existence of the excuse,' that would have a face, and then prevention and lifting are equal. So let it be considered. As for al-Nawawi's transmission of consensus, it does not refute Abu Zayd, for he is before al-Nawawi – he died in 435 AH and al-Nawawi was born in 631 AH. Rather, his choice and the presented disagreement affect the consensus if the latter is not meant as [consensus of] the school. End. This was transmitted by some virtuous people, and after it he said: I say: What al-Nawawi narrated is the consensus of the umma, so it cannot be applied to the school consensus. He said in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab: The umma agreed that menstruation prohibits prayer, obligatory and voluntary, and they agreed that the obligatory prayer falls from her (yusqitu) so she does not make it up when she becomes pure. Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn statement 'It (menstruation) prevents prayer and fasting.' The commentator lists fourteen rulings of hayd in detail. The first ruling: it prevents the validity of purification (wudu/ghusl), but the baths for Hajj are still performed for cleansing, not for purity. It is recommended for a menstruating woman to perform wudu at each prayer time and sit in dhikr. The second ruling: it prevents the obligation of prayer. The commentator explains that hayd lifts the obligation not because the woman is incapable of address, but because the benefit of obligation (performance or makeup) is absent. The obligation of prayer is removed due to the hardship of makeup, especially for those with long cycles. In contrast, fasting is still obligatory because makeup is easy (max 15 days/year). This argument refutes the view that the term 'yusqitu' (lifts) implies prior obligation, as some like Abu Zayd held. The commentator defends the general view that hayd prevents obligation from the start. He then lists the remaining rulings: prohibition and invalidity of prayer, prohibition of fasting, and its invalidity, prohibition of touching mushaf, reciting Quran, entering mosque, sujud al-tilawa, i'tikaf, and tawaf. The passage ends with a detailed discussion of the term 'yusqitu' vs 'yamna'u', citing al-Nabulsi and al-Nawawi, and concludes that the preferred view is that hayd prevents obligation rather than lifting it after attachment.

Key Terms
يسقط (yusqitu): In this passage, it is debated whether this word means that the obligation of prayer is lifted after being established (Abu Zayd) or that the obligation never attaches (majority).
يمنع (yamna'u): Prevents: the ruling of hayd that prayer does not become obligatory at all.
العذر (al-'udhr): Excuse: here, menstruation is an excuse that prevents the obligation of prayer.
المبتدأة (al-mubtadi'a): A woman who menstruates for the first time. The passage mentions that Abu Zayd's view would not include her if prior obligation is meant.

What is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) opinion regarding the obligation of prayer during hayd?
A) It was obligatory and then lifted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi is that hayd prevents the obligation of prayer (yamna'u), not that it lifts a pre-existing obligation.

The 22 Rulings of Nifas and the 5 Exclusive to Hayd
Enumeration of rulings with correction of earlier jurists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 22 rulings that apply to nifas and identify which five are exclusive to hayd.

Core Idea
Of the 22 rulings of nifas, five are exclusive to hayd: ‘iddah, istibra, puberty, divorce separation, and fast continuity.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the full scope of rulings that apply to postpartum bleeding (nifas) and corrects the misconception that the sets of rulings for hayd and nifas are identical. The author enumerates 22 rulings associated with nifas and highlights five that are specific to menstruation (hayd). Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying taharah and family law rulings.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the full list of 22 rulings
Identify that the passage references rulings from the 15th to the 22nd (mentioned explicitly) and implies the first 14 were discussed earlier. These rulings cover prohibitions (e.g., Tawaf, intercourse, divorce), obligations (ghusl), and legal effects (puberty, ‘iddah, istibra, fast continuity).
The passage begins with 'fifteenth' because the first 14 were covered in preceding text.
2
Identify the five exclusive rulings
Locate the sentence: 'except five: completion of waiting period, istibra, the ruling of her attaining puberty, the separation between two divorces of bid'ah, and not breaking continuity in the fast.' These are specific to hayd and do not apply to nifas.
Note the slight OCR uncertainty: 'انقضاء السنة' likely means 'انقضاء العدة' (completion of 'iddah).
3
Understand why these five are exclusive
Each exclusive ruling depends on the passage of a specific number of days (e.g., ‘iddah, istibra) or a clear distinction between hayd and istihadah (e.g., puberty, divorce separation); nifas has no minimum duration (maximum 40 days), so it cannot serve these purposes.
For fast continuity: hayd breaks tawali (consecutive days) in expiation oaths; nifas does not because it is longer.
4
Appreciate the correction of earlier sources
The author notes that earlier works (al-Nihaya, Mi'raj al-Diraya) claimed only 12 rulings for both, with 8 shared and 4 exclusive to hayd. The passage asserts this is not comprehensive; the correct number is 22 for nifas, with only 5 exclusives.
This correction is typical in later Hanafi commentaries (mutakhkhirin) refining earlier matns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقضاء السنة مذهبنا فاندفع به ما نقله النووي في شرح المهذب من نقل الإجماع على عدم صحة طوافها مطلقاً. الخامس عشر يمنع وجوب طواف الصدر السادس عشر يحرم الوطء وما هو في حكمه السابع عشر يحرم الطلاق الثامن عشر تبلغ به الصبية. التاسع عشر يتعلق به العدة. العشرون يتعلق به الاستبراء الحادي والعشرون يوجب الغسل بشرط الانقطاع على ما حققناه الثاني والعشرون لا يقطع التتابع في صوم كفارة القتل والفطر بخلاف كفارة اليمين ونحوها حيث تقطع على ما حققه الإمام الدبوسي في التقويم. وهذه الأحكام كلها متعلقة بالنفاس إلا خمسة وهي : انقضاء العدة والاستبراء والحكم ببلوغها والفصل بين طلاقي والبدعة وعدم قطع التتابع في الصوم فإن هذه مختصة بالحيض، فظهر بما قررناه أن ما في النهاية ومعراج الدراية وغيرهما من أن أحكام الحيض والنفاس اثنا عشر ثمانية مشتركة وأربعة مختصة بالحيض ليس بجامع.
The completion of the year? Our madhhab, and it is repelled by what al-Nawawi transmitted in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab from transmission of consensus on the invalidity of her Tawaf absolutely. Fifteenth: prevents obligation of Farewell Tawaf. Sixteenth: prohibits intercourse and what is in its ruling. Seventeenth: prohibits divorce. Eighteenth: the girl attains puberty by it. Nineteenth: the waiting period is established by it. Twentieth: istibra is established by it. Twenty-first: necessitates ghusl with the condition of cessation according to what we verified. Twenty-second: does not break the continuity in the fast of expiation for killing and breaking fast, contrary to expiation of oath and similar where it breaks according to what Imam al-Dabusi verified in al-Taqwim. And all these rulings are related to nifas except five: completion of waiting period, istibra, the ruling of her attaining puberty, the separation between two divorces of bid'ah, and not breaking continuity in fast. Indeed these are specific to hayd. So it is clear from what we established that what is in al-Nihaya and Mi'raj al-Diraya and others that the rulings of hayd and nifas are twelve, eight shared and four specific to hayd, is not comprehensive.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of rulings related to nifas (postpartum bleeding) and identifies which of them are exclusive to hayd (menstruation). The author lists rulings from the fifteenth to the twenty-second, showing that 22 rulings are associated with nifas, but five of these are actually specific to hayd: the completion of the waiting period ('iddah), istibra (verification of pregnancy), the attainment of puberty, the separation between two divorces of bid'ah, and not breaking the continuity of a fast. The author corrects earlier sources that claimed only twelve rulings exist for both, with eight shared and four exclusive to hayd. The commentator emphasizes that all 22 rulings apply to nifas except those five. The list includes prohibitions, obligations, and legal effects. For example, during nifas, a woman cannot perform Tawaf, intercourse is prohibited, divorce is prohibited, and she attains puberty when she experiences it. The ruling on ghusl requires the cessation of bleeding. The mention of 'اختصاص' (exclusivity) is important for distinguishing between hayd and nifas in legal practice.

Key Terms
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding after childbirth, with rulings similar to hayd.
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstrual blood from a woman of menstruating age, with specific restrictions.
الاستبراء (al-istibra): Verification that a woman is not pregnant, often by waiting for one menstrual period.
العدة (al-'iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death before remarriage.
الطلاق البدعة (al-talaq al-bid'ah): Innovated divorce that is sinful but legally effective, e.g., divorce during menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Only five rulings are exclusive to hayd: ‘iddah, istibra, puberty, divorce separation, and continuation of fast; all other rulings of nifas are shared with hayd.

Classification of Hayd and Nifas Rulings by Timing
Classification of hayd/nifas rulings by timing trigger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the rulings of hayd and nifas into three timing categories: at blood appearance, at beginning of minimum duration, and at cessation.

Core Idea
The rulings of hayd and nifas are not all triggered at once; they depend on blood appearance, minimum duration, or cessation.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author classifies the various rulings of hayd (menstruation) and nifas (postpartum bleeding) based on the moment they become effective. This classification helps us understand why some rulings apply immediately upon seeing blood, others only after the minimum duration of hayd has begun, and others only after bleeding stops. Let's parse this classification step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three timing categories
The passage explicitly names three categories: (1) rulings tied to the appearance of blood (buruz al-dam), (2) rulings tied to the minimum duration of hayd (nisab) but based on its beginning, and (3) rulings tied to its cessation (inqida').
Note that the first category is defined by the chosen madhhab (al-madhhab al-mukhtar) as appearance, but Imam Muhammad uses sensation (ihsas).
2
List the rulings in each category
The passage gives examples: second category includes the ruling of puberty (bulugh) and obligation of ghusl; third category includes completion of 'iddah and istibra; all other rulings (like prohibition of prayer, fasting, etc.) fall into the first category.
The specific rulings for the first category are not enumerated here but are assumed from previous context.
3
Understand the legal significance of the classification
This classification determines when each ruling becomes legally effective. For example, the obligation of ghusl only arises after the minimum duration of hayd has passed, not immediately at the start of bleeding.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that certain obligations are linked to the continuity of the bleeding, not just its onset.
4
Note the intra-madhhab difference
Imam Muhammad holds that the first category (triggering most rulings) begins with the internal sensation of blood, while the majority (including Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) consider external appearance as the trigger. The chosen madhhab position is appearance.
This difference affects when a woman is considered in hayd for rulings like abandoning prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذه الأحكام التي ذكرناها منها ما يتعلق ببروز الدم على المذهب المختار وعند محمد بالاحساس ومنها ما يتعلق بنصاب الحيض لكن يستند إلى ابتدائه، ومنها ما يتعلق بانقضائه، فالثاني هو الحكم ببلوغها ووجوب الغسل، والثالث هو انقضاء العدة والاستبراء وبقية الأحكام متعلقة بالقسم الأول.
Then these rulings that we mentioned: from them are those related to the appearance of blood according to the chosen madhhab, and according to Muhammad by sensation; from them are those related to the minimum period of hayd but are based on its beginning; and from them are those related to its cessation. So the second is the ruling of her attaining puberty and obligation of ghusl; the third is completion of waiting period and istibra; the rest of the rulings are related to the first section.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage classifies the previously enumerated rulings into three categories based on timing. The first category pertains to the moment blood appears (or is sensed, according to Imam Muhammad). The second category is tied to the minimum duration of hayd (one full day and night in the Hanafi school) but relies on the beginning of the flow; this includes the ruling that she attains puberty and that ghusl becomes obligatory after cessation. The third category is related to the end of hayd, covering the completion of the waiting period ('iddah) and istibra. All other rulings (like prohibition of prayer, fasting, etc.) fall into the first category. This classification helps in understanding when each ruling becomes applicable and its legal consequence.

Key Terms
بروز الدم (buruz al-dam): Physical appearance of blood externally.
الاحساس (al-ihsas): The sensation of blood moving internally but before external appearance.
نصاب الحيض (nisab al-hayd): The minimum duration of hayd, which is 3 days in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The rulings of hayd are classified by their temporal trigger: appearance (first), beginning of nisab (second), or cessation (third).

Dispute on Obligation for Menstruating Woman
Presents the usul dispute on whether rulings are established for the menstruating woman despite the prohibition of performance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether rulings are established for a menstruating woman and evaluate the practical consequence of each view on intention.

Core Idea
Does obligation exist when performance is impossible? Al-Bazdawi says no, Abu Zaid says yes but it falls.

The question of whether a menstruating woman is obligated to pray or fast even though she cannot perform them may seem abstract, but it has direct implications for the intention (niyya) when making up missed acts. The Hanafi school witnessed a refined internal dispute among its usuliyyun, with the later authority of al-Bazdawi superseding the earlier view of Abu Zaid al-Dabusi. This lesson walks through the arguments and the crucial distinction between 'fall' (suqut) and 'negation' (intifa').

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the earlier view (Abu Zaid al-Dabusi: obligation is established but falls due to hardship) and the later view (al-Bazdawi: no obligation ever existed).
Both are Hanafi positions; the dispute is within the school.
2
Examine the proofs for each
For Abu Zaid: human has ahliyya wujub; for al-Bazdawi: if obligation existed, performance would count, but it does not by consensus (analogy with child).
Al-Bazdawi uses a qiyas with a child whose acts are not considered fulfillment of obligation.
3
Reconcile apparent contradictions
Al-Quduri's statement that rulings are established is interpreted by al-Dabusi, but later scholars reinterpret 'suqut' as 'intifa' to align with al-Nawawi's consensus.
The key is whether 'suqut' means 'fall' or 'negation'.
4
Identify the practical consequence
If obligation exists (Abu Zaid), she intends qada' when making up; if it never existed (al-Bazdawi), she intends 'ada'.
This affects the validity of the intention.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Bazdawi's view is the relied-upon position in the later school.
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq affirms this.

That the obligation or not? Apparently the dispute is verbal, except that there should be no difference in the fall of obligation if it occurs after the entry of time. End. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: This issue, the usuliyyun differed in it: whether the rulings are established for the child, the insane, and the menstruating woman or not? Abu Zaid al-Dabusi chose that they are established and the fall is due to the excuse of hardship. He said: because the human is qualified for the imposition of rights upon him. And the statement of the Shaykh meaning al-Quduri is based on this. And al-Bazdawi said: We were on this for a while, then we left it and said there is no obligation. End. And the apparent meaning of the speech of al-Nahr is to keep the speech of al-Quduri on what is understood from it, as carried by al-Siraj and others, and that with this it does not contradict the consensus transmitted by al-Nawawi because the fall is agreed upon. But it is not hidden that he said: the fall of something is a branch of its existence. So there is no alternative but to interpret the fall in al-Nawawi's expression as negation, as the author did to make the transmission of consensus valid; otherwise, its apparent meaning is definitely that, like the statement of al-Dabusi. So his saying: 'Indeed the fall is a predetermined agreed upon...' if not interpreted as negation, it is prevented. So it appears that the meaning of fall is negation in the expressions of al-Quduri and al-Nawawi, and there is no need to carry al-Quduri's expression on the opinion of Abu Zaid, for it is an opinion rejected by the investigators because it contains a deficiency in the imposition of the Shari'ah of benefit in the world, which is the realization of the meaning of tribulation, and in the Hereafter and in reward. And because if the child had it established upon him and then fell due to hardship, then if he performs it should be considered as performing an obligation, like a traveler who fasts Ramadan while traveling. But since the performer is not considered as performing the obligation by agreement, it indicates the negation of obligation entirely. And his saying that the dispute is clearly verbal followed al-Subki in it, but he said it regarding fasting. He said: because leaving it in the state of excuse is permissible by agreement, and making it up after the removal of the excuse is obligatory by agreement. End. And some investigators said: But it is not like that; rather, the benefit of the dispute between them is, as in al-Dhakha'ir, regarding when we say that one must specify intention for performance or makeup. If we say it is obligatory upon her, she intends makeup; otherwise, she intends performance, then it is time...
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents an important usul al-fiqh dispute within the Hanafi school regarding whether the rulings of worship (like prayer and fasting) are actually obligatory upon a menstruating woman (as well as a child and an insane person) or not. The issue stems from the fact that she is prohibited from performing these acts during menstruation, so can it be said that she is obligated to do them? Abu Zaid al-Dabusi held that she is indeed obligated, but the obligation falls due to hardship. Al-Bazdawi initially agreed but later retracted, asserting no obligation exists. The author discusses the apparent contradiction between al-Quduri and al-Nawawi, noting that al-Quduri's statement that rulings are established for her (which al-Dabusi uses) is interpreted by al-Bazdawi's final view. The consensus reported by al-Nawawi that rulings are not required is reconciled by interpreting 'squat' (fall) as 'negation' (intifa) rather than 'fall from obligation.' The practical consequence is in the intention: if she intends to make up a missed prayer, the dispute affects whether she intends 'ada' (performance) or qada' (makeup). The author favors the view of non-obligation, as it avoids the logical problem that if she performed the act despite prohibition, it would not count as fulfilling an obligation.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): The quality of being obligatory; the dispute is whether the rulings are actually obligatory on the menstruating woman.
السقوط (al-suqut): The fall of obligation due to an excuse; here it is distinguished from negation (intifa').
الانتفاء (al-intifa'): Negation; the obligation never existed in the first place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Obligation is established but falls
B) Obligation never existed
C) She is required to perform rituals in secret
D) She must make up all acts immediately

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi position is that no obligation exists for a menstruating woman; the term 'suqut' in early texts is best interpreted as 'intifa' (negation).

Ruling on Qada' of Fasts and Prayers for the Menstruating Woman
Ruling with proof: qada' of fasts wajib, prayers not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a menstruating woman must make up missed fasts but not prayers, and explain the proof from hadith and consensus.

Core Idea
She makes up fasts, not prayers: the command and consensus are clear.

A common question in women's fiqh is whether a menstruating woman must make up missed prayers and fasts. The Hanafi school, based on a well-known hadith and consensus, gives a clear answer: she must make up fasts but not prayers. This lesson unpacks the textual proof, the wisdom behind the ruling, and addresses a possible objection regarding the obligation of qada' when performance was not required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qada' (making up) of missed fasts and prayers. The ruling: making up fasts is wajib; making up prayers is not required.
Note the distinction: the ruling is the same for menstruation and postpartum bleeding.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The main proof is the hadith of Mu'adha questioning Aisha (radiAllahu anha), recorded in the six canonical books. Aisha states that the wives of the Prophet were commanded to make up fasts but not prayers.
The hadith is ahad but supported by consensus (ijma).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit phrase 'we were ordered to make up the fast and were not ordered to make up the prayer' shows a command (amr) for fasts and absence for prayers. In Hanafi usul, amr indicates obligation unless diverted.
The absence of command does not automatically prohibit, but here it is combined with consensus.
4
Understand the wisdom and the objection
The text mentions the story of Hawwa to illustrate wisdom: making up prayers would cause hardship due to daily repetition, while fasting is only one month. The objection is: if she is not addressed for performance, how can she be addressed for qada'? Two answers are given: either a new command, or the cause (being alive during Ramadan) suffices.
The wisdom story is not a proof but an illustration.
5
Confirm the consensus and the final ruling
The text states 'upon this is consensus' (ijma). The ruling is definitive: qada' of fasts wajib, qada' of prayers not required. Making up prayers is considered 'khilaf al-awla' (contrary to the preferred).
The mufta bihi is clear and undisputed within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فتقضيه (دونها أي فتقضي الصوم لزوماً دون الصلاة لما في الكتب الستة عن معاذة قالت : سألت عائشة فقلت : ما بال الحائض تقضي الصوم ولا تقضي الصلاة؟ فقالت : أحرورية أنت ! قلت: لست بحرورية ولكني اسأل قالت كان يصيبنا ذلك فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة وعليه انعقد الاجماع، ولأن في قضاء الصلاة حرجاً بتكررها في كل يوم وتكرر الحيض في كل شهر بخلاف الصوم حيث يجب في السنة شهراً واحداً والمرأة لا تحيض عادة في الشهر إلا مرة فلا حرج وإنما وجب عليها قضاء الصوم وإن نفست رمضان كله لأن وجوده في رمضان كله نادر فلا يعتبر وذكر في آخر الفتاوى الظهيرية أن حكمته أن حواء لما رأت الدم أول مرة سألت آدم فقال : لا أعلم. فأوحي إليه أن تترك الصلاة فلما طهرت سألته فقال : لا أعلم فأوحي إليه أن لا قضاء عليها، ثم رأته في وقت الصوم فسألته فأمرها بترك الصوم وعدم قضائه قياساً على الصلاة فأمرها الله تعالى بقضاء الصوم من قبل أن آدم أمرها بذلك من غير أمر الله تعالى. وفي معراج الدراية : إن قضائه ترك حوّاء السؤال له وقياسها الصوم على الصلاة فجوزيت بقضائه بسبب ترك السؤال. فإن قيل : إنها غير مخاطبة بالصوم حال حيضها لحرمته عليها فكيف يجب عليها القضاء ولم يجب عليها الأداء؟ قلنا: أما من قال من مشايخنا وغيرهم بأن القضاء يجب بأمر جديد فلا إشكال، وأما على قول الجمهور من مشايخنا أن القضاء يجب بما يجب به الأداء فانعقاد السبب يكفي لوجوب القضاء وإن لم تخاطب بالأداء. وهل يكره لها قضاء الصلاة؟ لم أره صريحاً، وينبغي أن يكون خلاف الأولى كما لا يخفى والحرورية فرقة من الخوارج منسوبة إلى حروراء قرية بالكوفة كان بها أول تحكمهم واجتماعهم، والمراد أنها في التعمق في سؤالها كأنها خارجية لأنهم تعمقوا في أمر الدين حتى خرجوا. كذا في المغرب.
His saying: 'She makes it up without it' i.e., she makes up the fast necessarily without the prayer, because of what is in the six books from Mu'adha: she said: I asked Aisha and said: Why does the menstruating woman make up the fast and not make up the prayer? She said: Are you a Harurite? I said: I am not a Harurite, but I am asking. She said: That used to happen to us, so we were ordered to make up the fast and were not ordered to make up the prayer. And upon this is consensus. And because in making up the prayer there is hardship due to its recurrence every day and the recurrence of menstruation every month, contrary to fasting, which occurs in the year only one month, and a woman usually menstruates only once a month, so there is no hardship. And it is only obligatory upon her to make up the fast even if she experienced postpartum bleeding during the entire Ramadan, because its occurrence during the entire Ramadan is rare, so it is not considered. And it is mentioned at the end of al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya that its wisdom is that when Hawwa saw blood the first time, she asked Adam; he said: I do not know. So it was revealed to him that she should leave prayer. When she became pure, she asked him; he said: I do not know. So it was revealed to him that there is no make-up upon her. Then she saw it during the time of fast and asked him; he ordered her to leave fast and not make it up by analogy to prayer. So Allah Almighty ordered her to make up the fast before Adam ordered her to do so without Allah's command. And in Mi'raj al-Diraya: Her making up is because Hawwa left questioning him and her analogy of fast to prayer, so she was recompensed by making up due to leaving the question. If it is said: She is not addressed to fast during her menstruation because it is prohibited for her, so how is make-up obligatory upon her while performance was not obligatory? We say: As for those of our teachers and others who say that make-up is obligatory by a new command, there is no problem. As for the majority of our teachers that make-up is obligatory by that which makes performance obligatory, the occurrence of the cause suffices for the obligation of make-up even if she is not addressed for performance. And is it disliked for her to make up the prayer? I have not seen it explicitly, and it should be that it is contrary to the first preference as is not hidden. And the Harurites are a sect of the Khawarij, attributed to Harura, a village in Kufa, where their first ruling and gathering occurred. The meaning is that she is in her deep inquiry as if she is a Kharijite because they exaggerated in religious matters until they left the mainstream. So in al-Maghrib.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that a menstruating woman must make up missed fasts (qada' al-sawm) but not missed prayers (qada' al-salah). The proof is a hadith from Aisha (radiAllahu anha) narrated by Mu'adha, which is in the six canonical books. Aisha responded sharply—'Are you a Harurite?'—meaning a Kharijite who goes to extremes in questioning. Then she explained that the practice of the Prophet's wives was that they were commanded to make up fasts but not prayers, and this became consensus. The wisdom is that making up prayers involves hardship because prayers are daily and menstruation occurs monthly, while fasting is only one month a year. Even if a woman has postpartum bleeding throughout Ramadan, she must still make up the fast, though that is rare. The story of Hawwa (Eve) is mentioned as a wisdom: after the first menstruation, she was told not to pray and also not to make it up; but when she compared fasting to prayer, she was ordered to make up fasts. The passage also addresses an objection: if she is not obligated to perform the fast (since it is prohibited during menstruation), how can she be obligated to make it up? The answer is that either the command to make up is a separate command (amr jadid), or the cause of obligation (being alive during Ramadan) suffices for qada' even if performance was not required. The commentator notes that making up missed prayers is not explicitly disliked but is 'khilaf al-awla' (contrary to the preferred). The term 'Harurite' is explained as a reference to the Kharijites.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up a missed ritual act after its prescribed time.
الحرورية (al-haruriyya): A sect of Kharijites who went to extremes in questioning; Aisha used it as a rebuke for excessive questioning.
خلاف الأولى (khilaf al-awla): Something that is not prohibited but contrary to what is preferable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because she disliked women asking questions
D) To indicate she was a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
The menstruating woman must make up missed fasts but not prayers, based on definitive hadith and consensus.

Prohibition of Entering the Mosque for Menstruating Woman
States the prohibition of entering the mosque for a menstruating woman and discusses whether other places have the same ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menstruating woman entering the mosque and distinguish between places that have the ruling of a mosque and those that do not.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman is prohibited from entering a masjid proper, but may enter other prayer areas like the Eid musalla.

A common fiqh question is whether a menstruating woman may enter a mosque. The Hanafi school draws a clear distinction between the masjid proper and other places of worship. This lesson examines the source text from al-Bahr al-Raiq, which states the prohibition and discusses the scope of what counts as a mosque. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon and the legal classification (hukm level). Here, the act is entering the mosque for a menstruating woman, and it is haram.
The prohibition also applies to someone in janaba.
2
Locate the dalil
The ruling is based on Quran (4:43) and Sunnah, though not explicitly quoted in this passage. The prohibition is well-established.
Zahir al-Riwayah texts affirm this.
3
Define the scope: what is a masjid?
The passage excludes other places like the Eid musalla, funeral prayer ground, madrasa, and ribat. Only a place legally designated as a masjid (with laws of i'tikaf, etc.) carries the prohibition.
There is ikhtilaf on whether some of these places have mosque status at certain times.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Khulasa states that musalla is not a mosque; al-Quniyya says a madrasa can be a mosque if people are allowed to pray in it; Fatawa Qadikhan gives musalla mosque ruling only for prayer time validity, not for entry prohibition.
The mufta bihi is that musalla is not a mosque for entry prohibition.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a menstruating woman may attend Eid prayer at the musalla, but she may not enter a masjid proper.
Also, iqtida is valid in musalla at prayer time even if rows are disconnected, according to Fatawa Qadikhan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ودخول (مسجد) أي يمنع الحيض دخول المسجد وكذا الجنابة. وخرج بالمسجد غيره كمصلى العيد والجنائز والمدرسة والرباط فلا يمنعان من دخولها ولهذا قال في الخلاصة : المتخذ لصلاة الجنازة والعيد الأصح أنه ليس له حكم المسجد. واختار في القنية من كتاب الوقف أن المدرسة إذا كان لا يمنع أهلها الناس من الصلاة في مسجدها فهي مسجد. وفي فتاوی قاضیخان الجبانة ومصلى الجنازة لهما حكم المسجد عند أداء الصلاة حتى يصح الاقتداء وإن لم تكن الصفوف متصلة وليس لهما حكم المسجد في حق المرور، وحرمة الصلاة : توجه الخطاب والله سبحانه تعالى أعلم. نعم يبقى في كلام المصنف إيهام أن الصوم حكمه حكم أنه واجب عليها، ولذا قال في النهر : يمنع صلاة أي حلها لتناسب المعطوفات فالأولى ما في القدوري ويحرم عليها الصوم .اهـ. قوله : (وينبغي أن يكون خلاف الأولى) قال في النهر : ويدل
His saying: 'And entering a mosque' means that menstruation prohibits entering the mosque, and likewise janaba. And by 'mosque' is excluded other places like the prayer ground of Eid and funerals, the madrasa, and the ribat; these do not prohibit her entry. For this reason, it is said in al-Khulasa: The place made for funeral and Eid prayer, the correct view is that it does not have the ruling of a mosque. And in al-Quniyya from the book of Waqf, it is chosen that if the people of a madrasa do not prevent people from praying in its mosque, then it is a mosque. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: The graveyard and the funeral prayer place have the ruling of a mosque at the time of prayer such that following (iqtida) is valid even if the rows are not connected, but they do not have the ruling of a mosque regarding passing through. And the sanctity of prayer: The address of the line, and Allah Almighty knows best. Yes, there remains in the author's speech an illusion that fasting has the same ruling that it is obligatory upon her. For this reason, al-Nahr said: 'Menstruation prohibits prayer i.e., its permissibility to suit the conjuncts, so the better is what is in al-Quduri: and it prohibits fasting upon her.' End. His saying: 'And it should be that it is contrary to the first preference' said in al-Nahr: and it indicates...
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of a menstruating woman from entering the mosque, which also applies to someone in janaba (major impurity). The ruling is based on the general prohibition mentioned in the Quran and Sunnah. However, the commentator clarifies that this prohibition is specific to a place that has the full legal status of a masjid (mosque). Other places like the Eid prayer ground (musalla), funeral prayer ground, madrasa (school), and ribat (hostel) are not considered mosques in this regard, so a menstruating woman may enter them. There is a difference of opinion: according to al-Khulasa, the place built for funeral and Eid prayers does not have the ruling of a mosque. In al-Quniyya, a madrasa can be considered a mosque if people are allowed to pray in it. According to Fatawa Qadikhan, the graveyard and funeral prayer place have the ruling of a mosque for the purpose of prayer validity (iqtida) but not for prohibiting entry. The text then notes an illusion in the author's wording that suggests fasting is prohibited like prayer; al-Nahr corrects this by stating the matn should say 'it prohibits fasting' (not prayer). The last part mentions that the ruling of making up prayers being 'khilaf al-awla' (contrary to preference) is supported by al-Nahr.

Key Terms
المسجد (al-masjid): A place dedicated for prayer with the legal status of a mosque; entry prohibited for menstruating woman.
مصلى العيد (musalla al-'id): The open prayer ground for Eid prayers; not considered a mosque for this prohibition.
الاقتداء (al-iqtida'): Following an imam in prayer; valid in funeral prayer place due to its mosque-like status at prayer time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a menstruating woman enter a madrasa (school) where students pray?
A) Yes, unless it is legally a mosque
B) No, it is prohibited
C) Yes, but only for learning
D) No, unless she performs wudu

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of entering the mosque for a menstruating woman is specific to places that hold the legal status of a masjid; other places like musalla and madrasa are generally permissible.
