The Menstruating Woman: Qada of Fast, Not Prayer
Commentary on a matn explaining the qada ruling for hayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada of prayer and fast for a menstruating woman, cite the primary hadith evidence, and explain the rational reasoning and the intra-Hanafi difference on the basis of qada.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman must make up fasts but not prayers; the ruling is explicit in hadith and upheld by ijma.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling that a menstruating woman is required to make up missed fasts but not missed prayers. The commentary unpacks the primary evidence from a well-known hadith of Aisha (ra), adds a rational justification grounded in avoiding hardship, and clarifies the reasoning behind the obligation of qada for fasting even when performance was not required. It also touches on an intra-Hanafi discussion about whether qada is imposed by a new command or by the original command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Note that the passage states: the menstruating woman must make up the fast (wajib) but not the prayer. Understand that this applies to any missed fasts (e.g., Ramadan) and any missed prayers during the period of hayd.
The ruling is specific to hayd; istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding) does not prevent prayer or fasting.
2
Locate the primary proof (dalil)
The commentary cites the hadith of Mu'adhah from Aisha (ra): 'We were commanded to make up the fast but not the prayer.' This is a clear textual evidence from the Sunnah. Also, the author states that consensus (ijma) has been formed on this ruling.
The hadith is recorded in the six major hadith collections (al-kutub al-sittah), indicating its authenticity.
3
Understand the rational justification
The commentary adds a rational reason: making up prayers would cause hardship because prayers are obligatory every day, and menstruation recurs monthly, leading to a large number of missed prayers. In contrast, fasting is only one month a year, and a woman typically menstruates only once a month, so the burden of making up missed fasts is manageable.
This reasoning (qiyas) supports the ruling but the primary basis remains the hadith and ijma.
4
Analyze the objection and its response
The passage raises an objection: how can qada be obligatory if the woman was not commanded to perform the fast during her hayd (since fasting is forbidden during hayd)? The response explains two positions within the Hanafi school: (1) qada is by a new command, or (2) qada is by the original command, where the mere existence of the cause (obligation of Ramadan) suffices for qada even without the address to perform.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is that qada is wajib regardless of the theoretical basis.
5
Note the wisdom story (optional)
The commentary includes a story about Hawwa (Eve) as a wisdom behind the ruling. While not a legal proof, it illustrates that the qada of fast is a divine command, not a human analogy.
This story is not relied upon for legal derivation; it is mentioned for spiritual benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما نقص من ذلك أو زاد استحاضة وما سوى البياض الخالص حيض يمنع صلاة وصوماً فتقضيه دونها ودخول مسجد والطواف وقربان ما تحت الأزار وقراءة القرآن
قوله : (فتقضيه (دونها أي فتقضي الصوم لزوماً دون الصلاة لما في الكتب الستة عن معاذة قالت : سألت عائشة فقلت : ما بال الحائض تقضي الصوم ولا تقضي الصلاة؟ فقالت : أحرورية أنت ! قلت: لست بحرورية ولكني اسأل قالت كان يصيبنا ذلك فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة وعليه انعقد الاجماع، ولأن في قضاء الصلاة حرجاً بتكررها في كل يوم وتكرر الحيض في كل شهر بخلاف الصوم حيث يجب في السنة شهراً واحداً والمرأة لا تحيض عادة في الشهر إلا مرة فلا حرج وإنما وجب عليها قضاء الصوم وإن نفست رمضان كله لأن وجوده في رمضان كله نادر فلا يعتبر وذكر في آخر الفتاوى الظهيرية أن حكمته أن حواء لما رأت الدم أول مرة سألت آدم فقال : لا أعلم. فأوحي إليه أن تترك الصلاة فلما طهرت سألته فقال : لا أعلم فأوحي إليه أن لا قضاء عليها، ثم رأته في وقت الصوم فسألته فأمرها بترك الصوم وعدم قضائه قياساً على الصلاة فأمرها الله تعالى بقضاء الصوم من قبل أن آدم أمرها بذلك من غير أمر الله تعالى. وفي معراج الدراية : إن قضائه ترك حوّاء السؤال له وقياسها الصوم على الصلاة فجوزيت بقضائه بسبب ترك السؤال. فإن قيل : إنها غير مخاطبة بالصوم حال حيضها لحرمته عليها فكيف يجب عليها القضاء ولم يجب عليها الأداء؟ قلنا: أما من قال من مشايخنا وغيرهم بأن القضاء يجب بأمر جديد فلا إشكال، وأما على قول الجمهور من مشايخنا أن القضاء يجب بما يجب به الأداء فانعقاد السبب يكفي لوجوب القضاء وإن لم تخاطب بالأداء. وهل يكره لها قضاء الصلاة؟ لم أره صريحاً، وينبغي أن يكون خلاف الأولى كما لا يخفى والحرورية فرقة من الخوارج منسوبة إلى حروراء قرية بالكوفة كان بها أول تحكمهم واجتماعهم، والمراد أنها في التعمق في سؤالها كأنها خارجية لأنهم تعمقوا في أمر الدين حتى خرجوا. كذا في المغرب.
What is less than that or more is istihadah, and anything other than pure white is hayd. It prevents prayer and fasting, so she makes up the fast but not the prayer, and [it prevents] entering the mosque, circumambulation, approaching what is under the waist-wrapper, and reciting the Qur'an.
His saying: (she makes it up but not it) i.e. she makes up the fast necessarily not the prayer because of what is in the six books from Mu'adhah who said: I asked Aisha, 'Why does a menstruating woman make up the fast and not the prayer?' She said: 'Are you a Haruriyyah?' I said: 'I am not a Haruriyyah but I am asking.' She said: 'That used to happen to us, and we were commanded to make up the fast but not commanded to make up the prayer.' And upon this consensus has been formed. Because in making up the prayer there is hardship due to its repetition every day and the repetition of menstruation every month, unlike the fast which is obligatory only one month in the year, and a woman does not usually menstruate in a month except once, so there is no hardship. And it is obligatory upon her to make up the fast even if she experiences postpartum bleeding the entire Ramadan, because its occurrence throughout Ramadan is rare and not considered. And it was mentioned in the end of Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah that its wisdom is that when Hawwa saw blood for the first time she asked Adam, and he said he did not know. So it was revealed to him that she should leave the prayer. When she became pure she asked him, and he said he did not know, so it was revealed to him that there is no qada upon her. Then she saw it at the time of fasting and asked him, so he commanded her to leave the fast and not to make it up by analogy to the prayer. Then Allah commanded her to make up the fast before Adam commanded her with that from Allah's command. And in Mi'raj al-Dirayah: Her making it up is because Hawwa left off asking him and her analogizing the fast to the prayer, so she was requited with making it up due to leaving off asking. If it is said: She is not addressed with the fast during her menstruation because it is forbidden for her, so how can qada be obligatory upon her while performance was not? We say: As for those of our scholars and others who say that qada is obligatory by a new command, there is no problem. As for the view of the majority of our scholars that qada is obligatory by the same command by which performance is obligatory, then the occurrence of the cause suffices for the obligation of qada even if she was not addressed with performance. Is it disliked for her to make up the prayer? I did not see it explicitly, and it should be contrary to the first preference, as is not hidden. And the Haruriyyah are a sect of the Khawarij attributed to Harura, a village in Kufa, where their first ruling and gathering occurred. The meaning is that she is in her deepening of questioning like a Khariji because they deepened in religious matters until they exited. Thus in al-Mughrib.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, explaining the rulings related to menstruation (hayd). It first defines what is hayd: blood that is not pure white, excluding the minimum (3 days) and maximum (10 days) – anything else is istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding). Hayd prevents prayer and fasting, but the woman must make up the fast, not the prayer. The proof is a hadith from Aisha (ra) reported by Mu'adhah, who asked why the menstruating woman makes up the fast but not the prayer. Aisha responded by relating the practice: they were commanded to make up the fast but not the prayer, and consensus (ijma) has formed on this. The commentary adds rational reasoning: making up the prayer would cause hardship due to its daily repetition, whereas fasting occurs only once a year, and menstruation typically happens once a month, so no hardship. A story about Hawwa is mentioned as wisdom, though primarily the ruling is based on the hadith and ijma. The passage also answers an objection: how can qada be obligatory if the woman was not commanded to perform the fasting during menstruation? The response is that either qada is by a new command, or the cause (the obligation of Ramadan) suffices for qada. The text concludes with an explanation of 'Haruriyyah', a Kharijite sect, emphasizing that the questioner was not a Kharijite but merely asking for knowledge.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood; it is the blood that naturally exits from the womb at certain times, and its minimum is 3 days and maximum 10 days in the Hanafi school. Anything else is istihadah.
استحاضة (istihadah): Non-menstrual bleeding; it does not prevent prayer, fasting, etc.
حرورية (haruriyyah): A sect of the Khawarij who were known for excessive questioning and strictness. Here it is used to rebuke the questioner for asking too many questions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a menstruating woman regarding qada of missed prayers and missed fasts?
A) She must make up both prayers and fasts.
B) She must make up the fasts but not the prayers.
C) She must make up the prayers but not the fasts.
D) She does not need to make up either.

Key Takeaway
A menstruating woman must make up missed fasts (wajib) but is not required to make up missed prayers, a ruling firmly established by hadith, consensus, and rational reasoning.

Prohibition of Entering the Mosque for Menstruating Woman (Hanafi vs Shafi'i)
Dispute on crossing the mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menstruating woman entering a mosque, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument from Quran 4:43, and apply the Hanafi response to resolve the dispute.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman cannot enter a mosque even for crossing, except for necessity—the Shafi'i 'passing through' exception is rejected by Hanafi as misinterpreting the Quranic verse.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the prohibition of a menstruating woman (and junub) from entering the mosque. The Hanafi position is absolute prohibition, while Imam al-Shafi'i permits crossing through based on the Quranic exception 'illa abiri sabil.' We will examine the proof texts and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is entering the mosque for a menstruating woman and junub. The Hanafi ruling is haram (prohibition).
The prohibition applies to entering even for crossing, except in cases of necessity.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of Aisha: 'I do not make the mosque lawful for a menstruating woman or junub.'
This hadith is explicit and absolute in its prohibition.
3
Identify the Shafi'i dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i cites Quran 4:43: 'wa la junuban illa abiri sabil' (except as passers-by), interpreting 'prayer' as 'place of prayer' to permit crossing.
Shafi'i argues the exception allows crossing the mosque for junub and, by analogy, menstruating women.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafi scholars respond that the verse's exception refers to the traveler who uses tayammum, not to crossing the mosque. The hadith takes precedence as a specific prohibition.
The Hanafi interpretation is that 'illa abiri sabil' means 'except when traveling (and you have no water)' - the exception is for tayammum, not for entering the mosque.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi forbids even brief entry; Shafi'i allows crossing without staying. This affects real-life mosque access policies.
Hanafi mufta bihi is prohibition except for necessity (e.g., door of house opens into mosque).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ليس له حكم المسجد. واختار في القنية من كتاب الوقف أن المدرسة إذا كان لا يمنع أهلها الناس من الصلاة في مسجدها فهي مسجد. وفي فتاوی قاضیخان الجبانة ومصلى الجنازة لهما حكم المسجد عند أداء الصلاة حتى يصح الاقتداء وإن لم تكن الصفوف متصلة وليس لهما حكم المسجد في حق المرور، وحرمة الصلاة : توجه الخطاب والله سبحانه تعالى أعلم. نعم يبقى في كلام المصنف إيهام أن الصوم حكمه حكم أنه واجب عليها، ولذا قال في عبور فلكم أن تقربوها بغير اغتسال وبالتيمم يصدق أنه بغير اغتسال. نعم مقتضى ظاهر الاستثناء ورد عليه قولهم لو غسل رأسه بدل المسح كره قوله : (وأما ما في شرح الزاهدي الخ) قيل : ينبغي تقييده بما إذا لم تجعل أما إذا لم يكن شيء من هذين الأمرين كذلك، به أن البقعة الخارجة عن جدران المسجد ليست منه ليكون ما في هوائها له حكم المسجد كما هو فرض
His saying: (and entering the mosque) i.e., hayd prevents entering the mosque, and likewise janabah. Excluded from 'mosque' are other places like the Eid prayer place, funeral prayer place, school, and ribat – these do not prevent from entering. For this reason, he said in al-Khulasa: The place made for funeral and Eid prayer, the correct view is that it does not have the ruling of a mosque. And he chose in al-Qunyah from the book of waqf that a school, if its inhabitants do not prevent people from praying in its mosque, then it is a mosque. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: The cemetery and funeral prayer place have the ruling of a mosque at the time of performing prayer such that following the imam is valid even if rows are not connected, but they do not have the ruling of a mosque regarding passing through, and the prohibition of prayer: the address of the command and Allah, exalted, knows best. Yes, there remains in the author's speech an implication that the fast has the ruling that it is obligatory upon her, so he said in al-Nahr: It prevents prayer, i.e., its validity, to harmonize the conjunctions. The better is what is in al-Quduri: 'and fasting is forbidden for her.' End. His saying: 'and it should be contrary to the first preference' – he said in al-Nahr: and it indicates from her, may Allah be pleased with her, she said: Entry for the junub, and the courtyard of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque with respect to the permissibility of following the imam even if rows are not connected and the mosque is not full. End. As for the permissibility of the menstruating woman entering, the courtyard does not have the ruling of a mosque in it. And as for what is in Sharh al-Zahidi that the roof of the mosque and its porch (zulla) are in its ruling, it is not unrestricted but restricted in the porch that it is in its ruling with respect to permissibility of following, not with respect to the prohibition of entry for the junub and menstruating woman, as is not hidden. And the author of al-Durar wa al-Ghurar restricted the prohibition of their entering the mosque to when there is no necessity, and he said: It is forbidden for the junub to enter the mosque even for passing except for necessity, like if the door of his house opens to the mosque. End. This is good although it contradicts the unrestricted statements of the scholars, and it should be restricted to when he cannot change his door to other than the mosque and he is not able to live elsewhere, as is not hidden; otherwise the necessity is not realized. This is supported by what was narrated from Uflat from Jasrah bint Dajaja from Aisha: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) came and the fronts of his companions' houses opened into the mosque. He said: 'Turn these houses away from the mosque,' then he entered and the people did nothing, hoping that a dispensation would be revealed concerning them. So he went out to them and said: 'Turn these houses away from the mosque, for I do not make the mosque lawful for a menstruating woman or for a junub.' Narrated by Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and al-Bukhari in his Tarikh al-Kabir. Al-Khattabi reported its weakness due to the unknown status of Uflat and Dajaja (with a kasra on the dal), unlike the singular of chickens. And it is, with its unrestricted form, a proof against al-Shafi'i in his permitting entry for the purpose of crossing, and against Abu al-Yusr among our companions in his permitting entry for other than prayer, as was transmitted from him in Khazanat al-Fatawa. Al-Shafi'i argued with the verse: 'O you who believe, do not approach prayer while you are intoxicated until you know what you are saying, nor while you are in a state of janabah except as passers-by' (al-Nisa 4:43), building on his interpretation that 'prayer' means 'place of prayer' metaphorically, so the prohibition is against approaching the place of prayer for the junub, not in the state of crossing. Or building on his use of the word 'prayer' in both its literal and metaphorical sense, so the prohibited thing is approaching the prayer and its place. There is no doubt that this is a deviation from the apparent meaning, and there is no reason for it except the illusion of the necessity of permitting prayer in a state of janabah when one is a passerby, because it is excepted from the prohibition that is conditioned by washing. This illusion is not necessary because the ruling must be that the intended meaning is its permissibility in the state of passing, i.e., a traveler with tayammum, because the meaning of the construction 'do not approach it in janabah until you wash except in the state of passing' is that you may approach it without washing, and with tayammum it is true that it is without washing. Indeed, the apparent meaning of the exception is refuted by their statement: if he washes his head instead of wiping it, it is disliked. His saying: (As for what is in Sharh al-Zahidi, etc.) It is said: It should be restricted to when the porch was not made a part of the mosque from the beginning or not annexed to it as such, as Ibn Amir Haj pointed out where he said: As for the porch of the door being in its ruling with respect to this ruling we are discussing, it is only established if it was made a part of the mosque from the beginning or annexed. If neither of these two things exists, then the area outside the walls of the mosque is not from it for what is in its air to have the ruling of the mosque, as is the default.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn's statement that hayd prevents entering the mosque. It clarifies that the prohibition applies to the mosque specifically, not to other prayer places like Eid musalla, funeral prayer area, school, or ribat unless they legally constitute a mosque. The Hanafi position is that a menstruating woman (and a junub) cannot enter a mosque, even for the purpose of passing through, except in cases of necessity (e.g., if her house door opens into the mosque and she cannot change it). The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i allows crossing through the mosque, citing the verse 'except as passers-by' in Surah al-Nisa, interpreting 'prayer' as 'place of prayer' metaphorically. The Hanafi response is that this is a deviation from the apparent meaning, and the exemption is not for crossing the mosque but for the traveler who uses tayammum. The Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Aisha (ra) that the Prophet (saw) said he does not make the mosque lawful for a menstruating woman or junub, thus the prohibition is absolute except for necessity. The commentary also discusses the status of the mosque's courtyard (fina') and porch (zulla), restricting their ruling to matters of following the imam, not the prohibition of entry.

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): The building or area designated for the five daily prayers, with walls and a roof; its ruling extends to the attached courtyard (فناء) and possibly porch (ظلة) but only regarding following the imam, not the prohibition of entry for the menstruating woman.
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl; the same prohibition of entering the mosque applies as for the menstruating woman.
عابر سبيل (abir sabīl): One who is passing through; in the Shafi'i interpretation, a menstruating woman or junub may cross a mosque without staying. Hanafi rejects this as the correct meaning of the Quranic exception is a traveler with tayammum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied upon) Hanafi ruling regarding a menstruating woman entering a mosque for passing through?
A) Permitted because she is just passing through.
B) Prohibited except in cases of necessity.
C) Permitted only if she recites Quran.
D) Permitted if she is in a state of wudu.

Key Takeaway
A menstruating woman (and junub) is prohibited from entering a mosque even for crossing, except for genuine necessity, in contrast to the Shafi'i view which permits crossing based on the Quranic exception.

Rulings on Junub and Menstruating Woman Entering the Mosque
Inter-madhhab dispute on junub crossing mosque with hadith and Quranic tafsir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a junub entering the mosque for crossing, distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, and apply the necessity exception correctly.

Core Idea
A junub may not enter the mosque even to cross, except for dire necessity — the Quran's 'passers-by' means travelers, not a loophole.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical issue of ritual purity and sacred space: may a person in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah) or a menstruating woman enter the mosque? The Hanafi school holds that this is strictly prohibited, even just to pass through, unless a genuine necessity compels it. The discussion not only clarifies the ruling but also defends it against the Shafi'i interpretation of the Quranic verse that seems to allow crossing. As you work through this lesson, you will learn the key evidence, the precise conditions of the exception, and how to navigate this inter-madhhab disagreement with intellectual rigour.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act being ruled on? (Entering the mosque for a junub or menstruating woman.) Determine the Hanafi hukm level (haram) and the key exception (necessity).
The act includes standing on the mosque's fina' (courtyard) and under its awning.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Examine the hadith from Aisha (RA) and the incident of the Prophet (SAW) ordering houses away from the mosque. Understand why this hadith is the cornerstone of the Hanafi position despite its chain being considered weak by some.
The hadith states: 'I do not make the mosque permissible for a menstruating woman or a junub.'
3
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-argument
Understand the Shafi'i interpretation of Quran 4:43 'except as passers-by (illa 'abiri sabil).' How do they use this to permit crossing?
Shafi'i takes 'abiri sabil' literally as anyone crossing through.
4
Trace the Hanafi response
Learn why the Hanafi school reinterprets 'abiri sabil' as travelers (musafirun) who may perform tayammum. See how they reconcile the verse with the hadith.
The response also addresses the issue of tayammum not removing janabah.
5
Apply the necessity exception
Apply the condition: when is entry permissible? The text gives the example of a house door opening directly into the mosque with no alternative. Understand that this necessity must be genuine and not optional.
The exception is limited; it does not permit lingering or unnecessary movement.

Book of Purification - Chapter of Menstruation. ... [Full translation omitted for brevity but would be literal]
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the rulings regarding junub (state of major impurity) and menstruating women entering the mosque. The commentary opens with the rule that the courtyard of the mosque (fina') has the same ruling as the mosque itself for the permissibility of following the imam in prayer, even if rows are not connected. However, this does not apply to the permissibility of a menstruating woman entering. The awning of the door is also limited: it has the mosque's ruling only for following the imam, not for prohibition of entry for junub or menstruating women. The author then quotes from al-Durar wa al-Ghurar that it is haram for a junub to enter the mosque even for crossing, except for necessity, e.g., if his house door opens into the mosque. A hadith is cited from Aisha (RA) where the Prophet (SAW) ordered houses' doors away from the mosque, stating 'I do not make the mosque permissible for a menstruating woman or a junub.' The chain is considered weak but supported by other narrations. The main inter-madhhab dispute arises with Imam al-Shafi'i, who allows crossing (عبور) for a junub based on the Quranic verse [4:43] 'except as passers-by.' The Hanafi response clarifies that 'عابري سبيل' refers to travelers, not merely those crossing the mosque, and that the exception is for those who can perform tayammum. The Hanafi position is further supported by the hadith of Ali (RA) that only he and the Prophet were allowed to be in a state of janabah in the mosque. The discussion also addresses the issue of tayammum not raising the state of janabah but being a substitute for necessity.

Key Terms
فناء المسجد (fina' al-masjid): The courtyard or open area attached to the mosque.
ظلة الباب (zullat al-bab): The awning or canopy over the door of the mosque.
عابري سبيل (abiru sabil): Literally 'passers-by'; in the Quranic context, interpreted by Hanafis as travelers (musafirun).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what is the ruling on a menstruating woman entering the mosque courtyard (fina') for a necessity?
A) Permitted unconditionally
B) Haram even with necessity
C) Permitted only if her house door opens into the courtyard and cannot be changed
D) Makruh not haram

Key Takeaway
A junub or menstruating woman may not enter the mosque even for crossing unless genuine necessity (e.g., no alternative entrance) exists; the Quranic 'passers-by' is interpreted as travelers with no water.

Taharah — Purification

Tayammum for the Resident Junub and the Meaning of 'Abir al-Sabil'
Defending Hanafi permissibility of tayammum for resident junub via Quranic interpretation and istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on tayammum for a resident junub and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i argument using the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Tayammum for the resident junub is permissible because the need for purification overrides the literal exemption of travelers.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defends the Hanafi ruling that a resident in a state of major impurity (junub) may perform tayammum when water is unavailable. The apparent meaning of Quran 4:43 exempts only travelers, but Abu Hanifa argues that the underlying cause—inability to find water—applies equally to residents. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi usul principles like istihsan operate in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by clearly articulating that the Hanafi school permits tayammum for a resident junub when water is absent.
This is the mufta bihi position within the school.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Recognize that the Shafi'i school (and a literal reading) restricts tayammum to travelers based on the explicit exception in Quran 4:43.
The verse says 'except travelers on a road'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's response: the prohibition is tied to ability to use water; inability triggers the same need as for the sick, and istihsan allows extending the exception.
The Hanafi response uses qiyas to the sick and istihsan to override the literal meaning.
4
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is that a resident junub who cannot find water may pray with tayammum according to Hanafis, while Shafi'is would require waiting for water or not praying.
This showcases how a difference in usul leads to a different daily practice.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Why does the Hanafi school rely on this position? Because the literal text is zanni al-dalala and istihsan based on need is considered stronger.
The authority of Abu Hanifa and the consensus on the sick person's case support this interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة - باب الحيض المقيم إطلاق القربان حال العبور لكن يثبت اشتراط التيمم فيه بدليل آخر وليس هذا ببدع، فظهر بهذا أن المراد بعابري السبيل المسافرون كما هو منقول عن أهل التفسير. وعلى هذا فالآية دليلهما على منع التيمم للجنب المقيم في المصر ظاهراً فإنه استثنى من المنع المسافرين فكان داخلاً في المنع، وجوابه من قبل أبي حنيفة أنه القدرة على الماء في المصر من المنع في الآية كما أنها مطلقة في المريض وقد أجمعوا على تخصيص حالة القدرة يتيمم المريض القادر على استعمال الماء وأجماعهم إنما كان للعلم بأن شرعيته للحاجة إلى الطهارة عند العجز عن الماء، فإذا تحقق في المصر جاز، وإذا لم يتحقق في المريض لا يجوز . فإن قيل في الآية دليل حينئذ على أن التيمم لا يرفع الحدث وأنتم تأبونه قلنا : قد ذكرنا أن محصلها لا تقربوها جنباً حتى تغتسلوا إلا عابري سبيل فاقربوها بلا اغتسال بالتيمم لأن المعنى فاقربوها جنباً بلا اغتسال بالتيمم، فالرفع وعدمه مسكوت عنه، ثم استفيد كونه رافعاً من خارج على ما قدمناه في باب التيمم.
The Book of Purification - Chapter on Menstruation: The resident [is mentioned] with the absolute [permission] of approaching at the time of passing, but the condition of tayammum is established by another evidence, and this is not unprecedented. So it appears that by 'travelers on a journey' are meant the travelers, as transmitted from the commentators of the Quran. And accordingly, the verse is a proof for them [the opponents] on the prohibition of tayammum for the resident junub in the city apparently, for it exempts the travelers from the prohibition, so the resident falls under the prohibition. The response from Abu Hanifa is that the ability to [find] water in the city is from the prohibition in the verse, just as it is absolute regarding the sick. And they have agreed on specifying the case of inability: the sick person who can use water does tayammum, and their consensus is only due to the knowledge that its legislation is for the need of purification when unable to get water. So if [inability] is realized in the city, it is permissible; if not realized, it is not permissible for the sick. If it is said: In the verse there is evidence that tayammum does not remove the state of ritual impurity (hadath), and you [Hanafis] reject that, we say: We have mentioned that its purport is: 'Do not approach them while you are junub until you wash, except travelers on a journey, so approach them without washing by tayammum' because the meaning is 'approach them while junub without washing by tayammum.' So the removal or non-removal is silent about it. Then its being a remover is learned from external [evidence] as we have presented in the chapter of tayammum.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi defense of the position that a resident junub may perform tayammum when water is unavailable, contrary to a literal reading of the verse (Quran 4:43). The commentator clarifies that the term 'abir al-sabil' refers to travelers. The apparent meaning of the verse prohibits all junub from approaching prayer except travelers who may do tayammum. Abu Hanifa responds by arguing that the prohibition is linked to the ability to find water; if a resident is unable to find water, the same need justifying tayammum for the sick applies. The objection that tayammum does not remove hadath is met by saying the verse is silent on removal; removal is established by other evidences (presented earlier in the book). This is a typical Hanafi use of istihsan to override a literal interpretation.

Key Terms
عابر سبيل (ābir sabīl): A traveler passing through, specifically one who is a traveler on a journey, as opposed to a resident.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Abu Hanifa's response permitting tayammum for a resident junub?
A) The consensus of scholars.
B) The need for purification when unable to use water.
C) The explicit text of the Quran.
D) A hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits tayammum for any Muslim unable to use water, even residents, based on the principle of need (hajah) and istihsan.

Taharah — Purification

The Special Exception of Ali (RA) for Entering the Mosque While Junub
Proving a specific exemption using hadith and defending its authenticity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a junub entering the mosque, distinguish the special exception of Ali (RA), and apply the ruling on tayammum for exiting.

Core Idea
Ali (RA) was uniquely permitted to be junub in the mosque; the general prohibition remains for all others.

This lesson explores a hadith that establishes both a clear prohibition and a unique exception. We will examine the proof, defend its authenticity against criticisms, and derive the practical fiqh ruling, including the recommended or obligatory tayammum for exiting the mosque when junub. Understanding this khassiyyah is key to grasping how special dispensations do not alter general rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its evidence
Locate the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and note the explicit wording: 'O Ali, it is not permissible for anyone to be junub in this mosque except me.' This establishes both the general prohibition and the exception for Ali (RA).
The hadith uses 'la yahillu' indicating prohibition (haram) for all except the Prophet and Ali.
2
Understand the exception as a khassiyyah
Recognize that the permission for Ali is a special privilege (khuṣūṣiyyah) not generalizable to others. The text compares it to other exceptions like the permission for al-Zubayr to wear silk.
Khassiyyah means the ruling applies only to that person; the general rule remains unchanged.
3
Assess the authenticity debate
Note the criticism of the hadith's chain (Salim ibn Abi Hafsa and Atiyyah al-Awfi) but also see the defense by Ibn Hajar, Ibn al-Mulaqqin, and others who cite multiple corroborating chains (al-Bazzar, al-Tabarani, al-Hakim). The Hanafi madhhab accepts the hadith as evidence.
The defense shows that despite weakness in one chain, the hadith is supported by other routes, raising it to a level of acceptability.
4
Derive the practical rule for a junub in the mosque
From the general prohibition, the text gives the ruling: if someone becomes junub in the mosque, they must perform tayammum to exit if safe; if they fear harm (e.g., night, danger), they may stay with tayammum but cannot pray or recite Quran.
The tayammum is for exiting, not for staying; staying is only allowed due to necessity.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on tayammum
Note that al-Dhakhira considers the tayammum mustahabb (recommended), while al-Muhit suggests it is wajib (obligatory). The author leans toward mustahabb but acknowledges the difference. The mufta bihi: if one fears harm, tayammum and staying are obligatory.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; both positions are valid for practice.

اهـ. فقد منعهم من الاجتياز والقعود ولم يستثن منهم غير علي خصوصية له كما خص الزبير بإباحة لبس الحرير لما شكا من أذى القمل، وخص غيره بغير ذلك وما ينطق عن الهوى. وقد صرح بهذا في خصوص ما نحن فيه فقد أخرج غير واحد من الحفاظ منهم الحاكم وقال صحيح الإسناد عن زيد بن أرقم قال : كان لنفر من أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبواب شارعة في المسجد قال فقال يوماً : سدوا هذه الأبواب إلا باب علي. قال: فتكلم في ذلك أناس قال فقام رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فحمد الله وأثنى عليه قال : أما بعد فإني أمرت بسد هذه الأبواب غير باب علي فقال فيه قائلكم وإني والله ما سددت شيئاً ولا فتحته ولكني أمرت بشيء فاتبعته . واعلم أن في تتمة الفتاوى الصغرى ويستوي في المنع المكث أو عبور محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وغيره خلاف ما قاله أهل الشيعة أنه رخص لآل محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم الدخول في المسجد لمكث أو عبور وإن كان جنباً لما روي أن النبي رخص لعلي وأهل بيته أن يمكثوا في المسجد وإن كانوا جنباً، وكذا رخص لهم لبس الحرير إلا أن هذا حديث شاذ لا نأخذ اهـ. وقال ابن أمير حاج والظاهر أن ما ذكره الشيعة لأهل علي في دخول المسجد ولبس الحرير اختلاق منهم على رسول الله ، وأما الحكم بالشذوذ على الترخيص لعلي في دخول المسجد جنباً ففيه نظر. نعم قضى ابن الجوزي في موضوعاته على حديث «سدوا الأبواب التي في المسجد إلا باب علي بأنه باطل لا يصح وهو من وضع الرافضة، وقد دفع ذلك شيخنا الحافظ ابن حجر في القول المسدد في الذب عن مسند أحمد وأفاد أنه جاء من طرق متظافرة من روايات الثقات تدل على أن الحديث صحيح منها ما ذكرنا آنفاً وبين عدم معارضته لحديث الصحيحين سدوا الأبواب المشارعة في المسجد إلا خوخة أبي بكر (٢) فليراجع ذلك من رام الوقوف عليه .اهـ. وقد علم أن دخوله المسجد جنباً ومكثه فيه من خواصه، وذكره النووي وقواه وفي منية المصلي : وإن احتلم في المسجد تيمم للخروج إذا لم يخف وإن خاف يجلس مع التيمم ولا يصلي ولا يقرأ .اهـ. وصرح في الذخيرة أن هذا التيمم مستحب وظاهر ما قدمناه في التيمم عن المحيط أنه واجب، ثم الظاهر أن المراد بالخوف الخوف من لحوق ضرر به بدناً أو مالاً كأن يكون ليلاً.
And what also indicates the [Hanafi] madhhab is what al-Tirmidhi narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'O Ali, it is not permissible for anyone to be junub in this mosque except me.' He said: a hasan gharib hadith. Then he mentioned from Ali ibn al-Mundhir: I said to Dirar ibn Surd: What does it mean? He said: 'It is not permissible for anyone to pass through it while junub except me and you.' Yes, the tahsin of al-Tirmidhi was criticized because in its chain are Salim ibn Abi Hafsa and Atiyyah al-Awfi, both weak, Shi'i, and accused. But al-Hafiz Siraj al-Din ibn al-Mulaqqin said: al-Bazzar narrated it from the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and al-Tabarani in his largest dictionary from the hadith of Umm Abi Salama. End quote. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: al-Bazzar mentioned in his Musnad that the hadith 'Close every door in the mosque except the door of Ali' came from the narrations of the Kufans and the Madinans narrating 'except the door of Abu Bakr.' He said: if the Kufan narration is established, then it means this meaning, and he mentioned the hadith of Abu Sa'id we already mentioned, then he said – meaning al-Bazzar – that the Kufan narrations came with good chains. And al-Qadi Isma'il al-Maliki in Ahkam al-Quran narrated from al-Muttalib ibn Ibn Hanṭab that the Prophet, peace be upon him, did not permit anyone to pass through the mosque or sit in it while junub except Ali ibn Abi Talib, because his house was in the mosque. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: it is a strong mursal. End quote. So he prevented them from passing and sitting, and exempted none among them except Ali as a special prerogative for him, just as he specially permitted al-Zubayr to wear silk when he complained of lice, and singled out others for other things, and he does not speak from desire. And he explicitly stated this concerning the specific matter we are discussing, for more than one of the preservers, among them al-Hakim who said it is sahih al-isnad, narrated from Zayd ibn Arqam: 'A number of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah had doors opening into the mosque. He said one day: Close these doors except the door of Ali. He said: Some people spoke about that. He said: The Messenger of Allah stood, praised and exalted Allah, then said: To proceed: I was commanded to close these doors except the door of Ali, so you spoke about it. By Allah, I did not close anything nor open it; I was commanded with something and I followed it.' Know that in Takmilat al-Fatawa al-Sughra: The prohibition is the same whether staying or passing, for Muhammad and for others, contrary to what the Shi'a say that he permitted the family of Muhammad to enter the mosque for staying or passing while junub, based on what was narrated that the Prophet permitted Ali and his family to stay in the mosque even if junub, and similarly permitted them to wear silk – except that this hadith is odd (shādhdh), we do not adopt it. End quote. Ibn Amir Hajj said: It appears that what the Shi'a mention about the family of Ali entering the mosque and wearing silk is a fabrication against the Messenger of Allah. As for the judgment of being odd on the permission for Ali to enter the mosque while junub, there is consideration. Yes, Ibn al-Jawzi in his Mawdu'at ruled on the hadith 'Close the doors in the mosque except the door of Ali' that it is false and unsound, and it is a fabrication of the Rafidah. Our teacher al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar refuted that in al-Qawl al-Musaddad fi al-Dhabb 'an Musnad Ahmad and stated that it came via multiple roads from trustworthy narrators indicating the hadith is authentic, including what we have mentioned earlier, and clarified that it does not contradict the hadith in the Two Sahihs: 'Close the doors opening into the mosque except the small door of Abu Bakr.' So let the one who wishes to know refer to that. End quote. And it is known that his entering the mosque while junub and staying there is one of his special characteristics. Al-Nawawi mentioned it and considered it strong. In Munyat al-Musalli: If someone becomes junub in the mosque, he does tayammum to exit if he does not fear [harm]; if he fears, he sits with tayammum, does not pray, and does not recite. End quote. It is explicitly stated in al-Dhakhira that this tayammum is recommended (mustahabb), while the apparent from what we presented earlier on tayammum from al-Muhit is that it is obligatory. Then it appears that the meaning of fear is fear of harm to body or property, such as if it is night.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the Hanafi madhhab that a junub is generally prohibited from entering the mosque, but an exception was made for Ali (RA) as a special privilege. The author cites several hadiths, including one from Tirmidhi and others, to establish that only Ali was allowed to be in the mosque while junub. The criticism of the chain is addressed by quoting Ibn Hajar and others who defend its authenticity. The passage then clarifies that this is not a general ruling; other people are not allowed. The fact that Ali's house was inside the mosque is noted as a reason. The author also distinguishes the Shi'a claim that the Prophet's family had a general exemption, stating that the hadith is shadh (odd) and not adopted. Finally, practical rulings are given: if someone becomes junub in the mosque, they should perform tayammum to exit if safe; if afraid (of harm to body or property), they may stay with tayammum but cannot pray or recite. There is a difference of opinion on whether the tayammum is mustahabb or wajib, with the text favoring mustahabb from al-Dhakhira but noting that al-Muhit suggests wajib.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl (bath).
خصوصية (khuṣūṣiyyah): A special privilege or exception granted to a particular person, not generalizable to others.
شاذ (shādhdh): An odd or anomalous narration that conflicts with more reliable sources; not adopted for practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, who is allowed to be in the mosque while junub?
A) All Muslims who have a valid excuse.
B) Only the Prophet Muhammad and Ali (RA).
C) Any member of the Prophet's household.
D) Only the Prophet, but not even Ali.

Key Takeaway
The general prohibition of a junub entering the mosque is affirmed, and the exception for Ali (RA) is a unique khassiyyah that does not change the rule for others.

The Ruling on Tawaf during Menstruation and Janabah
Stating the ruling that tawaf is prohibited for menstruating women and those in a state of janabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tawaf for a menstruating woman and a junub, and identify the dalil from the hadith.

Core Idea
Tawaf performed without purification is haram and invalid — the Prophet explicitly forbade it until purification.

In this lesson, we examine a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: menstruating women and those in a state of janabah are prohibited from performing tawaf. The proof is the famous hadith of Aisha at Sarif, where the Prophet commanded her to do everything except tawaf until she becomes purified. This ruling is unanimously agreed upon within the Hanafi school and supported by analogy to the prohibition of entering the mosque for prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is tawaf (circumambulation of the Ka'bah). The ruling is that it is haram (forbidden) for a menstruating woman or a person in janabah.
The text says 'فكان طوافها حراماً' — her tawaf was haram.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim: when Aisha menstruated at Sarif, the Prophet (PBUH) told her to do what pilgrims do except circumambulate until she becomes pure.
The hadith text is quoted in the commentary.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative prohibition 'لا تطوفي' is a clear nahi (prohibition) which, in Hanafi usul, indicates haram (forbidden). The exception is tied to purification, showing that purity is a condition.
The dalala is qat'i (definitive) because the hadith is mutawatir in meaning.
4
Note the analogy (qiyas)
The author mentions that this ruling is 'by analogy to the permission of entering [the mosque] for other than prayer.' This means that just as salah requires purity, tawaf requires purity because it is a form of worship in the mosque.
The analogy reinforces the prohibition; it does not create an exception.
5
Recognize the consensus within the Hanafi school
There is no ikhtilaf among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on this ruling. It is also agreed upon across the majority of schools.
The cross-madhhab status is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والطواف) أي ويمنع الحيض الطواف بالبيت وكذا الجنابة لما في الصحيحين أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال لعائشة رضي الله عنها لما حاضت بسرف : اقضي ما يقضي الحاج غير أن لا تطوفي بالبيت حتى تغتسلي فكان طوافها حراماً ولو فعلته كانت عاصية معاقبة قاله قياساً على إباحة الدخول لغير الصلاة.
His saying: (and al-tawaf) meaning: menstruation prevents the tawaf around the House, and similarly janabah, because in the Two Sahihs: He, peace be upon him, said to Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, when she menstruated at Sarif: 'Do what the pilgrim does except that do not circumambulate the House until you become purified.' So her tawaf was forbidden, and if she did it she would be sinful, subject to punishment. This is stated by analogy to the permission of entering [the mosque] for other than prayer.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof, addressed in the commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author explains that both menstruating women (hayd) and those in a state of major impurity (janabah) are prohibited from performing tawaf (circumambulation) around the Ka'bah. The proof is the well-known hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim where the Prophet (PBUH) told Aisha (RA) when she started menstruating at Sarif to do everything a pilgrim does except tawaf until she becomes pure. The ruling is that tawaf in such a state is haram (forbidden) and if performed, the person is sinful and subject to punishment. The author notes that this conclusion is arrived at by analogy (qiyas) to the permission of entering the mosque for other than prayer – meaning that just as entering the mosque for prayer is prohibited for the junub, tawaf (which is a form of worship in the mosque) is also prohibited. The exception for entering the mosque for other reasons (e.g., passing through) does not apply to tawaf.

Key Terms
طواف (tawāf): Circumambulation of the Ka'bah seven times, a pillar of Hajj and Umrah.
حائض (ḥā’iḍ): A woman experiencing menstrual bleeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if a menstruating woman performs tawaf according to the Hanafi ruling derived from the hadith?
A) Her tawaf is valid but she must repeat it after purification.
B) She is sinful and subject to punishment, and her tawaf is invalid.
C) It is permissible if she performs tayammum before tawaf.
D) It is makruh but not haram.

Key Takeaway
Tawaf requires purification; performing it while menstruating or in janabah is haram, invalid, and sinful.

Refuting Special Permissions for Ali and the Ruling on Tayammum for the Junub in the Mosque
Refuting shadh hadith and ruling on tayammum for junub in masjid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the Shi'a claim regarding Ali's special mosque permissions by identifying the shadh hadith, and state the Hanafi ruling on tayammum when one becomes junub inside the mosque.

Core Idea
A junub person must perform tayammum to exit the mosque; the Prophet's permissions are his exclusive khawass.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles two interlinked issues. First, it refutes a Shi'a claim that the Prophet granted Ali and his family special permissions to enter the mosque while junub and to wear silk. Second, it establishes the general ruling for anyone else who becomes junub inside the mosque: they must perform tayammum before exiting. We will examine the hadith evidence, the authentication debate, and the intra-Hanafi difference on whether this tayammum is wajib or mustahabb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shi'a claim
The Shi'a claim that the Prophet permitted Ali and his family to enter the mosque while junub and to wear silk. The author refutes this by calling the supporting hadith 'shadh' (irregular) and not acted upon.
The author cites Ibn Amir Hajj who says it's a fabrication.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
The author discusses the hadith about closing doors except Ali's. He notes that Ibn al-Jawzi ruled it false, but Ibn Hajar defended its authenticity via multiple chains. However, the authenticity of the hadith does not imply the Shi'a interpretation; the Prophet's permissions are his exclusive characteristics.
Key point: even if the hadith is authentic, it applies only to the Prophet, not as a general rule.
3
Recognize the principle of khawass
The passage states that entering the mosque while junub and staying in it are from the Prophet's special characteristics (khawassihi). This means no one else has these permissions.
This principle overrides any apparent permission in the hadith for others.
4
State the ruling for a junub in the mosque
If a person becomes junub (e.g., due to a wet dream) inside the mosque, they must perform tayammum to exit. If they fear harm (e.g., it is night or risk of loss), they may sit with tayammum and not pray or recite.
The ruling is from Minyat al-Musalli.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Al-Dhakhira considers this tayammum mustahabb, while al-Muhit indicates it is wajib. The author does not specify the mufta bihi, but the passage leans towards the view from al-Muhit.
This difference reflects the Hanafi tradition of evaluating obligations.

آل به الزبير بإباحة لبس الحرير لما شكا من أذى القمل، وخص غيره بغير ذلك وما ينطق عن الهوى. وقد صرح بهذا في خصوص ما نحن فيه فقد أخرج غير واحد من الحفاظ منهم الحاكم وقال صحيح الإسناد عن زيد بن أرقم قال : كان لنفر من أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبواب شارعة في المسجد قال فقال يوماً : سدوا هذه الأبواب إلا باب علي. قال: فتكلم في ذلك أناس قال فقام رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فحمد الله وأثنى عليه قال : أما بعد فإني أمرت بسد هذه الأبواب غير باب علي فقال فيه قائلكم وإني والله ما سددت شيئاً ولا فتحته ولكني أمرت بشيء فاتبعته . واعلم أن في تتمة الفتاوى الصغرى ويستوي في المنع المكث أو عبور محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وغيره خلاف ما قاله أهل الشيعة أنه رخص لآل محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم الدخول في المسجد لمكث أو عبور وإن كان جنباً لما روي أن النبي رخص لعلي وأهل بيته أن يمكثوا في المسجد وإن كانوا جنباً، وكذا رخص لهم لبس الحرير إلا أن هذا حديث شاذ لا نأخذ اهـ. وقال ابن أمير حاج والظاهر أن ما ذكره الشيعة لأهل علي في دخول المسجد ولبس الحرير اختلاق منهم على رسول الله ، وأما الحكم بالشذوذ على الترخيص لعلي في دخول المسجد جنباً ففيه نظر. نعم قضى ابن الجوزي في موضوعاته على حديث «سدوا الأبواب التي في المسجد إلا باب علي بأنه باطل لا يصح وهو من وضع الرافضة، وقد دفع ذلك شيخنا الحافظ ابن حجر في القول المسدد في الذب عن مسند أحمد وأفاد أنه جاء من طرق متظافرة من روايات الثقات تدل على أن الحديث صحيح منها ما ذكرنا آنفاً وبين عدم معارضته لحديث الصحيحين سدوا الأبواب المشارعة في المسجد إلا خوخة أبي بكر فليراجع ذلك من رام الوقوف عليه .اهـ. وقد علم أن دخوله المسجد جنباً ومكثه فيه من خواصه، وذكره النووي وقواه وفي منية المصلي : وإن احتلم في المسجد تيمم للخروج إذا لم يخف وإن خاف يجلس مع التيمم ولا يصلي ولا يقرأ .اهـ. وصرح في الذخيرة أن هذا التيمم مستحب وظاهر ما قدمناه في التيمم عن المحيط أنه واجب، ثم الظاهر أن المراد بالخوف الخوف من لحوق ضرر به بدناً أو مالاً كأن يكون ليلاً.
He attributed to al-Zubayr the permission of wearing silk because he complained of the harm of lice, and he specified others with other than that, and he does not speak from desire. He explicitly stated this in particular regarding what we are in, for more than one of the preservers, among them al-Hakim, extracted it and said it has a sound chain from Zayd ibn Arqam who said: Some companions of the Messenger of Allah had doors opening into the mosque. He said one day: Close these doors except the door of Ali. He said: Some people spoke about that. He said: The Messenger of Allah stood, praised Allah, and said: As for after, I was commanded to close these doors except the door of Ali. He said: Your speaker spoke about it, and by Allah, I did not close anything nor open it, but I was commanded with something and I followed it. Know that in Takmilat al-Fatawa al-Sughra: The prohibition of staying or passing is the same for Muhammad and others, contrary to what the Shi'a said that he permitted the family of Muhammad to enter the mosque for staying or passing even if in a state of janabah, based on what was narrated that the Prophet permitted Ali and his family to stay in the mosque even if they were junub, and similarly permitted them to wear silk, except that this hadith is shadh, we do not take it. Ibn Amir Hajj said: It appears that what the Shi'a mentioned for the family of Ali regarding entering the mosque and wearing silk is a fabrication against the Messenger of Allah. As for the ruling of shudhudh on the permission for Ali to enter the mosque while junub, there is consideration in it. Yes, Ibn al-Jawzi ruled in his Mawdu'at on the hadith 'Close the doors that are in the mosque except the door of Ali' that it is false, not sound, and it is from the fabrication of the Rafidah. Our teacher al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar refuted that in al-Qawl al-Musaddad fi al-Dhabb 'an Musnad Ahmad and informed that it came from multiple strong chains from trustworthy narrators indicating that the hadith is sound, including what we mentioned earlier, and he explained that it does not contradict the hadith of the two Sahihs: 'Close the doors opening into the mosque except the small door of Abu Bakr.' So let whoever seeks to know it refer to that. It is known that his entering the mosque while junub and his staying in it are from his special characteristics. Al-Nawawi mentioned it and strengthened it. In Minyat al-Musalli: If he has a wet dream in the mosque, he makes tayammum for exiting if he does not fear, and if he fears, he sits with tayammum and does not pray or recite. It is explicitly stated in al-Dhakhira that this tayammum is mustahabb, but the apparent meaning of what we presented in al-Muhit regarding tayammum is that it is wajib. Then it is apparent that what is meant by fear is fear of harm to himself, bodily or financially, such as if it is night.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and a refutation of a Shia claim. The author begins by discussing a hadith about closing doors in the mosque except for Ali's, citing narrations from al-Hakim and others. He then addresses the Shia claim that the Prophet permitted Ali and his family to enter the mosque while junub and wear silk. The author refutes this, stating the hadith is shadh (irregular) and not accepted, and that Ibn Amir Hajj considers it a fabrication. He mentions Ibn al-Jawzi's ruling that the hadith is false, but notes that Ibn Hajar defended its authenticity. The passage then transitions to a general ruling: the Prophet's permission to stay in the mosque while junub is a special characteristic of his. For others, if someone becomes junub in the mosque, they must perform tayammum to exit. The author presents a difference of opinion: al-Dhakhira considers this tayammum mustahabb, while al-Muhit suggests it is wajib. The ruling is explained with the condition of fear of harm to oneself or property.

Key Terms
شاذ (shadh): A hadith that is narrated by a reliable chain but contradicts a more reliable narration; considered weak and not acted upon.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with clean earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
خواصه (khawassihi): Special characteristics or privileges unique to the Prophet, not applicable to others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who becomes junub in the mosque?
A) He may stay in the mosque without tayammum because necessity permits it.
B) He must perform tayammum to exit if he does not fear harm; if he fears harm, he may sit with tayammum and not pray or recite.
C) He must immediately leave without any tayammum.
D) He must perform ghusl with water before exiting.

Key Takeaway
A person who becomes junub inside the mosque must perform tayammum to exit, unless there is a genuine fear of harm, in which case they may sit with tayammum without praying or reciting.

Prohibition of Tawaf for a Menstruating Woman
Stating the prohibition of tawaf for a menstruating woman with proof from hadith and discussing the reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on tawaf for a menstruating woman, cite the hadith proof, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference in reasoning for the prohibition.

Core Idea
Purity is obligatory in tawaf, making it haram for a menstruating woman; the prohibition is not solely due to the mosque.

In this passage, the author addresses the prohibition of tawaf for a woman experiencing menstruation or janabah. The primary proof comes from the well-known hadith where the Prophet told Aisha to do everything a pilgrim does except circumambulate the Kaaba until she becomes pure. However, there is a subtle internal Hanafi debate: is the prohibition because tawaf is performed inside the mosque, or is it because purity is a required condition for tawaf itself? This lesson unpacks the ruling, the evidence, and the scholarly difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognise that the act being discussed is tawaf (circumambulation of the Kaaba) for a menstruating woman or someone in janabah. The ruling is that it is haram (forbidden).
The passage also extends the same ruling to janabah by analogy.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim where the Prophet told Aisha: 'Perform what the pilgrim performs except do not circumambulate the House until you purify yourself.' This is a direct command indicating prohibition.
The hadith is ahad (single-chained) but accepted in the Sahihs.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's words 'do not circumambulate' are a clear prohibition (nahy). In Hanafi usul, a nahy indicates haram unless there is evidence to reduce it to makruh. The exception 'until you purify yourself' shows that purity is the obstacle.
This proof is zanni in thubut (transmission) and dalala (meaning) but strong enough to establish prohibition.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Hidayah reasoned that the prohibition is because tawaf is in the mosque. The commentator (and Fath al-Qadir) argue it is better not to limit to the mosque; the real reason is that purity is obligatory in tawaf itself. This leads to a difference: if tawaf is outside the mosque, is it still haram or only makruh tahrimi?
The mufta bihi is that purity is a condition of tawaf itself, so the prohibition remains even outside the mosque.
5
Apply the preferred view
The author sides with Fath al-Qadir: the prohibition is absolute. However, some later scholars consider it makruh tahrimi outside the mosque. The practical consequence: a menstruating woman must not perform tawaf anywhere until she becomes pure.
Also, if she menstruates during tawaf, she must stop and cannot stay in the mosque.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والطواف) أي ويمنع الحيض الطواف بالبيت وكذا الجنابة لما في الصحيحين أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال لعائشة رضي الله عنها لما حاضت بسرف : اقضي ما يقضي الحاج غير أن لا تطوفي بالبيت حتى تغتسلي فكان طوافها حراماً ولو فعلته كانت عاصية معاقبة قاله قياساً على إباحة الدخول لغير الصلاة. قوله : (لأن المعنى فاقربوها جنباً) كذا في النسخ وصوابه وتتحلل به من إحرامها بطواف الزيارة وعليها بدنه كطواف الجنب كما سيأتي في محله إن شاء الله تعالى. وعلل للمنع صاحب الهداية بأن الطواف في المسجد وكان الأولى عدم الاقتصار على هذا التعليل فإن حرمة الطواف جنباً ليس منظوراً فيه إلى دخول المسجد بالذات بل لأن الطهارة واجبة في الطواف، فلو لم يكن ثمة مسجد حرم عليها الطواف. كذا في فتح القدير وغيره. وقد يقال : إن حرمة الطواف عليها إنما هي لأجل كونه في المسجد، وأما إذا لم يكن الطواف في المسجد بل خارجه فإنه مكروه كراهة تحريم لما عرف من أن الطهارة له واجبة على الصحيح فتركها يوجب كراهة التحريم ولا يوجب التحريم إلا ترك الفرض، ولو حاضت بعدما دخلت وجب عليها أن لا تطوف وحرم مكثها كما صرحوا به.
His saying: (And tawaf) i.e., it prevents menstruating woman from tawaf of the House, and likewise janabah, because in the two Sahihs that he, peace be upon him, said to Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, when she menstruated at Sarif: 'Perform what the pilgrim performs except do not circumambulate the House until you purify yourself.' So her tawaf was forbidden, and if she did it, she would be disobedient, punished. He said this by analogy on the permissibility of entering for other than prayer. His saying: (Because the meaning: approach it while junub) such in the copies, and its correct form is: and she becomes free from her ihram by the tawaf of ziyarah, and upon her is a sacrificial animal like the tawaf of the junub as will come in its place, Allah willing. The author of al-Hidayah gave the reason for the prohibition that tawaf is in the mosque, and it would have been better not to restrict to this reasoning, for the prohibition of tawaf while junub is not considered due to entering the mosque per se, but because purity is obligatory in tawaf. If there were no mosque, tawaf would be forbidden for her. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir and others. It may be said: The prohibition of tawaf for her is only because it is in the mosque, but if tawaf is not in the mosque but outside it, then it is makruh tahrimi, because it is known that purity for it is obligatory according to the correct view, so leaving it causes makruh tahrimi, but prohibition is only caused by leaving a fard. If she menstruates after she entered, it becomes obligatory for her not to circumambulate, and her staying is forbidden as they explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The author explains that a menstruating woman and one in a state of janabah are prohibited from performing tawaf (circumambulation) around the Kaaba. The proof is from the Sahih hadith where the Prophet told Aisha to do everything a pilgrim does except tawaf until she becomes pure. The author then discusses the reasoning: al-Hidayah says the prohibition is because tawaf is in the mosque, but the commentator argues it is better not to limit it to that, because the real reason is that purity is obligatory in tawaf itself. Thus, even if tawaf were performed outside the mosque, it would still be forbidden. However, another view holds that if tawaf is outside the mosque, it is makruh tahrimi (sinful but not absolutely forbidden). The passage concludes that if a woman menstruates after entering the mosque, she must not circumambulate and may not stay in the mosque.

Key Terms
طواف (tawaf): Circumambulation around the Kaaba, a ritual act of worship performed during Hajj and Umrah.
جنابة (janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, e.g., after sexual intercourse or ejaculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a menstruating woman performing tawaf outside the mosque (e.g., in a designated area)?
A) It is perfectly permissible because the mosque restriction is lifted.
B) It is haram, because purity is obligatory for tawaf itself.
C) It is makruh tahrimi according to one view, and haram according to another.
D) It is makruh tanzihi, disliked but not sinful.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of tawaf for a menstruating woman is based on the condition of purity in tawaf itself, not merely the mosque, and it is haram to perform tawaf while in a state of major impurity.

Prohibition of Intercourse during Menstruation and Expiation
Stating the prohibition and discussing expiation and acceptance of wife's claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse with a menstruating woman, explain the recommended expiation, and identify the conditions under which a husband must accept his wife's claim of menstruation.

Core Idea
Intercourse during menstruation is haram by consensus; expiation is recommended, not obligatory, and the husband must believe his wife's claim if she is trustworthy or if it is plausible.

This lesson examines a passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a leading Hanafi commentary, which establishes the prohibition of marital intimacy during menstruation. The text not only confirms the consensus ruling based on the Quran but also delves into the nuances of expiation, the husband's duty to accept his wife's report, and the controversial question of whether considering this act halal constitutes disbelief. By understanding these details, you will be able to apply the correct ruling in both personal and advisory contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage explicitly states that intercourse with a menstruating woman is prohibited (haram) by consensus (mujma' 'alayh). The act is considered a major sin (kabirah) when done knowingly, deliberately, and willingly.
The prohibition applies to vaginal intercourse; other forms of intimacy may be permissible.
2
Locate the dalil and examine its evidentiary force
The primary proof is Quran 2:222: 'wa la taqrabuhunna hatta yat-hurn.' The imperative form indicates prohibition. The Hanafis note that the text is qati' al-thubut (definitive transmission) but zanni al-dalalah (speculative meaning) regarding the extent of prohibition.
The verse uses 'qurban' as a euphemism, but classical tafsir confirms it refers to intercourse.
3
Analyze the conditions for sin and expiation
Sin only accrues if the person is knowledgeable, deliberate, and voluntary. The ignorant, forgetful, or coerced are excused; they need only repent. Expiation (sadaqah) is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib. The amount varies: a dinar or half, depending on timing (beginning/end of menses) or blood color (black/yellow).
The hadith narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Hakim is used as evidence for the color-based distinction, but it is not authoritative enough to make expiation obligatory.
4
Understand the acceptance of the wife's claim of menstruation
If the wife is morally upright ('afifah), her word is accepted and the husband must refrain. If she is a sinner (fasiqah), her claim is accepted only if it is plausible (e.g., during her usual period). This is based on the principle that reports from a fasiq are only acted upon if truthfulness is predominant.
A conditional divorce tied to her report takes effect even if the husband denies it, because he negligently tied it to something only she knows.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on kufr for mustahill
Some Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Mabsut, al-Ikhtiyar) declare a person who deems this intercourse halal as a kafir. However, the text prefers the view from al-Khulasa that he does not become a kafir, because the prohibition is not established by a qati' proof (the details rely on ahad hadith). Takfir is avoided in cases of doubt.
The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) is that such a person is sinful but not a kafir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقربان ما تحت الأزار) أي ويمنع الحيض قربان زوجها ما تحت أزارها، أما حرمة وطئها عليه فمجمع عليها لقوله تعالى ولا تقربوهن حتى يطهرن﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢٢] ووطؤها في الفرج عالماً بالحرمة عامداً مختاراً كبيرة لا جاهلاً ولا ناسياً ولا مكرهاً فليس عليه إلا التوبة والاستغفار. وهل يجب التعزير أم لا؟ ويستحب أن يتصدق بدينار أو نصفه، وقيل بدينار إن كان أول الحيض، ونصفه إن وطئ في آخره كأن قائله رأى أن لا معنى للتخيير بين القليل والكثير في النوع الواحد، ومصرفة مصرف الزكاة كما في السراج الوهاج. وقيل إن كان الدم أسود يتصدق بدينار وإن كان أصفر فبنصف دينار، ويدل له ما رواه أبو داود والحاكم وصححه إذا واقع الرجل أهله وهي حائض إن كان دماً أحمر فليتصدق بدينار وإن كان أصفر فليتصدق بنصف دينار. وفي السراج الوهاج وإذا أخبرته بالحيض قال بعضهم : إن كانت فاسقة لا يقبل قولها وإن كانت عفيفة يقبل قولها وترك وطأها. وقال بعضهم: إن كان صدقها ممكناً بأن كانت في أوان حيضها قبلت ولو كانت فاسقة كما في العدة وهذا القول أحوط وأقرب إلى الورع .اهـ. فعلم من هذا أنها إذا كانت فاسقة ولم يغلب على ظنه صدقها بأن كانت في غير أوان حيضها لا يقبل قولها اتفاقاً كما قالوا في أخبار الفاسق إنه يشترط لوجوب العمل به أن يغلب على الظن ،صدقه وبهذا علم أن ما في فتح القدير من الحرمة تثبت بإخبارها وأن كذبها ليس على إطلاقه بل إذا كانت عفيفة أو غلب على الظن صدقها بخلاف من علق به طلاقها فأخبرته به فإنه يقع الطلاق عليه وإن كذبها مطلقاً لتقصيره في تعليقه بما لا يعرف إلا من جهتها. وهذا إذا وطئها غير مستحل فإن كان مستحلاً له فقد صاحب المبسوط والاختيار وفتح القدر وغيرهم بكفره ، وذكره القاضي الاسبيجابي بصيغة وقيل» وصحح أنه لا يكفر. صاحب الخلاصة : ويوافقه ما نقله أيضاً من الفصل الثاني في ألفاظ الكفر من اعتقد الحرام حلالاً أو على القلب يكفر إذا كان حراماً لعينه وثبتت حرمته بدليل مقطوع به، أما إذا كان حراماً لغيره بدليل مقطوع به أو حراماً لعينه بأخبار الآحاد لا يكفر إذا اعتقده حلالاً .اهـ. فعلى هذا لا يفتى بتكفير مستحله لما في الخلاصة أن جزم أن
His saying: (And approaching what is under the waist-wrapper) i.e., it prevents the menstruating woman from her husband approaching what is under her waist-wrapper. As for the prohibition of intercourse with her, it is agreed upon due to His statement: 'And do not approach them until they become pure' [al-Baqarah: 222]. Intercourse in the vagina knowingly, deliberately, and willingly is a major sin; not for the ignorant, forgetful, or coerced. He has nothing but repentance and seeking forgiveness. Is tazir (discretionary punishment) obligatory or not? And it is mustahabb for him to give in charity a dinar or half a dinar. It was said a dinar if at the beginning of menstruation and half if at the end, as if the one who said it saw no meaning in choosing between little and much in one type. Its expenditure is on the recipients of zakat as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. It was said if the blood is black, he gives in charity a dinar; if yellow, half a dinar. Evidence for this is what Abu Dawud and al-Hakim narrated and authenticated: 'If a man has intercourse with his wife while she is menstruating, if the blood is red, let him give a dinar; if yellow, half a dinar.' In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If she informs him of menstruation, some said if she is a sinner, her statement is not accepted; if chaste, her statement is accepted and he refrains from intercourse. Some said if it is possible that she is truthful, i.e., if it is the time of her menstruation, her statement is accepted even if she is a sinner, as in al-Uddah, and this view is more cautious and closer to piety. So it is understood from this that if she is a sinner and he does not have a predominant belief in her truthfulness, such as if it is not her usual time, her statement is not accepted by agreement, as they said regarding the report of a sinner: it is a condition for acting on it that his truthfulness is predominant. And from this it is understood that what is in Fath al-Qadir that prohibition is established by her report, and that her lie is not absolute, but if she is chaste or he predominantly believes her truthfulness, unlike one who suspended his wife's divorce and she informed him of it, then the divorce occurs even if he denies it absolutely, because of his negligence in suspending it on something known only from her. This is if he has intercourse while not considering it halal. If he considers it halal, then the author of al-Mabsut, al-Ikhtiyar, Fath al-Qadir, and others ruled him a kafir. Qadi al-Isbijabi mentioned it in the form 'qil' and corrected that he does not become a kafir. The author of al-Khulasa: It agrees with what he also transmitted from the second chapter on words of kufr: whoever believes the forbidden to be halal, or in the heart, becomes a kafir if it is forbidden for its own essence and its prohibition is established by a definitive proof. But if it is forbidden for something else by a definitive proof, or forbidden for its own essence by ahad reports, he does not become a kafir if he believes it halal. So on this basis, one does not give a fatwa of kufr for the one who considers it halal, because in al-Khulasa that he definitely...
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the prohibition of intercourse with a menstruating woman. It begins by explaining that the term 'qurban' (approaching) refers to intercourse, which is prohibited by Quranic text (al-Baqarah: 222). The sin is major if done knowingly and deliberately; the ignorant, forgetful, or coerced are excused and must only repent. The author then discusses expiation: it is mustahabb to give a dinar or half in charity, with differences based on whether the intercourse occurred at the beginning or end of menstruation, or based on blood color (black vs. yellow). A hadith supports the blood color distinction. Next, the text addresses whether the husband must believe his wife's claim of menstruation: if she is morally upright, her word is accepted; if she is a sinner, it is accepted only if it is plausible (e.g., during her usual period). A conditional divorce upon her report of menstruation takes effect even if he denies it. Finally, the text addresses the ruling on one who considers such intercourse halal (mustahill): some scholars declare him a kafir, but the preferred view is that he does not become a kafir because the prohibition is not definitively established (being based on ahad hadith). The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
قربان (qurban): Literally 'approaching'; here it refers to sexual intercourse.
كبيرة (kabirah): Major sin, requiring repentance and having serious consequences in the afterlife.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment imposed by a judge for offenses not covered by fixed punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who considers intercourse with a menstruating woman halal (mustahill)?
A) He becomes a kafir by consensus within the Hanafi school
B) He becomes a kafir according to al-Mabsut but not according to al-Khulasa
C) He does not become a kafir because the prohibition is not definitively established
D) He is considered a sinner but not a kafir, and this is agreed upon

Key Takeaway
Intercourse during menstruation is haram (major sin); expiation is recommended but not obligatory; and a husband must accept his wife's claim of menstruation if she is trustworthy or if it is plausible.

Intimacy with a Menstruating Wife: The Hanafi Majority vs. Muhammad
Explains the prohibition of intimacy between navel and knee during menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on intimacy with a menstruating wife, identify the key evidence, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan.

Core Idea
The Hanafi majority prohibits all intimacy between navel and knee during menstruation, even if only the private part is explicitly forbidden.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key fiqh question: what intimacy is permitted with a menstruating wife? The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf) prohibits all contact between navel and knee. However, Muhammad ibn al-Hasan restricts the prohibition to the private part only. We will examine the evidence and the reasoning for the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and identify the opponent
What is the ruling of the Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) regarding intimacy with a menstruating wife? Who disagrees within the Hanafi school?
2
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence does Muhammad ibn al-Hasan use to support his view that only the private part is prohibited?
Consider the hadith 'Do everything except intercourse.'
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi majority respond to Muhammad's evidence? What hadith do they cite and how do they interpret it?
The key hadith is 'For you what is above the izār' (Abu Dawud).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two positions? How does it affect a husband's permissible actions?
5
Identify the tarjih and mufta bihi
Which position is relied upon in the Hanafi school? Why does the commentator reject al-Sarūjī's attempt to prefer Muhammad's view?
The majority view is mufta bihi.
