The issue: If [a ruling] contains aspects that necessitate disbelief and one aspect that prevents, the mufti must incline to that aspect. As for intimacy with her other than intercourse, the school of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Malik is that what is between the navel and the knee is prohibited upon him, and that is what is meant by 'under the izār'. Thus in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. In al-Muḥīṭ and Fatāwā al-Walwālijī, interpretation of 'izār' according to their [the two] views: Some said: the known izār, and he may enjoy what is above the navel and not what is beneath it. Some said: it means covering; so if she covers, enjoyment becomes permissible for him. The apparent is what is limited in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and Ahmad said: nothing besides the private part is prohibited; and from the Mālikīs, Aṣbagh chose this; and from the Shāfi'īs, al-Nawawī. Because the group except al-Bukhārī narrated that the Jews, when their women menstruated, they would not eat with them nor cohabit with them in the houses; so the Companions asked the Prophet ﷺ about that, and Allah revealed 'They ask you about menstruation' [2:222]; the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Do everything except marriage,' and in a narration 'except intercourse.' And from the group: that she asked the Messenger of Allah ﷺ about what is permissible for me from my wife while she is menstruating; he said: 'For you what is above the izār.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and he remained silent on it, so it is a proof. Thus the tarjīḥ is for it because that [the opinion prohibiting] is a preventing [factor] and that [the other] is permissive, and due to the report: 'Whoever circles around the protected sanctuary is likely to fall into it.' As for al-Sarūjī's tarjīḥ of Muhammad's saying because his evidence is explicitly worded and our evidence is implied (mafhūm) and the explicit (manṭūq) is stronger, so it is preferred—that is incorrect. First, because it is not necessary that our evidence is implied; rather it is possible that it is explicit, for the questioner asked about all that is permissible for him from his menstruating wife; so his saying 'for you what is above the izār' means all that is permissible for you is what is above the izār, to match the answer to the question. Second, because even if it were implied, this implied meaning is stronger than the explicit, because it indicates the implied meaning by way of necessity, due to the obligation of his answer to match the questioner's question; and if this implied meaning were not intended, it would not match; thus its establishment is obligatory from the wording in a manner that does not accept specification or alteration for this accidental reason, while the explicit as such accepts that; so tarjīḥ in the matter by explicitness and lack of preferability by implicitness is not valid. And his action was upon that, so it was not specific. The letter alif after was dropped by the first copyist's pen. His saying: 'And one could say it is permissible etc.' Al-Nahr said: The requirement of consideration is to say that it is prohibited for her to touch him where [her touch] is between her navel and knee, not where it is between his navel and knee, as when she puts her hand on his private part... Some virtuous ones said: It is a strong objection because direct touch (mubāshara) is reciprocal from both sides; so as it is prohibited upon him, it is prohibited upon her. So the statement of al-Baḥr 'and he and she are absent' is conceded but it does not benefit. Because we did not consider that; rather as long as she is described with menstruation, direct touch is prohibited, whether it is... until he touches one of them while she is menstruating until he orders her to wear the izār—agreed upon. As for His saying 'And do not approach them until they are pure' [2:222], if it is a prohibition of intercourse itself, then it is not improbable that we establish another prohibition in another place by the Sunnah. Beware of thinking that this is an addition to the text by a solitary hadith, because it restricts an unrestricted text, so it would be conflicting with it in some of its implications, and what the Sunnah has established in our matter is legislation of what the Qur'anic text did not address, so it is not from the chapter of addition. And if it is a prohibition of what is more general than intercourse, then intercourse is one of the individuals of the prohibited thing, because it includes the prohibition of enjoyment with her, meaning from intercourse and other enjoyments. Then specification of some of them appears by the hadith indicating the permissibility of things other than what is between the navel and knee, so what is between them remains included in the general prohibition of approaching them, even if this consideration is not needed for the establishment of the desired due to what we have explained. Thus in Fatḥ al-Qadīr with some abbreviation. Know that just as it is prohibited for him to enjoy what is between the navel and knee, it is prohibited for her to enable it, and I have not seen from them explicitly the ruling of her direct touching of him. And one could prohibit it because when enabling her enjoyment of him is prohibited, her action is prohibited by a fortiori. And one could permit it because the prohibition is upon him due to her being menstruating, and that is absent in her case, so enjoyment of him is permissible for her, and because her touching his penis at most is enjoyment with her palm, and that is absolutely permissible. Warnings: In some expressions, the term 'enjoyment' occurs, and it includes looking and touching with desire. In many expressions, the term 'direct touch' and 'approach' occurs, and their implication is prohibition of touching without desire. So between them is general and specific from a certain perspective. What appears is that prohibition is attached to direct touch even without desire, as opposed to looking even with desire, and it is not greater than kissing her face with desire, as is not hidden. And it has been known from... Some said: What al-Nahr said is good, and it appears that it is what the author of al-Baḥr intended, as understood by his reasoning for the first opinion and the second reasoning for the second opinion. His saying: 'And what appears etc.' Al-Nahr said: One could distinguish between them: looking at this specific [private area] with desire is enjoyment of what is not permissible, unlike kissing the face, as is apparent. But some virtuous ones said, it is objected: if he means by 'enjoyment of what is not permissible' that it is enjoyment of a place whose direct touch is not permissible, then it is conceded, but prohibition of direct touch does not necessitate prohibition of looking. And if he means that it is enjoyment of a place it is not permissible to look at, then it is exactly the claimed point, so it is circular. This is the bright proof for the claimant of al-Baḥr. That is, the Lawgiver prohibited direct touch, which is meeting of the two private parts without a barrier. But because the private part has a precinct, which is what is between the navel and knee, it is also prohibited from that, out of fear of falling into what might be fallen into by approaching this place, for 'whoever circles around the protected sanctuary is likely to fall into it.' Or it is said: The Lawgiver is wise, and these places are not free from filth impurity, so He prohibited approach out of fear of contamination. So looking at these places remains on the original permissibility due to marriage; its prohibition has no evidence. I say: It could be said that looking is from 'circling the protected sanctuary,' and for this it is prohibited with a non-mahram woman out of fear of falling into the forbidden. And it is supported by what is in al-Istiḥsān from al-Ḥaqā'iq from al-Tuḥfa and al-Khāniyya: The man avoids from the menstruating woman what is under the izār according to the first; and Muhammad said: He avoids the mark of blood, meaning intercourse, and for him what is above.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage from al-Baḥr al-Rā'iq is an expansion (sharḥ) on the ruling of intimacy with a menstruating wife. It begins by stating the majority position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Malik that what is between the navel and knee is prohibited. This is equated with 'what is under the izār' (a waist-wrapper). The commentator notes that in other sources there is disagreement on the meaning of izār: some say it is the known garment, so only the area beneath the navel is prohibited; others say it means 'covering,' so if she covers, all becomes permissible. The commentator prefers the first interpretation as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. Then it presents the view of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and Ahmad ibn Hanbal that only the private part itself is prohibited, supported by Aṣbagh from the Malikis and al-Nawawi from the Shafi'is. Their primary evidence is the hadith 'Do everything except intercourse' and the hadith 'For you what is above the izār.' The commentator refutes al-Sarūjī's attempt to prefer Muhammad's view by arguing that the Hanafi evidence is actually explicit (manṭūq), not just implied (mafhūm), and even if it were implied, it is stronger due to the necessity of matching the Prophet's answer to the question. Then it discusses the ruling of the wife's direct touching of her husband (mubāshara). There is disagreement: one view prohibits it because of reciprocity; another permits it because the prohibition is specific to the husband. The passage ends with a discussion on whether looking at the prohibited area with desire is prohibited. The relied-upon view is that direct touch is prohibited even without desire, but looking is not prohibited by strong evidence, though it should be avoided due to the principle of 'circling the protected sanctuary.'

Key Terms
الاستمتاع (al-istimtā'): Intimate enjoyment, including touching and looking with desire.
المباشرة (al-mubāshara): Direct skin-to-skin contact of private parts or between navel and knee.
ما بين السرة والركبة (mā bayna al-surrah wa al-rukbah): The area between navel and knee, considered the 'precinct' of the private parts.
الازار (al-izār): A waist-wrapper or loincloth; metaphorically the area it covers (navel to knee).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on a husband touching his menstruating wife's thigh (between navel and knee)?
A) Permissible, because only intercourse is prohibited.
B) Prohibited, because all intimacy between navel and knee is prohibited.
C) Permissible, because she can cover it with clothing.
D) Recommended, as it shows affection.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view prohibits all intimate contact between navel and knee during menstruation, based on the hadith 'For you what is above the izār' and the principle of avoiding proximity to the prohibited.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Ruling on Intimate Contact with a Menstruating Wife
Explains the prohibition of intercourse and the extent of permissible enjoyment between navel and knee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what is permissible and prohibited in intimate contact with a menstruating wife, and explain the evidence and intra-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Direct contact (mubasharah) between navel and knee is haram; looking is permissible even with desire.

Intimacy during a wife's menstruation is regulated by clear Quran and hadith. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, distinguishing between direct touching (prohibited) and looking (permissible), and addresses a nuance: can the wife touch her husband? We'll explore the evidence and the reasoning behind the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core prohibited act
The primary prohibition is direct contact (mubasharah) between the husband and the wife's area from navel to knee. Understand that this is based on the hadith of the izar.
The term mubasharah is narrower than istimta' (enjoyment).
2
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The hadith (Bukhari and Muslim) records that the Prophet would command a menstruating wife to wear an izar before intimacy. The Quranic verse (2:222) prohibits 'approaching' them until purification. The Hanafi analysis uses the hadith to specify (takhsis) the general verse, limiting the prohibition to direct contact between navel and knee.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalala according to the Hanafi view.
3
Understand the Hanafi distinction: mubasharah vs. looking
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq argues that prohibition is attached to direct contact (even without desire), while looking (even with desire) is not prohibited. This is supported by reasoning that the lawgiver only forbade direct contact, and the extension to the area between navel and knee is a protective measure (hurm) to prevent falling into the prohibited act.
Compare with kissing the face, which is clearly permissible.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on looking
Some later Hanafi scholars argued that looking is also prohibited because it is 'hovering around the sanctuary.' Al-Bahr al-Raiq responds that the analogy with a non-mahram woman is invalid, and there is no explicit evidence for prohibition.
The preferred (mufta bihi) position is permissibility of looking.
5
Determine the practical implications
The husband may enjoy his wife above the navel and below the knee without a barrier, and the area between navel and knee with a barrier. The wife's touching of her husband is subject to ikhtilaf; the passage notes that by analogy, if she cannot enable him to enjoy her, her active touching is more prohibited, but some argue it is permissible because the prohibition is on him.
The author does not give a definitive conclusion on the wife touching, but the default is permissibility due to absence of evidence.

حتى يباشر إحداهن وهي حائض حتى يأمرها أن تأتزر متفق عليه. وأما قوله تعالى ولا تقربوهن يطهرن ﴾ [البقرة : ۲۲۲] فإن كان نهياً عن الجماع عيناً فلا يمتنع أن نثبت حرمة أخرى في محل آخر بالسنة، وإياك أن تظن أن هذه من الزيادة على النص بخبر الواحد لأنها تقيد مطلق النص فتكون معارضة له في بعض متناولاته وما أثبتته السنة فيما نحن فيه شرع ما لم يتعرض له النص القرآني فلم يكن من باب الزيادة وإن كان نهياً عما هو أعم من الجماع كان الجماع من أفراد المنهي عنه لتناوله حرمة الاستمتاع بها أعني من الجماع وغيره من الاستمتاعات. ثم يظهر تخصيص بعضها بالحديث المفيد لحل ما سوى ما بين السرة والركبة فيبقى ما بينهما داخلاً في عموم النهي عن قربانه وإن لم يحتج إلى هذا الاعتبار في ثبوت المطلوب لما بينا . كذا في فتح القدير مع بعض اختصار. واعلم أنه كما يحرم عليه الاستمتاع بما بين السرة والركبة يحرم عليها التمكين منه ولم أر لهم صريحاً حكم مباشرتها له، ولقائل أن يمنعه لأنه لما حرم تمكينها من استمتاعه بها حرم فعلها بالأولى، ولقائل أن يجوزه لأن عليه لكونها حائضاً . وهو مفقود في حقه فحل لها الاستمتاع به ولأن غاية مسها لذكره أنه استمتاع بكفها وهو جائز قطعاً.

ثم اختلفوا في تفسير قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله قال بعضهم لا يباح الاستمتاع من النظر ونحوه بما دون السرة إلى الركبة ويباح ما وراءه وقال بعضهم يباح الاستمتاع مع الإزار اهـ. ومع النقل يبطل البحث والله تعالى الموفق
Until he has intercourse with one of them while she is menstruating until he commands her to wear an izar. Agreed upon. As for His statement 'And do not approach them until they are pure' [Al-Baqarah: 222], if it is a prohibition of intercourse specifically, then there is no prevention that we establish another prohibition in another place by the Sunnah. And beware of thinking that this is an addition to the text by a khabar wahid, because it restricts an absolute text, thus it contradicts it in some of its implications. And what the Sunnah has established in our subject is a prescription of what the Quranic text did not address, so it is not from the category of addition. And if it is a prohibition of what is more general than intercourse, then intercourse is among the individuals of the prohibited, because it includes the prohibition of enjoyment with her, meaning from intercourse and other enjoyments. Then the specification of some of them appears by the hadith that indicates the permissibility of what is other than between the navel and the knee, so what is between them remains within the general prohibition of approaching it, even if this consideration is not needed to establish the desired as we have explained. Thus in Fath al-Qadir with some abbreviation. And know that just as it is prohibited for him to enjoy what is between the navel and the knee, it is prohibited for her to enable him to do so. And I have not seen from them an explicit ruling on her touching him. And one may say that it is prohibited because when it is prohibited for her to enable him to enjoy her, her action is even more prohibited. And one may say that it is permissible because the prohibition is on him due to her being menstruating, and that is absent in her case, so it is permissible for her to enjoy him, and because the extent of her touching his penis is an enjoyment with her palm, and that is definitely permissible. Remarks: In some expressions the word 'enjoyment' occurred, which includes looking and touching with desire. In the expression of many, the words 'direct contact' and 'approaching' occurred, and their implication is the prohibition of touching without desire. So between them is generality and specificity in some respects. And what appears is that the prohibition is dependent on direct contact even without desire, unlike looking even with desire. And it is not greater than kissing her face with desire, as is not hidden. And it has been known from her or him. End. And some said: What he said in al-Nahr is good, and apparently it is what the author of al-Bahr intended, as understood from his reasoning for the first statement and the second reasoning for the second statement. He said: (And what appears, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And one may distinguish between them by saying that looking at this specific area with desire is enjoyment of what is not permissible, unlike kissing the face, as is apparent. End. But some excellent scholars objected to him, saying: If he means by his statement 'enjoyment of what is not permissible' that it is enjoyment of a place whose direct contact is not permissible, then it is conceded, but the prohibition of direct contact does not necessitate the prohibition of looking. And if he means that it is enjoyment of a place that it is not permissible to look at, then that is exactly the claim, so it would be a petition of principle. This and the evidence shines upon the claimant of al-Bahr. And the lawgiver only prohibited direct contact, which is the meeting of the two private parts without a barrier. But since the private part has a sanctum, which is what is between the navel and the knee, he also prohibited it out of fear of falling into what might be fallen into by approaching this area. For whoever hovers around the sanctuary is likely to fall into it. Or it is said: The lawgiver is wise, and these places are not free from traces of impurity, so he prohibited proximity for fear of contamination. So looking at these places remains on the original permissibility due to marriage. So its prohibition has no evidence. End. I say: It may be said that looking is from hovering around the sanctuary, and therefore it is prohibited in the case of a non-mahram woman for fear of falling into the prohibited. And it is supported by what is in al-Istihsan from al-Haqaiq, from al-Tuhfa and al-Khaniyya: The man should avoid from the menstruating woman what is under the izar according to the first. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: He should avoid the emblem of blood, meaning intercourse, and he has what is between them. And the legal opinions of their expressions state that it is permissible to enjoy the area above the navel and above it, and below the knee and below it, and the prohibited enjoyment is what is between them. From the expression of some: He enjoys what is above the navel and below the knee, as is not hidden. So it is permissible for him to enjoy other than what was mentioned by intercourse and other than that, even without a barrier, and likewise what is between them with a barrier without intercourse, even if it is stained with blood. And it is not disliked to cook her food, nor to use what she has touched of dough, water, or other things, unless she performed wudu with the intention of worship, as is recommended according to what we have presented, for then it becomes used water. And in al-Walwalji: It is not appropriate to isolate her from her bed, because that resembles the action of the Jews. And in al-Tanjis and others: A woman who menstruates from her anus does not abandon prayer, because this is not menstruation. And it is recommended that she perform ghusl when the blood stops. And if her husband refrains from coming to her, it is more beloved to me due to the appearance of blood from the vagina. End. And we have presented it previously from al-Khulasa. Then they differed concerning the interpretation of the statement of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. Some said: Enjoyment from looking and the like below the navel to the knee is not permissible, and what is beyond it is permissible. And some said: Enjoyment with the izar is permissible. End. And with the transmission, the research is invalid. And Allah is the grantor of success.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the prohibition of sexual relations with a menstruating woman. The author begins by citing the well-known hadith that the Prophet ﷺ would command his wives to wear an izar (a waist-wrapper) when he wanted to be intimate during menstruation, indicating that direct intercourse is prohibited but other forms of intimacy are permissible with a barrier. He then addresses the Qur'anic verse 'And do not approach them until they are pure' (al-Baqarah 222), explaining that whether the verse is taken as a prohibition of intercourse specifically or as a more general prohibition, the Sunnah (in the form of the hadith) specifies that what is between the navel and the knee remains prohibited, while other areas become permissible. The author clarifies that this is not an addition to the Qur'an by a solitary hadith but rather a specification (takhsis) of the general. The passage then discusses an important subsidiary issue: whether the menstruating woman is also prohibited from enabling her husband to enjoy her. The author notes that he has not seen an explicit ruling on her touching him, and presents two possible views: one analogizing from the husband's prohibition to a greater prohibition on her, and another seeing it as permissible for her because the prohibition applies specifically to him due to her state, which is absent in her case. A series of 'tanbihat' (remarks) follow, clarifying terminology. The word 'istimta'' (enjoyment) is broad, including looking and touching with desire. Other texts use 'mubasharah' (direct contact) and 'qurban' (approaching), which imply prohibition of touching even without desire. The author concludes that the correct view is that prohibition is attached to direct contact (mubasharah), even without desire, while looking, even with desire, is not prohibited. He compares it to kissing her face with desire, which is clearly permissible. The passage then records a disagreement between the authors of al-Nahr and al-Bahr. Al-Nahr had distinguished looking at the private area from kissing the face. Some later scholars criticized al-Bahr's view that looking is permissible, arguing that looking at the forbidden area is itself a form of enjoyment. But al-Bahr defends his position by noting that the Lawgiver (the Prophet) only prohibited direct contact (mubasharah), and the prohibition of the area between navel and knee is an extension (hurm) to prevent falling into the prohibited. Since the rationale is prevention, looking does not carry the same risk and remains on the original permissibility due to marriage. The passage ends with additional rulings: it is permissible for the husband to enjoy above the navel and below the knee even without a barrier, and between navel and knee with a barrier. It is not disliked to cook her food or use items she touched unless she performed wudu for worship. A woman who menstruates from the anus (istihadah) does not stop praying. And finally, there is a difference in interpreting Abu Hanifa's statement: some say no enjoyment of looking below navel to knee, others say permissibility with an izar. The author concludes that transmission (naql) resolves the debate. Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi position that the prohibition is limited to direct contact and the area between navel and knee, and that looking is not prohibited. This reflects the Hanafi principle of istihsan and the restriction of prohibitions to what is explicitly established by strong evidence.

Key Terms
الاستمتاع (al-istimta'): Enjoyment, which includes looking and touching with desire.
المباشرة (al-mubasharah): Direct contact, specifically the meeting of the two private parts without a barrier.
القربان (al-qurban): Approaching, used synonymously with mubasharah in some contexts, implying prohibition of proximity to the private parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on a husband looking at his wife's area between navel and knee during her menstruation?
A) It is haram even without desire.
B) It is makruh.
C) It is permissible even with desire.
D) It is permissible only without desire.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the prohibition during menstruation is limited to direct genital contact (mubasharah) and the area between navel and knee; looking and other non-contact forms of intimacy remain permissible.

Prohibition of Quran Recitation for Menstruating Woman and Junub
Ruling on the prohibition of reciting the Quran for a menstruating woman and a person in janabah, with hadith evidence and scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Quran recitation for a menstruating woman and a junub, identify the primary hadith evidence, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its legal classification.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman and a junub are prohibited from reciting the Quran, even less than an ayah—the hadith is explicit and the majority uphold the prohibition.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on Quran recitation for those in a state of major ritual impurity (menstruation and janabah). The Hanafi school, based on prophetic hadith and the practice of the Companions, holds that such recitation is forbidden (haram). However, there is a minority view within the school that considers it merely disliked (makruh). We will trace the evidence, understand the scope of the prohibition, and see how the majority position is established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'qira'at al-Quran' by a menstruating woman or a junub. The ruling according to the majority of Hanafi scholars is haram.
The prohibition applies to any recitation, whether complete verses or partial.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'لا تقرأ الحائض ولا الجنب شيئاً من القرآن' (Do not recite the Quran, the menstruating woman nor the junub, anything). Narrated by al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah, authenticated by al-Nawawi.
This hadith is ahad but has been considered strong by many muhaddithun.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The negation 'لا تقرأ' is a prohibition (nahy) in Arabic. The hadith does not differentiate between little or much, so the prohibition covers any amount of recitation.
Hanafi usul holds that a prohibition indicates haram unless specified otherwise.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafi scholars consider it makruh, citing the general hadith of Aisha (the Prophet remembering Allah in all states) and the hadith of Ali specifically excluding only janabah.
This intra-Hanafi dispute arises from different understandings of how to reconcile the evidence.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The majority of mashayikh (al-Karkhi, al-Hidayah, al-Bada'i) reject the weaker view, affirming that the specific prohibitions specify the general hadith of Aisha. The mufta bihi is haram.
Al-Bahr al-Raiq attributes the majority view to the generality of the mashayikh and authenticates it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقراءة القرآن) أي يمنع الحيض قراءة القرآن وكذا الجنابة لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «لا تقرأ الحائض ولا الجنب شيئاً من القرآن (۱) رواه الترمذي وابن ماجه وحسنه المنذري وصححه النووي وقال : إنه يقرأ بالرفع على النفي وهو محمول على النهي كيلا يلزم الخلف في الوعد، وبكسر الهمزة لالتقاء الساكنين على النهي وهما صحيحان وعن علي رضي الله عنه قال : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقرئنا القرآن على كل حال ما لم يكن جنباً . (۲) رواه أبو داود والترمذي وقال : إنه حسن صحيح. ثم كل من الحديثين يصلح مخصصاً لحديث مسلم عن عائشة أنه كان يذكر الله على كل أحيانه بعد القول بتناول الذكر قراءة القرآن وبقولنا قال أكثر أهل العلم الصحابة والتابعين كما حكاه الترمذي في جامعه وشمل إطلاقه الآية وما دونها وهو قول من الكرخي وصححه صاحب الهداية في التنجيس، وقاضي خان في شرح الجامع الصغير، والولوالجي في فتاواه ومشى عليه المصنف في المستصفى، وقواه في الكافي، ونسبه صاحب البدائع إلى عامة المشايخ وصححه معللاً بأن الأحاديث لم تفصل بين القليل والكثير لكن ذكر أن القراءة مكروهة، وفي كثير من الكتب أنها حرام، وفي رواية الطحاوي يباح لهما ما دون ذلك
His statement: (And recitation of the Quran) i.e., menstruation prevents the recitation of the Quran, and likewise janabah, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Do not recite the Quran, the menstruating woman nor the junub, anything from the Quran.' Narrated by al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah; al-Mundhiri considered it hasan and al-Nawawi authenticated it. And he said: It is read with the dammah on the nun to indicate negation, and it is considered as a prohibition, so that there is no contradiction in the promise, and with the kasra of the hamza due to the meeting of two sukoon on the prohibition, and both are correct. And from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, would teach us the Quran in every state as long as he was not junub. Narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi, who said it is hasan sahih. Then each of the two hadiths is suitable as a specifier for the hadith of Muslim from Aisha that he would remember Allah in all his states, after saying that remembrance includes recitation of the Quran. And according to our statement, most of the scholars among the Companions and Followers, as al-Tirmidhi narrated in his Jami', and his general application includes the verse and less than that, and this is the statement of al-Karkhi. And the author of al-Hidayah authenticated it in al-Tanjis, and Qadi Khan in his Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, and al-Walwalji in his Fatawa, and the author of al-Mustasfa followed it, and it was supported in al-Kafi, and the author of al-Bada'i attributed it to the generality of the mashayikh and authenticated it, giving as reason that the hadiths did not differentiate between little and much. But he mentioned that recitation is makruh, and in many books it is haram. And in the narration of al-Tahawi, it is permissible for both to recite less than that.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the ruling on a menstruating woman and a person in janabah (post-sexual impurity) reciting the Quran. The author begins by clarifying that menstruation and janabah prevent recitation of the Quran. The primary evidence is the hadith 'Do not recite the Quran, the menstruating woman nor the junub, anything from the Quran' narrated by al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah. There is also a hadith from Ali that the Prophet would teach them the Quran in every state except janabah. These hadiths serve to specify the general hadith of Aisha that the Prophet remembered Allah in all states, indicating that remembrance includes Quran recitation, but the exceptions are menstruation and janabah. The passage then discusses the scope of the prohibition. According to most Hanafi scholars (al-Karkhi, al-Hidayah, Qadi Khan, etc.), the prohibition applies to a complete ayah and less than an ayah, because the hadith does not distinguish between little and much. However, some later scholars consider it merely makruh (disliked) rather than haram. The author notes that in many books it is considered haram. There is also a narration from al-Tahawi that permits less than an ayah. Thus, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that it is haram for a menstruating woman and a junub to recite the Quran, even less than an ayah, but they may engage in dhikr (remembrance) that is not Quranic recitation.

Key Terms
قراءة القرآن (qira'at al-Quran): Recitation of the Quran, which is prohibited for a menstruating woman and a junub.
الجنابة (al-janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, which prevents recitation of Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, what is the ruling on a menstruating woman reciting less than an ayah of the Quran?
A) Permissible (mubah)
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Forbidden (haram)
D) Recommended (mustahabb)

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'لا تقرأ الحائض ولا الجنب شيئاً من القرآن' explicitly prohibits recitation, and the majority Hanafi position is that it is haram for any amount, including less than an ayah.

Zones of Physical Intimacy During Menstruation
States permissible and impermissible acts with menstruating wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on permissible and impermissible physical intimacy with a menstruating wife, distinguishing between zones with and without barrier.

Core Idea
Intimacy is not entirely forbidden; above navel and below knee are allowed even without barrier.

A common misconception is that all physical contact with a menstruating wife is haram. In this lesson, we examine a passage from later Hanafi fatwa collections that clarifies the precise zones of permissibility. Understanding these rulings helps maintain marital bonds while upholding the rules of purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the zones of permissibility
Read the passage to find which body areas are explicitly allowed without barrier (above navel and below knee) and which are prohibited without barrier (between navel and knee).
The phrase 'بما بينهما' likely refers to the area between navel and knee.
2
Apply the barrier rule
Note that intimacy between navel and knee becomes permissible with a barrier (ha'il) except for intercourse, which is absolutely prohibited.
Intercourse is never allowed during hayd.
3
Extract subsidiary rulings
List the additional rulings: cooking her food is not disliked, using water/dough she touched is valid unless she performed wudu (then water becomes musta'mal), withdrawing from her bed is disliked, and anal bleeding is not hayd.
These details help in everyday practice.
4
Recognise the fatwa authority
The passage cites al-Walwaliji and al-Tanjis, indicating these are from later authoritative fatwa works (fatawa mutakhkhirin), not from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.
Authority weight is 'fatawa_mutakhkhirin'.

كتاب الطهارة - باب الحيض بينهما وهي فتاوى عباراتهم أنه يجوز الاستمتاع بالسرة وما فوقها وبالركبة وما تحتها، والمحرم الاستمتاع بما [أحسن؟] من عبارة بعضهم يستمتع بما فوق السرة وما تحت الركبة كما لا يخفى، فيجوز له الاستمتاع فيما عدا ما ذكر بوطء وغيره ولو بلا حائل وكذا بما بينهما بحائل بغير الوطء ولو تلطخ دماً . ولا يكره طبخها ولا استعمال ما مسته من عجين أو ماء أو غيرهما إلا إذا توضأت بقصد القربة كما هو المستحب على ما قدمناه فإنه يصير مستعملاً. وفي الولوالجي : ولا ينبغي أن يعزل عن فراشها لأن ذلك يشبه فعل اليهود. وفي التنجيس وغيره: امرأة تحيض من دبرها لا تدع الصلاة لأن هذا ليس بحيض، ويستحب أن تغتسل عند انقطاع الدم. وإن أمسك زوجها عن الاتيان كان أحب إلي لمكان الصورة وهو الدم من الفرج اهـ. وقد قدمناه الخلاصة.
Book of Purity - Chapter of Menstruation. Between them are fatwas stating that it is permissible to enjoy the navel and above it, and the knee and below it. What is prohibited is enjoyment of what is [between?] according to the expression of some: one enjoys what is above the navel and below the knee, as is not hidden. So it is permissible for him to enjoy other than what was mentioned by intercourse or otherwise even without a barrier, and likewise what is between them with a barrier without intercourse even if smeared with blood. And it is not disliked to cook her food nor to use what she has touched of dough or water or other things, except if she performed wudu with the intention of drawing near (as is recommended as we mentioned), then it becomes used water. In al-Walwaliji: It is not appropriate to withdraw from her bed because that resembles the act of the Jews. In al-Tanjis and others: A woman who menstruates from her anus should not leave prayer because this is not menstruation, and it is recommended that she perform ghusl when the blood ceases. If her husband refrains from coming to her, that is more beloved to me because of the appearance of blood from the vagina. End. We have already presented it in al-Khulasah.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from various Hanafi fatwa collections regarding a menstruating woman. It begins by clarifying the permissible areas of physical intimacy: the area above the navel and below the knee is allowed even without a barrier, but the area between the navel and the knee (i.e., the belly and thighs) requires a barrier and intercourse is absolutely prohibited. Then it addresses practical issues: cooking her food is not disliked, nor is using water or dough she touched, unless she performed wudu with devotional intent—then that water becomes 'used' (musta'mal). It also mentions the disliked nature of withdrawing from her bed, as it resembles Jewish practice. A special case is a woman who bleeds from the anus: this is not considered menstrual blood (hayd), so she must continue praying and perform ghusl when the bleeding stops. The husband refraining from intercourse is preferred due to the appearance of blood from the vagina, even though it is not hayd. All these rulings are from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الاستمتاع (al-istimta'): Physical enjoyment or intimacy, not limited to intercourse, including touching and fondling.
حائل (ha'il): A barrier or cover, such as clothing, that prevents direct skin contact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Between the navel and the knee
C) The entire body
D) Only the genital area

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, physical intimacy with a menstruating wife is not entirely prohibited; the area above the navel and below the knee is permissible without barrier, while the area in between requires a barrier and intercourse is completely prohibited.

Quran Recitation for Junub and Menstruating Woman: The Dispute over Less than a Verse
Intra-Hanafi dispute over less than a verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on reciting less than a verse for a junub, explain the evidence for each view, and evaluate the tarjih.

Core Idea
A junub cannot recite even a single letter of Quran with the intention of recitation — the indefinite 'shay'an' makes it general.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi disagreement: Does the prohibition of Quran recitation for a menstruating woman or junub include less than a full verse? The source text from Bahr al-Ra'iq presents both sides, weighing the literal generality of the hadith against the argument that less than a verse lacks the full Quranic composition. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply usul principles like 'the indefinite in negation indicates generality' and to appreciate how later Hanafi jurists resolved conflicting riwayat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the majority view (prohibition for any amount) and the minority view (permissible for less than a verse). Note the key scholars on each side.
The minority includes al-Tahawi, Khulasah, and Fakhr al-Islam.
2
Examine the primary dalil
Read the hadith 'لا تقرأ الحائض ولا الجنب شيئاً من القرآن' and note its chain (Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah). Identify the key word 'shay'an'.
The hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut and al-dalalah.
3
Analyze the linguistic principle
Apply the Hanafi usul rule: al-nakirah fi siyaq al-nafi tu'amm (an indefinite in a negative context indicates generality). Show how this makes 'shay'an' include any amount, even less than a verse.
This is the core of the majority's proof.
4
Understand the opposing argument
The minority holds that the prohibition applies only to a full verse because the miraculous composition (nazm) is only complete in a full ayah. Less than a verse lacks this and is similar to ordinary speech.
They also note that prayer with less than a verse is invalid, implying it is not treated as Quran.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author prefers prohibition, citing that the hadith does not differentiate and that reasoning (ta'lil) cannot override the text. Precaution (ihtiyat) also supports prohibition. Also the narration from Ali 'not even a single letter' reinforces.
The mufta bihi is the majority prohibition for any amount intended as tilawah.

أو افتتاح أمر لا يمنع في أصح الروايات. وفي التسمية اتفاق أنه لا يمنع إذا كان على قصد الثناء أو افتتاح أمر . كذا في الخلاصة. وفي العيون لأبي الليث : ولو أنه قرأ الفاتحة على سبيل الدعاء أو شيئاً من حنيفة رحمه الله .اهـ. وبه يظهر ما في بحث المؤلف قوله: (وهو الظاهر في مثل الفاتحة الخ) قال في النهر : لقائل أن يقول كونه قرآناً في الأصل لا يمنع من إخراجه عن القرآنية بالقصد بالنسبة إلى قصد الثناء فالتلازم منفك، نعم ظاهر تقييد صاحب العيون بالآيات التي فيها معنى الدعاء يفهم أن ما ليس كذلك كسورة أبي لهب لا يؤثر قصد القرآنية في حله لكني لم أر التصريح به في كلامهم .اهـ. قلت : المفهوم معتبر ما لم يصرح بخلافه . قوله : (وكيف لا وهو معجز الخ) قال الشيخ إسمعيل : فيه بحث لأنه إذا لم يرد بها القرآن فات ما بها من المزايا التي يعجز عن الإتيان بها جميع المخلوقات إذ المعتبر فيها القصد إما تفصيلاً وذلك من البليغ، أو إجمالاً وذلك بحكاية
His statement: (and recitation of the Quran) means menstruation prevents the recitation of the Quran, and likewise janabah, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything of the Quran.' Narrated by Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah; al-Mundhiri declared it hasan, and al-Nawawi sahih. He said: it is read with a damma on the negative, and it is interpreted as a prohibition to avoid a breach of promise, and with a kasra on the hamza due to meeting two sukoons, indicating prohibition, and both are correct. And from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: 'The Messenger of Allah would teach us the Quran in every state except when he was junub.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi, who said it is hasan sahih. Then each of the two hadiths is suitable to specify the hadith of Muslim from Aisha that he would remember Allah in all his states, after the statement that dhikr includes Quran recitation. And our saying: most of the scholars of the Companions and Followers said this, as related by Tirmidhi in his Jami'. And his absolute statement includes the verse and less than it. This is the saying of al-Karkhi, and it was declared sahih by the author of al-Hidayah in al-Tanjis, and Qadi Khan in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, and al-Walwaliji in his Fatawa; the author (al-Kashghari) followed it in al-Mustasfa, and strengthened it in al-Kafi; the author of al-Bada'i' attributed it to the generality of the mashayikh and declared it sahih, reasoning that the hadiths did not differentiate between a little and much. However, he mentioned that recitation is makruh, and in many books it is haram. In the narration of al-Tahawi, it is permissible for them to recite less than a verse, and the author of al-Khulasah declared it sahih in the eleventh chapter on recitation; Fakhr al-Islam followed it in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, and al-Zahidi attributed it to the majority; its proponent from al-Muhit reasoned that the wording and meaning are limited to less than a verse, and similar occurs in people's conversations and speech, so the suspicion of it not being Quran has taken hold, and for this reason prayer with it is not permissible. End. The gist is that the sahih view has differed concerning less than a verse. What is appropriate is to give preponderance to the view of prohibition, because you know that the hadiths did not differentiate, and reasoning in opposition to the text is rejected because 'anything' as in al-Kafi is an indefinite noun in the context of negation, so it is general, and less than a verse is Quran, so it is prohibited like a verse. Moreover, it has also been answered by taking precaution in both matters, which is impermissibility in prayer and prohibition for the junub and those like him. This is supported by what al-Daraqutni narrated from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: 'Recite the Quran as long as none of you is junub; if he is, then not a single letter.' Then he said: 'This is sahih from Ali.' All this applies if he recites with the intention that it is Quran. As for if he recites with the intention of praise or starting an affair, it is not prohibited in the soundest narrations. Concerning the Basmalah, there is agreement that it is not prohibited if it is for praise or beginning an affair. Thus in al-Khulasah. In al-Uyun by Abu al-Layth: If one recites al-Fatihah as a supplication or some verses that contain the meaning of supplication, and does not intend recitation (as Quran), there is no harm. End. Al-Halwani chose it. It is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that it is the chosen view, but al-Hinduwani said: 'I do not give fatwa with this, even if it is narrated from Abu Hanifa.' End. And it (prohibition) is apparent in the case of al-Fatihah because what is permissible is only that which is not Quran, but this is literally and legally Quran; it is miraculous, by which challenge is made at the time of opposition, and the inability to produce its like is certain. Changing the prescribed (ruling) in such a case by mere intention is rejected upon its doer, unlike saying 'al-hamdu lillah' with the intention of praise, because the Quranic specificity in it is not inseparable; otherwise, the permissibility of uttering anything that contains letters found in the Quran would be negated, and that is not the case by consensus. Unlike al-Fatihah, because the Quranic specificity is certainly inseparable, and it is not within the speaker's ability to remove it from him, given its specific composition as prescribed. By this, what is in al-Khulasah regarding the non-prohibition of that which occurs in Arabic words is exposed... etc. The full text continues with a response about Hinduwani's reluctance to give fatwa despite the narration from Abu Hanifa, and further discussion on the miraculous nature.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the prohibition of Quran recitation for a menstruating woman or a person in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah). It begins by citing the primary proof: the hadith 'Do not recite anything of the Quran' and the hadith of Ali that the Prophet taught Quran in all states except janabah. The main point of disagreement is whether it is permissible to recite less than a full verse. The majority of Hanafi scholars, including al-Karkhi, the author of al-Hidayah, Qadi Khan, and others, hold that it is prohibited for a junub or menstruating woman to recite even less than a verse, because the hadith uses 'anything' (shay'an) which is general and includes small amounts. The minority, represented by al-Tahawi, al-Khulasah, and Fakhr al-Islam, hold it permissible for less than a verse, arguing that the prohibition is only for full verses because less than a verse may not be considered complete Quran. The text then discusses the issue of intention: if one recites Quranic phrases like the Fatihah with the intention of supplication (du'a) or praise (thana'), then it is not prohibited according to many Hanafi scholars, because the intention changes the nature. However, al-Hinduwani refused to give fatwa on this even though it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, fearing that the common person might misinterpret and think it is always allowed. The later scholars from al-Nahr and Shaykh Isma'il comment that the miraculous nature of the Quran is inherent in the words themselves regardless of intention, so reciting al-Fatihah as Quran even for supplication still involves the miraculous challenge. Ultimately, the text clarifies that for phrases like 'al-hamdu lillah' which are not uniquely Quranic, intention matters; but for uniquely Quranic compositions like al-Fatihah, intention alone cannot remove the Quranic specificity. The relied-upon position is that reciting any amount of Quran with the intention of reciting Quran is prohibited for the junub and menstruating woman, but if one intends only praise or supplication, it is permissible for phrases that are not exclusively Quranic (like 'al-hamdu lillah'), while for exclusive Quranic compositions like al-Fatihah, there is caution.

Key Terms
شيئاً (shay'an): Indefinite noun in a negative context, indicating generality (عموم) according to Hanafi usul.
القراءة (al-qira'ah): Recitation of the Quran with the intention of tilawah (reciting as divine revelation).
الجنب (al-junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, from sexual intercourse or wet dream.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi position prohibits a junub or menstruating woman from reciting any part of the Quran, even a single letter, when intended as tilawah.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Junub Reciting Quranic Words with Intention of Praise or Supplication
General ruling on junub reciting Quranic words with intention of praise or supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a junub reciting Quranic phrases with the intention of thana' or du'a, and distinguish this from recitation as Quran.

Core Idea
Intention transforms prohibited recitation into permissible praise or supplication.

For a person in a state of major impurity (janabah), reciting the Quran is prohibited. However, the Hanafi school recognizes that the same words can serve other purposes. If the junub intends to praise Allah (thana') or to supplicate (du'a), rather than to recite the Quran, the ruling changes to permissibility. This lesson examines the general principle and the internal scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is reciting words that are part of the Quran. For a junub, the default ruling is haram if done as tilawah.
2
Introduce the exception based on intention
If the reciter intends thana' (praise) or du'a (supplication), and does not intend it as Quranic recitation, the ruling becomes mubah (permissible) according to the most authentic narrations.
This includes saying 'Bismillah' before an action or reciting verses with supplicatory meaning.
3
Examine the evidence and authority
The text cites narrations from Abu Hanifa, as recorded in al-Khulasa and al-Uyun by Abu al-Layth. Al-Halwani chose this view. The author notes it is the chosen view in Ghayat al-Bayan.
The evidence is based on the principle that actions are judged by intentions.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Al-Hindwani refrained from giving fatwa on this permissibility, despite it being narrated from Abu Hanifa, due to fear of misuse or misunderstanding.
This disagreement shows that even within the school, caution may be exercised.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon view for fatwa is that reciting with intention of thana' or du'a is permissible for general phrases and supplication verses, though there is caution regarding inherently Quranic passages like al-Fatihah.
This will be explored further in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لأبي الليث : ولو أنه قرأ الفاتحة على سبيل الدعاء أو شيئاً من الآيات التي فيها معنى الدعاء ولم يرد به القراءة فلا بأس به .اهـ. واختاره الحلواني. وذكر في غاية البيان أنه المختار لكن قال الهندواني: لا أفتي بهذا وإن روي عن أبي حنيفة اهـ. وهو الظاهر في مثل الفاتحة فإن المباح إنما هو ليس بقرآن وهذا قرآن حقيقة وحكماً لفظاً وهو معجز يقع به التحدي عند المعارضة والعجز عن الإتيان بمثله مقطوع به وتغيير المشروع في مثله بالقصد المجرد مردود على فاعله بخلاف نحو الحمد لله» بنية الثناء لأن الخصوصية القرآنية فيه غير لازمة وإلا لانتفى جواز التلفظ بشيء من لاشتمالها على الحروف الواقعة في القرآن وليس الأمر كذلك إجماعاً بخلاف نحو الفاتحة فإن الخصوصية القرآنية لازمة قطعاً وليس في قدرة المتكلم إسقاطها عنه مع ما هو عليه من النظم الخاص كما هو في المفروض، وقد انكشف بهذا ما في الخلاصة من عدم حرمة ما يجري على وكيف لا ومعنى، الكلمات العربية من رد هذه الرواية بل قال ذلك لما يتبادر إلى ذهن من يسمعه من الجنب من غير اطلاع على نية قائله جوازه منه، وكم من قول صحيح لا يفتى به خوفاً . من محذور آخر ولم يقل لا أعمل به، كيف وهو مروي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله .اهـ. وبه يظهر ما في بحث المؤلف قوله: (وهو الظاهر في مثل الفاتحة الخ) قال في النهر : لقائل أن يقول كونه قرآناً في الأصل لا يمنع من إخراجه عن القرآنية بالقصد بالنسبة إلى قصد الثناء فالتلازم منفك، نعم ظاهر تقييد صاحب العيون بالآيات التي فيها معنى الدعاء يفهم أن ما ليس كذلك كسورة أبي لهب لا يؤثر قصد القرآنية في حله لكني لم أر التصريح به في كلامهم .اهـ. قلت : المفهوم معتبر ما لم يصرح بخلافه . قوله : (وكيف لا وهو معجز الخ) قال الشيخ إسمعيل : فيه بحث لأنه إذا لم يرد بها القرآن فات ما بها من المزايا التي يعجز عن الإتيان بها جميع المخلوقات إذ المعتبر فيها القصد إما تفصيلاً وذلك من البليغ، أو إجمالاً وذلك بحكاية اللسان عند الكلام من آية قصيرة من نحو ثم نظر [المدثر : ٢١] أو لم يولد [الإخلاص : ٣]. ثم اعلم أنهم قالوا هنا وفي باب ما يفسد الصلاة أن القرآن يتغير بعزيمته فأورد الإمام الخاصي كما نقله عنه السراج الهندي في التوشيح بأن العزيمة لو كانت مغيرة للقراءة لكان ينبغي أنه إذا قرأ الفاتحة في الأوليين بنية الدعاء لا تكون مجزئة وقد نصوا على أنها مجزئة . وأجاب بأنها إذا كانت في محلها لا تتغير بالعزيمة حتى لو لم يقرأ في الأوليين فقرأ في الأخريين بنية الدعاء لا يجزئه .اهـ. والمنقول في التنجيس أنه قرأ في الصلاة فاتحة الكتاب على قصد الثناء جازت صلاته لأنه وجدت القراءة في محلها فلا يتغير حكمها بقصد اهـ. ولم يقيد بالأوليين، ولا شك أن الأخريين محل القراءة المفروضة فإن القراءة فرض في ركعتين غير عين وإن كان تعيينها في الأوليين واجباً. وذكر في القنية خلافاً فيما إذا قرأ الفاتحة على قصد الدعاء فرقم لشرح شمس الأئمة الحلواني أنها لا تنوب عن القراءة .اهـ. وأما الأذكار فالمنقول إباحتها مطلقاً ويدخل فيها اللهم اهدنا إلى آخره . وأما اللهم إنا نستعينك إلى آخره» الذي دعاء القنوت عندنا فالظاهر من المذهب أنه لا يكره لهما وعليه الفتوى. كذا في الفتاوى هو الظهيرية وغيرها. وعن محمد يكره لشبهة كونه قرآناً لاختلاف الصحابة في كونه قرآناً فلا يقرأه احتياطاً. قلنا: حصل الإجماع القطعي اليقيني على أنه ليس بقرآن، ومعه توجب الاحتياط المذكور. نعم المذكور في الهداية وغيرها في باب الأذان استحباب الوضوء لذكر الله تعالى وترك المستحب لا يوجب الكراهة
All this is when he recites with the intention that it is Quran. But if he recites it with the intention of praise or opening a matter, it is not prevented in the most authentic narrations. Regarding Bismillah, there is agreement that it is not prevented if it is with the intention of praise or opening a matter. Thus in al-Khulasa. In al-Uyun by Abu al-Layth: If he recites al-Fatihah by way of supplication or some verses that have the meaning of supplication, not intending recitation, then there is no harm. Al-Halwani chose this. It is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that it is the chosen view, but al-Hindwani said: I do not give fatwa on this even if it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. And it is apparent in the case of al-Fatihah that what is permissible is not Quran, and this is Quran in reality and in legal ruling, in wording, and it is miraculous, with which challenge occurs in opposition, and the inability to produce the like of it is certain. Changing the prescribed ruling in such a case by mere intention is rejected from its doer, unlike 'al-hamdu lillah' with intention of praise, because the quality of being Quranic is not inherent in it; otherwise, the permissibility of uttering anything that includes letters occurring in the Quran would be negated, and that is not the case by consensus. Unlike al-Fatihah, the quality of being Quranic is inherent absolutely, and it is not in the power of the speaker to remove it from him given what it is of specific composition as in the prescribed. Thus is uncovered what is in al-Khulasa regarding the non-prohibition of what occurs... How not? And the meaning of the Arabic words from rejecting this narration... rather he said that because of what comes to the mind of the one who hears it from a junub without knowledge of the intention of the speaker, that it is permissible for him. How many a correct statement is not given fatwa with due to fear of another harm? And he did not say I do not act upon it, even though it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. And by this appears the author's discussion: 'It is apparent in the case of al-Fatihah...' He said in al-Nahr: One could say that being Quran in origin does not prevent removing it from Quranicness by intention regarding the intention of praise, so the correlation is broken. Yes, the apparent restriction of the author of al-Uyun to verses that contain the meaning of supplication implies that what is not like that, such as Surat Abi Lahab, does not affect the intention of Quranicness in its permissibility, but I have not seen explicit mention of it in their statements. I say: The understood is considered as long as there is no explicit statement contrary. His statement: 'How not, and it is miraculous...' Shaykh Isma'il said: There is discussion in it because if he did not intend the Quran by it, the merits by which all creatures are incapable of producing the like are lost, because what matters is the intention, either in detail (which is for the eloquent) or in summary (which is by repetition of the tongue when speaking of a short verse like 'Then he looked' [74:21] or 'He was not born' [112:3]. Then know that they said here and in the chapter on what invalidates prayer that the Quran changes by intention. Imam al-Khassi, as narrated from him by al-Siraj al-Hindi in al-Tawshih, argued: If intention (azima) were to change the recitation, it would imply that if he recited al-Fatihah in the first two rak'ahs with intention of supplication, it would not be sufficient, but they have stated that it is sufficient. He answered: If it is in its proper place, it does not change by intention; even if he did not recite in the first two rak'ahs and recited in the last two rak'ahs with intention of supplication, it does not suffice him. The transmitted view in al-Tanjis is that if he recited Fatihah in prayer with intention of praise, his prayer is valid because recitation occurred in its proper place, so its ruling does not change by intention. And it was not restricted to the first two rak'ahs. There is no doubt that the last two rak'ahs are the place for prescribed recitation, for recitation is obligatory in two rak'ahs unspecified, although specifying them in the first two is wajib. It is mentioned in al-Qunyah a difference when one recites al-Fatihah with intention of supplication; [the text says] 'for the explanation of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani that it does not suffice for recitation.' As for adhkar, the transmitted view is that it is permissible absolutely, and this includes 'Allahumma ihdina...' etc. As for 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka...' the supplication of qunut in our school, the apparent view in the madhhab is that it is not disliked for them (junub/ha'id), and fatwa is given accordingly. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah and others. From Muhammad, it is disliked due to a suspicion that it is Quran, because of the Companions' difference over it being Quran, so he does not recite it as a precaution. We say: Definitive consensus (ijma' qat'i) has been established that it is not Quran, and with this, the mentioned precaution becomes obligatory. Yes, it is mentioned in al-Hidayah and others in the chapter on adhan that it is recommended to perform wudu for remembrance of Allah, and leaving a recommended act does not entail dislike (karahah).
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of intention when a person in a state of janabah recites words that are Quranic. The key ruling is that if the junub recites Quranic phrases with the intention of praise (thana') or supplication (du'a), rather than recitation as Quran (tilawah), it is permissible in the most authentic narrations of the Hanafi school. This includes saying 'Bismillah' before an action, reciting al-Fatihah as a supplication, or any verse that has the meaning of supplication. However, there is internal dispute: al-Hindwani refrained from giving fatwa on this permissibility though it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. The author then discusses the specific case of al-Fatihah, arguing that it is inherently Quranic and cannot be stripped of its Quranic nature by mere intention, unlike general phrases like 'al-hamdu lillah'. The passage then shifts to the issue of reciting Quranic words in prayer; whether reciting al-Fatihah with intention of supplication fulfills the obligatory recitation. Different views are presented: some say it does not suffice, others say it does if it is in its proper place (first two rak'ahs). The discussion extends to the du'a of qunut ('Allahumma inna nasta'inuka'), noting that the relied-upon view is that it is not disliked for a junub to recite it, because it has been established by consensus that it is not Quran, only a supplication. The overall message is that intention matters greatly, but the inherently Quranic nature of certain texts imposes limitations.

Key Terms
ثناء (thana'): Praise; reciting Quranic words with the intention of praising Allah, not as a recitation of the Quran.
دعاء (du'a): Supplication; invoking Allah using Quranic words without intending them as recitation.
عزيمة (azima): Here it means the firm intention or resolve to read as Quran or as du'a; in the context of prayer, it refers to the intention that changes the nature of recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most authentic Hanafi narrations, which of the following is permissible for a junub?
A) Reciting the entire Quran with the intention of receiving reward
B) Saying 'Bismillah' before eating with the intention of praise
C) Reciting al-Fatihah with the intention of du'a, without the intention of recitation
D) Reciting any verse with the intention of teaching others the Quran

Key Takeaway
Intention can transform prohibited recitation into permissible praise or supplication for a junub, but the inherent Quranic nature of certain texts imposes limitations.

Taharah — Purification
advanced
10 min
The Special Case of al-Fatihah: Inherent Quranic Nature and the Limits of Intention
Detailed analysis of the ruling on reciting al-Fatihah with du'a intention, focusing on its inherent Quranic nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school restricts the permissibility of du'a intention for al-Fatihah, and apply this distinction to prayer and other contexts.

Core Idea
Al-Fatihah's miraculous composition makes its Quranic nature inseparable, so intention cannot override it.

The previous lesson established that a junub can recite Quranic words with praise or supplication intention. However, al-Fatihah presents a unique challenge. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq argues that al-Fatihah is inherently miraculous (mu'jiz), and its specific composition cannot be stripped of its Quranic character by mere intention. This lesson explores that argument, the internal dispute within the Hanafi school, and its implications for prayer.

Guided Path
1
State the contested issue
Whether al-Fatihah can be recited by a junub with intention of du'a, and whether it fulfills the required recitation in prayer.
2
Present the Hanafi position of the author
Al-Fatihah is inherently Quranic; its miraculous nature is inseparable. Therefore, reciting it even with du'a intention is still recitation of Quran and haram for a junub.
The author calls this 'the apparent view'.
3
Present the opposing view within the school
Al-Halwani and Abu al-Layth allow it based on the general principle of intention. They see no difference between al-Fatihah and other supplicatory verses.
This view is supported by the basic rule that actions are by intentions.
4
Analyze the author's proof
The Quranic quality of al-Fatihah is not just in its words but in its composition, which is miraculous. Removing it from Quranicness by intention is not possible. The author contrasts al-Fatihah with phrases like 'al-hamdu lillah' which lack this inherent quality.
He argues that if it were possible, then any phrase with letters from the Quran would be permissible, which is not the case.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In prayer: reciting al-Fatihah with du'a intention in the last two rak'ahs may not fulfill the obligatory recitation, whereas reciting it in the first two rak'ahs (its proper place) does not change the ruling because it is in the proper context.
This leads to the distinction that in its proper place, intention does not negate the recitation's validity.

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of intention when a person in a state of janabah recites words that are Quranic. The key ruling is that if the junub recites Quranic phrases with the intention of praise (thana') or supplication (du'a), rather than recitation as Quran (tilawah), it is permissible in the most authentic narrations of the Hanafi school. This includes saying 'Bismillah' before an action, reciting al-Fatihah as a supplication, or any verse that has the meaning of supplication. However, there is internal dispute: al-Hindwani refrained from giving fatwa on this permissibility though it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. The author then discusses the specific case of al-Fatihah, arguing that it is inherently Quranic and cannot be stripped of its Quranic nature by mere intention, unlike general phrases like 'al-hamdu lillah'. The passage then shifts to the issue of reciting Quranic words in prayer; whether reciting al-Fatihah with intention of supplication fulfills the obligatory recitation. Different views are presented: some say it does not suffice, others say it does if it is in its proper place (first two rak'ahs). The discussion extends to the du'a of qunut ('Allahumma inna nasta'inuka'), noting that the relied-upon view is that it is not disliked for a junub to recite it, because it has been established by consensus that it is not Quran, only a supplication. The overall message is that intention matters greatly, but the inherently Quranic nature of certain texts imposes limitations.

Key Terms
ثناء (thana'): Praise; reciting Quranic words with the intention of praising Allah, not as a recitation of the Quran.
دعاء (du'a): Supplication; invoking Allah using Quranic words without intending them as recitation.
عزيمة (azima): Here it means the firm intention or resolve to read as Quran or as du'a; in the context of prayer, it refers to the intention that changes the nature of recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, why is reciting al-Fatihah with du'a intention prohibited for a junub?
A) Because al-Fatihah is too long
B) Because its composition is miraculous and inherently Quranic
C) Because it is agreed upon by all scholars
D) Because it contains the name of Allah

Key Takeaway
Al-Fatihah's inherent miraculous nature restricts the effect of intention; for a junub, reciting it even as du'a remains prohibited in the cautious view, and in prayer the context matters.

Ruling on a Menstruating Woman Reciting Other Scriptures
Clarification of the ruling on haid reading Torah, Gospel, and Psalms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menstruating woman or junub reading the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms, and explain the evidence and the preferred view.

Core Idea
Even if partly altered, these scriptures still contain Allah's speech; therefore, a ha'id or junub should avoid reading them out of reverence.

This lesson addresses a nuance in taharah: the respect due to divine revelations beyond the Quran. While the Quran is forbidden for a ha'id or junub to touch or recite, what about the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms? The Hanafi school holds that these scriptures, despite alterations, remain sacred and should be avoided by those in a state of major impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
We are examining the act of a menstruating woman or a person in a state of janabah reading the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms. The ruling in the Hanafi school is that it is disliked (makruh tanzih).
Note that the ruling is not haram, but it is strongly discouraged.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites the hadith 'Leave what makes you doubt for what does not make you doubt' and the principle that when the prohibited and permissible conflict, the prohibited prevails. The basis is the reverence for divine speech.
The hadith is a general principle applicable here.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
These scriptures are originally the speech of Allah (kalam Allah). Even though parts have been altered, most remains unchanged. Since we cannot distinguish, caution (ihtiyat) dictates avoiding them when in a state of impurity.
The principle of 'al-ghalib' (the majority) is considered: most of the text is divine.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Shafi'i scholars permit using these scriptures for istinja (cleansing after toilet) and, by extension, reading them. They argue the texts are abrogated and altered.
This is a significant practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The later Hanafi authorities (Ibrahim al-Halabi, al-Zayla'i) prefer karahah based on the hadith and the principle that abrogation does not negate the divine origin. The mufta bihi is karahah.
The Shafi'i view is considered reckless (mujazafah) by Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In al-Khulasa: It is not appropriate for a menstruating woman; no doubt in his statement, and both are negated then, as is not hidden, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). And if 'Hudham' says, how is it said that it is rejected? His statement: (And there is no doubt that the last two [rak'ahs]...). He said in al-Nahr: I say: What al-Khassi said is based on assigning the first two rak'ahs for obligation, which is a statement of our companions as will come, and what is in al-Tanjis is based on its absence; then they conflict. His statement: (Leaving a recommended act does not necessitate dislike). He opposed it in al-Nahr: because leaving it is contrary to the better, which is the source of tanzih; so it is not that it does not necessitate dislike absolutely—this is prevented. I say: And in it is discussion that will come up in the disliked acts of prayer, Allah willing, before the section. His statement: (And in al-Khulasa 'it is not appropriate') the scholar Ibrahim al-Halabi said: The statement of the author of al-Khulasa 'with it fatwa is given' appears that he gives fatwa with the statement of al-Tahawi indicating no dislike, but the correct view is dislike because some of it is changed—unspecified—and most of it is unchanged, and it is obligatory to honor and protect it. And when the prohibited and the permissible are combined, the prohibited prevails. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Leave what makes you doubt for what does not make you doubt.' And by this appears the corruption of the statement of those who said it is permissible to wipe with what they have in their hands of the Torah and Gospel—the Shafi'is—for it is a great recklessness, because Allah has not informed us that they have changed it entirely, and its being abrogated does not remove it from being the speech of Allah, like an abrogated verse from the Quran. Al-Zayla'i said: It is disliked for them (junub and haid) to read the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms, because all is the speech of Allah except what has been changed from it. And similar is in al-Nahr, and likewise he said in...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a menstruating woman (ha'id) or a junub reading scriptures other than the Quran—namely the Torah (Taurat), Gospel (Injil), and Psalms (Zabur). The text cites al-Khulasa saying 'it is not appropriate' for a ha'id, and then the author examines whether this implies karahah. The central issue is that these books originally contained the speech of Allah, but parts have been altered (tabdil). Despite alterations, much remains unchanged, and out of respect for the divine words, reading them is disliked for those in a state of major impurity. The passage refutes the Shafi'i view that it is permissible to use Torah or Gospel for istinja (cleaning) after defecation, arguing that since we do not know which parts are unchanged, we must treat them all with reverence and avoid misuse. The hadith 'Leave what makes you doubt' is cited. The relied-upon Hanafi view is that it is makruh (disliked) for a junub/ha'id to read these scriptures, and it is certainly impermissible to use them for cleaning. This ruling is based on the principle that when the sacred and the permissible conflict, the sacred (i.e., precaution) prevails.

Key Terms
حائض (ha'id): A woman in the state of menstruation (hayd).
توراة (Taurat): The Torah, originally revealed to Musa (Moses).
إنجيل (Injil): The Gospel, originally revealed to Isa (Jesus).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible and not disliked
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Forbidden (haram)
D) Recommended (sunnah)

Key Takeaway
A ha'id or junub should not read the Torah, Gospel, or Psalms because they contain Allah's speech and must be honored, even if parts are altered.

Prohibition of Touching Quran for Menstruating Woman
Ruling with proof for general prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menstruating woman touching the Quran and identify the primary evidence from hadith and Quran.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman must not touch the Quran directly — only through a wrapper.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common question: Can a menstruating woman touch or recite the Quran? The Hanafi school establishes a clear prohibition based on a hadith and a Quranic verse. However, as we will see in the next lesson, necessity may create exceptions. Here we focus on the general rule and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is touching the Quran by a menstruating woman. The ruling is prohibition (makruh tahrimi). The passage also mentions that touching through a wrapper is permitted.
The ruling applies also to junub and nifas.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The hadith narrated by Hakim ibn Hizam: 'Do not touch the Quran except when you are pure.' Also the Quranic verse: 'None touch it except the purified' (56:79).
3
Understand the Hanafi interpretation
The hadith is zanni al-thubut in transmission but clear in meaning (qati). The verse is used as supporting evidence. The prohibition applies to anyone in a state of ritual impurity.
4
Note the exception
Touching through a wrapper (ghilaf) is permitted. This is a minimal concession for handling the Quran without purity.
The wrapper can be a cloth or cover.
5
Recognize the scope
The prohibition includes menstruating women, postpartum women (nifas), and those in major impurity (junub). The text also mentions that a junub may read other divine books like Torah and Gospel.
The teaching exception is treated separately.
